فما ذكرت في جميع معانى الربوبية سواء ، فيكون في ذلك تدافع وتمانع ؛ مع ما كان ذلك صفة فرد سموه عددا ، أو تختلف ، فيكون الأتم لها أحق بالربوبية . وأما الأفعال، اتساق جميع العالم - مع تناقض الطبائع وتضادها صارت كأنها أشكال في قوام بعض ببعض ، وكون بعض البعض عونا وناصرا في البقاء وإن كانوا بالوجه الذي ذكرت ثبت أن ذلك التألف من التضاد لا يحتمل إلا بمدير من حكيم عليم لطيف، لا ينازع في التدبير ، ولا يخالف في التقدير ، ولا قوة إلا بالله

وإذا ثبت القول بوحدانية الله تعالى والألوهية له لا على جهة وحدانية العدد ، إذ كل واحد في العدد له نصف وأجزاء - لزم القول بتعاليه عن الأضداد ؛ إذ في إثبات الضد نفى إلهيته ، وفى التشابه نفى وحدانيته ؛ اذ الخلق كلهم تحت اسم الأشكال والأضداد ، وهما علم احتمال الفناء والعدم ونفى التوحيد عن الخلق .

والله واحد لا شبيه له ، دائم قائم لا ضد له ولا ند ، وهذا تأويل قوله : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ . وأصل ذلك أن كل ذى مثل واقع تحت العدد فيكون أقله اثنين ، وكل ذى ضد تحت الفناء إذ يهلك ضده ، وعلى ذلك كل شيء سواه له ضد يفنى به، وشكل يعد له ويصير به زوجا ، فحاصل تأويل قوله : واحد ، أي في العظمة والكبرياء ، وفى القدرة والسلطان ، وواحد بالتوحد من الأشباه والأضداد ؛ ولذلك بطل القول فيه بالجسم والعرض إذ هما تأويلا الأشياء. وإذ ثبت ذا بطل تقدير جميع ما يضاف إليه من الخلق ، ويوصف به من الصفات بما يفهم منه لو أضيف إلى الخلق ووصف به ، وبالله التوفيق

وفي ذلك ظهور تعبت المشبهة ، وذلك سبب الحد من الحد : أنه ظن به ما احتمله الشاهد . فمنهم من جعله أحد الأعيان ، وأنكر الصانع للعالم وأدعى أنه على ما عليه في الأزل ، ومنهم من صبره محتملا للحوادث وأنكر حدثة

وزعم ستيته ضرورة فقالوا ، ونفوا عنه جميع ما احتمل غيره ، إذ احتمل غيره التفاضل في الذوات ، والإختلافي في الصفات، أو احتمل غيره الاستحالة والتغير بما يتمكن فيه الزيادة والنقصان، وإن كان بعض ذلك ثوابت فهو نوع المحتمل لذلك ، على أن ثباته بالتسخير على ما هو عليه من دوام الحركة أو السكون ، أو باحتمال التقاد الذي هو آفة الموجود بما فيه احتمال الفناء ووجود الأشباه له لبطل عنه صفة الكمال والتمام ، أو تمكن النهاية له ، والحمد الذي يتوهم الأتم والأنقص ، والأوفر والأقصر ، فهذه الوجوه من آيات حدث العالم وأدلة محدثة . فلو كان المحدث مما به عرف حدث العالم وأن له محدثا ليلحقه من ذلك الوجه ما لحق غيره ، وفيه فساد العالم ، وشهادته على محدث حكيم عليم متعال عن الأشباه والأضداد / مع ما كان كل غير له حدث الوجوه ، فلو كان لشئ منه شبه يسقط عنه من ذلك القدم ، أو عن غيره الحدث، أن غيره حوادث اعترضت بتوته وهم القول أصحاب الهيولى والمسلمون ، ولا قوة إلا بالله

على أن الشبه من كل جهة في الخلق جهة دون جهة ، فلو وصف بالشبه بغيره بجهة فيصير من ذلك الوجه كأحد الخلق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
What you mentioned is equal in all meanings of lordship, so there would be mutual opposition and conflict; while that was a description of an individual they called a number, or they differ, so the more complete of them is more entitled to lordship. As for actions, the coherence of the entire world—despite the contradiction of natures and their opposition—became as if they are forms in the strength of some through others, and some being a helper and supporter for others in survival, even though they are in the manner I mentioned, it is established that that composition from opposition cannot be except through a manager, from a wise, knowing, kind [God], who does not dispute in management and does not oppose in decree, and there is no power except with God.

And when the statement is established of the oneness of God Most High and that divinity belongs to Him not in the manner of numerical oneness, for every one in number has a half and parts—then the statement follows of His transcendence above opposites; for in affirming an opposite there is negation of His divinity, and in similarity there is negation of His oneness; for all creatures are under the name of forms and opposites, and they are two signs of the possibility of annihilation and non-existence and the negation of tawhid from creation.

And God is one, no likeness to Him, eternal, self-subsistent, no opposite to Him and no rival. And this is the interpretation of His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him.' And the basis of that is that everything that has a like falls under number, so its least is two; and everything that has an opposite falls under annihilation, for its opposite destroys it. And on that, everything other than Him has an opposite by which it perishes, and a form which is counted for it and by which it becomes a pair. So the outcome of the interpretation of His saying 'One' is: in majesty and grandeur, and in power and sovereignty, and one by being unique from likes and opposites. Therefore the statement about Him of body and accident is invalid, for they are the interpretations of things. And when this is established, the estimation of all that is attributed to Him of creation and described of attributes with what is understood from it if attributed to creation and described with it is invalid. And with God is success.

And in that appears the confusion of the anthropomorphists, and that is the reason for the deviation of the deviator: that he thought about Him what the witnessed [world] implies. Some of them made Him one of the entities, and denied the Maker of the world and claimed that He is as He was in pre-eternity. And some of them rendered Him susceptible to accidents and denied temporality.

And they claimed His stillness necessarily and said, and denied about Him all that others might imply, for others might imply differentiation in essences and difference in attributes, or others might imply change and alteration with what allows increase and decrease, and even if some of that is constant, it is a type that is receptive to that; on the basis that its constancy is by subjugation as it is from continuity of motion or stillness, or by accepting decay which is a defect of existence including possibility of annihilation and existence of likes for it, then the attribute of perfection and completion would be negated from it, or an end may be possible for it, and praise which is imagined as more perfect and more deficient, more abundant and shorter. So these aspects are signs of the temporality of the world and proofs of a temporal beginning. If the temporally originated thing were that by which the temporality of the world is known and that it has a maker, then from that aspect would afflict it what afflicts others, and therein is corruption of the world, and its testimony to a wise, knowing maker transcendent above likes and opposites. With that, every other than Him has temporal aspects; if anything had a likeness to Him, the attribute of pre-eternity would drop from Him, or temporality from other than Him, that other things are temporally originated events that confronted the doubt of the supporters of hylic matter and the Muslims. And there is no power except with God.

On the basis that likeness from every aspect in creation is an aspect without another aspect; so if He is described with likeness to other in one aspect, He becomes from that aspect like one of the creatures. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and refutation. Maturidi begins by considering the hypothetical possibility of multiple lords. If all were equal in lordship, they would conflict; if one were superior, that one would deserve lordship alone. He then turns to the observable world: despite the conflicting natures and opposites found in creation, the world functions coherently as if it were a single structure of mutual support. This harmony from opposing elements can only be explained by a single wise, knowledgeable, and subtle manager who has no rival in governance. This establishes tawhid not as numerical oneness (which allows parts and division), but as unique oneness transcendent beyond opposites and similarities. Maturidi then explains the meaning of 'one' in the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him': everything that has a like falls under number (at least two), and everything that has an opposite is subject to annihilation. God alone has no like or opposite, so describing Him as body or accident is invalid. The passage then refutes the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) who mistakenly attribute to God what is known from the witnessed world. Some made Him a physical entity, others made Him susceptible to accidents, both errors arising from unwarranted comparison. Maturidi further argues that if creation shows signs of temporality and need for a maker, that maker cannot be like His creation in any aspect, otherwise the temporal imperfections would apply to Him. The argument concludes that any likeness in even one aspect would reduce Him to the level of creatures.

Key Terms
توحيد (tawhid)
أضداد (opposites)
طبائع (natures)
مدير (manager)
حكيم (wise)
عليم (knowing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the world's order could arise from multiple gods or from chance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that multiple gods would cause conflict, and the specific harmony from opposing elements demands a single wise manager.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the coherence of the world despite contradictory natures prove there is one manager?
A) Because multiple managers would also produce coherence.
B) Because contradictory natures cannot naturally produce harmony without a single wise director.
C) Because the world is too complex for any manager.
D) Because the world is eternal and needs no manager.

Key Takeaway
The coherent order of the world from conflicting elements necessitates a single, wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The harmony of the world demands one Manager. Where in your life have you resisted His single management, trying to serve multiple masters or relying on chance? What concrete step will you take today to submit your will entirely to His governance?
?
Maturidi says composition from opposition requires a manager. Think of a time when your inner contradictions—like good intentions versus laziness—felt irreconcilable. How will you consciously invite the Wise Manager to bring coherence to that area of your life starting tonight?
?
The passage ends with 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (no power except with God). In what specific situation this week have you been acting as if your own strength suffices? Write down one thing you will do differently to acknowledge that all power comes from Him alone.

God’s Oneness is Transcendent, Not Numerical
Maturidi distinguishes true oneness from numerical and refutes body/accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between numerical oneness and the unique oneness of God, and why God is beyond opposites and likenesses.

Core Idea
God's oneness is not numerical; it is unique transcendence above all opposites and likes.

After establishing that there is one Creator, Maturidi clarifies that God's oneness is not like the numerical oneness of created things. Numerical oneness implies divisibility and parts, which are impossible for God. Instead, God's oneness means He has no opposite or like, and He is transcendent above all categories that imply limitation or annihilation. This passage also explains the meaning of 'There is nothing like unto Him' and why describing God as body or accident is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on transcendence
Read from 'وإذا ثبت القول بوحدانية الله' to 'وبالله التوفيق'. Focus on how Maturidi distinguishes God's oneness from numerical oneness.
2
Understand numerical oneness
Note that numerical oneness applies to things that have halves and parts. God cannot be divided, so His oneness is different.
This refutes any notion of God as a unit among many.
3
Grasp transcendence above opposites
Maturidi says affirming an opposite negates divinity, and similarity negates oneness. Reflect on why having an opposite would make God subject to annihilation.
Opposites destroy each other (e.g., heat and cold).
4
Examine the verse interpretation
The verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' is interpreted: God has no like (so not under number) and no opposite (so not under annihilation). Understand that everything else has both a like and an opposite.
5
Conclude on body and accident
Maturidi concludes that describing God as body or accident is invalid because these categories belong to created things that have likes and opposites.
This is a key argument against anthropomorphism.

Some of them made Him one of the entities, and denied the Maker of the world and claimed that He is as He was in pre-eternity. And some of them rendered Him susceptible to accidents and denied temporality.

And they claimed His stillness necessarily and said, and denied about Him all that others might imply, for others might imply differentiation in essences and difference in attributes, or others might imply change and alteration with what allows increase and decrease, and even if some of that is constant, it is a type that is receptive to that; on the basis that its constancy is by subjugation as it is from continuity of motion or stillness, or by accepting decay which is a defect of existence including possibility of annihilation and existence of likes for it, then the attribute of perfection and completion would be negated from it, or an end may be possible for it, and praise which is imagined as more perfect and more deficient, more abundant and shorter. So these aspects are signs of the temporality of the world and proofs of a temporal beginning. If the temporally originated thing were that by which the temporality of the world is known and that it has a maker, then from that aspect would afflict it what afflicts others, and therein is corruption of the world, and its testimony to a wise, knowing maker transcendent above likes and opposites. With that, every other than Him has temporal aspects; if anything had a likeness to Him, the attribute of pre-eternity would drop from Him, or temporality from other than Him, that other things are temporally originated events that confronted the doubt of the supporters of hylic matter and the Muslims. And there is no power except with God.

On the basis that likeness from every aspect in creation is an aspect without another aspect; so if He is described with likeness to other in one aspect, He becomes from that aspect like one of the creatures. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and refutation. Maturidi begins by considering the hypothetical possibility of multiple lords. If all were equal in lordship, they would conflict; if one were superior, that one would deserve lordship alone. He then turns to the observable world: despite the conflicting natures and opposites found in creation, the world functions coherently as if it were a single structure of mutual support. This harmony from opposing elements can only be explained by a single wise, knowledgeable, and subtle manager who has no rival in governance. This establishes tawhid not as numerical oneness (which allows parts and division), but as unique oneness transcendent beyond opposites and similarities. Maturidi then explains the meaning of 'one' in the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him': everything that has a like falls under number (at least two), and everything that has an opposite is subject to annihilation. God alone has no like or opposite, so describing Him as body or accident is invalid. The passage then refutes the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) who mistakenly attribute to God what is known from the witnessed world. Some made Him a physical entity, others made Him susceptible to accidents, both errors arising from unwarranted comparison. Maturidi further argues that if creation shows signs of temporality and need for a maker, that maker cannot be like His creation in any aspect, otherwise the temporal imperfections would apply to Him. The argument concludes that any likeness in even one aspect would reduce Him to the level of creatures.

Key Terms
وحدانية العدد (numerical oneness)
أضداد (opposites)
أشباه (likes)
جسم (body)
عرض (accident)
تشبيه (tashbih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's oneness means He is one among many possible gods, like a numerical unit.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that numerical oneness implies divisibility, which is impossible for God. His oneness is unique and transcendent.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does describing God as 'body' or 'accident' become invalid?
A) Because these terms are not found in the Quran.
B) Because body and accident imply having likes or opposites, which are limited to creation.
C) Because body and accident are too physical for God.
D) Because Maturidi only accepts negative theology.

Key Takeaway
God’s oneness is not like the oneness of creatures; it is absolute uniqueness beyond all limitation and comparison.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's oneness means He has no like or opposite, what does that imply about anything you have ever compared to Him—an idol, a leader, a loved one? Have you ever, even subtly, placed someone or something alongside Him in your heart? Tonight, sincerely ask yourself: Is there any person, status, or possession that you secretly think is 'like' what God can give or take? Commit to uprooting that from your heart.
?
Maturidi says everything other than God has an opposite that can annihilate it. Think about something you are most afraid of losing. Does its opposite exist? Could it be taken away by its opposite? How does knowing that God alone has no opposite and cannot be destroyed change how you cling to created things? Will you place your ultimate security in Him alone starting today?
?
You claim God is 'One' every day in your prayers. But do you truly understand that His oneness means He cannot be numbered, measured, or compared? When you say 'Allahu Akbar,' do you feel the weight that nothing—no power, no wealth, no person—can rival Him? If not, what would it mean for you to live as if this were really true? What one change can you make this week to act on this belief?

Refutation of Anthropomorphism
Maturidi refutes anthropomorphists and argues against any likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) and his argument that any likeness between God and creation would imply imperfection and temporality.

Core Idea
Attributing to God characteristics of creation leads to absurdity and negates His perfection.

Having established God's transcendence above likes and opposites, Maturidi now directly refutes the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha). They erroneously attribute to God what is observed in the created world, leading to two major errors: some make God a physical entity, others make Him susceptible to accidents. Maturidi argues that if God had any likeness to creation in any aspect, He would share in the imperfections of creation—such as temporality, change, and need—which is impossible for the eternal Creator. This passage also engages with deeper arguments about the temporality of the world and the absurdity of likening the Creator to His creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of mushabbiha
Read from 'وفي ذلك ظهور تعبت المشبهة' to 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' (both paragraphs). Identify the two errors Maturidi mentions.
2
Understand the cause of error
Maturidi says the deviator's confusion is due to applying what is known from the witnessed world to God. Reflect on why this is problematic.
The witnessed world includes limitation, change, and need.
3
Analyze the two mistaken positions
Some made God one of the entities (جسم), others made Him susceptible to accidents (عرض). Both reduce God to a creature.
4
Grasp the argument from temporality
Maturidi argues that if God had a like, He would be subject to temporality and imperfection. Trace the logic: any likeness would bring the attributes of creation, including temporality.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude on the necessity of transcendence
The passage ends by stating that any likeness in even one aspect would reduce God to the level of creatures. Therefore, God must be absolutely unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما ذكرت في جميع معانى الربوبية سواء ، فيكون في ذلك تدافع وتمانع ؛ مع ما كان ذلك صفة فرد سموه عددا ، أو تختلف ، فيكون الأتم لها أحق بالربوبية . وأما الأفعال، اتساق جميع العالم - مع تناقض الطبائع وتضادها صارت كأنها أشكال في قوام بعض ببعض ، وكون بعض البعض عونا وناصرا في البقاء وإن كانوا بالوجه الذي ذكرت ثبت أن ذلك التألف من التضاد لا يحتمل إلا بمدير من حكيم عليم لطيف، لا ينازع في التدبير ، ولا يخالف في التقدير ، ولا قوة إلا بالله

وإذا ثبت القول بوحدانية الله تعالى والألوهية له لا على جهة وحدانية العدد ، إذ كل واحد في العدد له نصف وأجزاء - لزم القول بتعاليه عن الأضداد ؛ إذ في إثبات الضد نفى إلهيته ، وفى التشابه نفى وحدانيته ؛ اذ الخلق كلهم تحت اسم الأشكال والأضداد ، وهما علم احتمال الفناء والعدم ونفى التوحيد عن الخلق .

والله واحد لا شبيه له ، دائم قائم لا ضد له ولا ند ، وهذا تأويل قوله : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ . وأصل ذلك أن كل ذى مثل واقع تحت العدد فيكون أقله اثنين ، وكل ذى ضد تحت الفناء إذ يهلك ضده ، وعلى ذلك كل شيء سواه له ضد يفنى به، وشكل يعد له ويصير به زوجا ، فحاصل تأويل قوله : واحد ، أي في العظمة والكبرياء ، وفى القدرة والسلطان ، وواحد بالتوحد من الأشباه والأضداد ؛ ولذلك بطل القول فيه بالجسم والعرض إذ هما تأويلا الأشياء. وإذ ثبت ذا بطل تقدير جميع ما يضاف إليه من الخلق ، ويوصف به من الصفات بما يفهم منه لو أضيف إلى الخلق ووصف به ، وبالله التوفيق

وفي ذلك ظهور تعبت المشبهة ، وذلك سبب الحد من الحد : أنه ظن به ما احتمله الشاهد . فمنهم من جعله أحد الأعيان ، وأنكر الصانع للعالم وأدعى أنه على ما عليه في الأزل ، ومنهم من صبره محتملا للحوادث وأنكر حدثة

وزعم ستيته ضرورة فقالوا ، ونفوا عنه جميع ما احتمل غيره ، إذ احتمل غيره التفاضل في الذوات ، والإختلافي في الصفات، أو احتمل غيره الاستحالة والتغير بما يتمكن فيه الزيادة والنقصان، وإن كان بعض ذلك ثوابت فهو نوع المحتمل لذلك ، على أن ثباته بالتسخير على ما هو عليه من دوام الحركة أو السكون ، أو باحتمال التقاد الذي هو آفة الموجود بما فيه احتمال الفناء ووجود الأشباه له لبطل عنه صفة الكمال والتمام ، أو تمكن النهاية له ، والحمد الذي يتوهم الأتم والأنقص ، والأوفر والأقصر ، فهذه الوجوه من آيات حدث العالم وأدلة محدثة . فلو كان المحدث مما به عرف حدث العالم وأن له محدثا ليلحقه من ذلك الوجه ما لحق غيره ، وفيه فساد العالم ، وشهادته على محدث حكيم عليم متعال عن الأشباه والأضداد / مع ما كان كل غير له حدث الوجوه ، فلو كان لشئ منه شبه يسقط عنه من ذلك القدم ، أو عن غيره الحدث، أن غيره حوادث اعترضت بتوته وهم القول أصحاب الهيولى والمسلمون ، ولا قوة إلا بالله

على أن الشبه من كل جهة في الخلق جهة دون جهة ، فلو وصف بالشبه بغيره بجهة فيصير من ذلك الوجه كأحد الخلق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
What you mentioned is equal in all meanings of lordship, so there would be mutual opposition and conflict; while that was a description of an individual they called a number, or they differ, so the more complete of them is more entitled to lordship. As for actions, the coherence of the entire world—despite the contradiction of natures and their opposition—became as if they are forms in the strength of some through others, and some being a helper and supporter for others in survival, even though they are in the manner I mentioned, it is established that that composition from opposition cannot be except through a manager, from a wise, knowing, kind [God], who does not dispute in management and does not oppose in decree, and there is no power except with God.

And when the statement is established of the oneness of God Most High and that divinity belongs to Him not in the manner of numerical oneness, for every one in number has a half and parts—then the statement follows of His transcendence above opposites; for in affirming an opposite there is negation of His divinity, and in similarity there is negation of His oneness; for all creatures are under the name of forms and opposites, and they are two signs of the possibility of annihilation and non-existence and the negation of tawhid from creation.

And God is one, no likeness to Him, eternal, self-subsistent, no opposite to Him and no rival. And this is the interpretation of His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him.' And the basis of that is that everything that has a like falls under number, so its least is two; and everything that has an opposite falls under annihilation, for its opposite destroys it. And on that, everything other than Him has an opposite by which it perishes, and a form which is counted for it and by which it becomes a pair. So the outcome of the interpretation of His saying 'One' is: in majesty and grandeur, and in power and sovereignty, and one by being unique from likes and opposites. Therefore the statement about Him of body and accident is invalid, for they are the interpretations of things. And when this is established, the estimation of all that is attributed to Him of creation and described of attributes with what is understood from it if attributed to creation and described with it is invalid. And with God is success.

And in that appears the confusion of the anthropomorphists, and that is the reason for the deviation of the deviator: that he thought about Him what the witnessed [world] implies. Some of them made Him one of the entities, and denied the Maker of the world and claimed that He is as He was in pre-eternity. And some of them rendered Him susceptible to accidents and denied temporality.

And they claimed His stillness necessarily and said, and denied about Him all that others might imply, for others might imply differentiation in essences and difference in attributes, or others might imply change and alteration with what allows increase and decrease, and even if some of that is constant, it is a type that is receptive to that; on the basis that its constancy is by subjugation as it is from continuity of motion or stillness, or by accepting decay which is a defect of existence including possibility of annihilation and existence of likes for it, then the attribute of perfection and completion would be negated from it, or an end may be possible for it, and praise which is imagined as more perfect and more deficient, more abundant and shorter. So these aspects are signs of the temporality of the world and proofs of a temporal beginning. If the temporally originated thing were that by which the temporality of the world is known and that it has a maker, then from that aspect would afflict it what afflicts others, and therein is corruption of the world, and its testimony to a wise, knowing maker transcendent above likes and opposites. And there is no power except with God.

On the basis that likeness from every aspect in creation is an aspect without another aspect; so if He is described with likeness to other in one aspect, He becomes from that aspect like one of the creatures. And there is no power except with God.

The Argument Against a Temporal Beginning
Maturidi reports the second opinion that the world has no temporal beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion reported by Maturidi that argues against a temporal beginning for the world based on the possibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
If every conceivable beginning has a conceivable 'before', there is no first moment.

Maturidi presents a view that challenges the idea of a finite beginning. Some thinkers argue that whenever you imagine a start to the world, you can always imagine an earlier time, making a true beginning impossible. This short passage introduces this argument, which Maturidi will later evaluate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once and then its translation. Notice the key terms: ibtidāʾ and ghāya.
The passage is very concise; the argument is packed into a single statement.
2
Understand the logic
The argument claims that for any time you consider as the world's beginning, you can always conceive a time before it. This means no finite beginning (ghāya) can be established.
This leads to an infinite regress of time.
3
Contextualize the view
Remember that Maturidi is reporting this view, not necessarily endorsing it. He will later discuss its validity.
This is part of a larger discussion on proving the existence of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول: ما من وقت يتوهم فيه ابتداء العالم إلا وقد يتوهم قبله، فيبطل له الغاية.
And among them is one who says: There is no time in which the beginning of the world is conceived except that something before it is also conceived, so its final limit is invalidated for it.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion regarding the indication of the visible on the unseen. Some held that whenever one imagines a beginning of the world, one can also imagine a time before that beginning. This leads to an infinite regress, and consequently the world cannot have a finite beginning (ghāya). The implication is that the world is eternal. Maturidi reports this view without endorsing it, setting the stage for further discussion.

Key Terms
ابتداء (ibtidāʾ)
غاية (ghāya)
توهم (tawahhum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument is definitive and represents Maturidi's own position.
Correction: Maturidi is merely reporting a view; he will later show its flaws.

Checkpoint

A) One reaches a definite first time
B) One can always conceive a time before that beginning, so no finite beginning exists
C) The world must have been created by a pre-existing cause
D) The concept of time is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Maturidi reports that some argue no finite beginning is possible because any supposed beginning has an earlier time conceived before it, but this is only one view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Maturidi reports this view without endorsing it. Are you patient enough to study the refutation before forming a final opinion, or do you tend to jump to conclusions about deep theological matters? What would change in your heart if you held your conclusions with more humility?

Inversion of Origin and Branch Refuted
Maturidi refutes the inversion of origin and branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the claim that the ancient is a branch of the originated as an inversion, and how the ancient is the origin.

Core Idea
The ancient cannot be a branch; it is the origin of all that is originated.

Maturidi begins by addressing a theological error: some claim that the eternal (qadim) is a branch (far') of the originated (hadith). He argues this is inverted logic, because the ancient existed without need of inference, while the originated depends on it. This lesson examines his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the compact style and the key terms 'origin' and 'branch'.
The phrase 'ما به استدلال' means 'that by which inference is made'.
2
Identify the inversion claim
Maturidi states that what the opponent claimed about origin and branch is inverted. Understand what inversion means: treating the cause as effect and effect as cause.
The opponent likely argued that the ancient (God) is a branch of the originated world, which Maturidi reverses.
3
Understand why the ancient is the origin
Since the ancient and eternity existed without any need for inference, it cannot be a branch. Rather, it is the origin because the originated exists through it.
This is a foundational principle in kalam: the self-sufficient is the origin, the dependent is the branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعم من الأصل والفرع فمقلوب؛ لما كان القديم والقدم ولم يكن ما به استدلال، فلذلك لم يجب جعله فرعا لهذا، بل هو الأصل لكون هذا به. ثم كل كائن بغيره من طريق العقل - تخارج من جوهرة في الشاهد كالبناء والكتابة، وكل أنواع الأفعال والأقوال التى هى أغيار لَمَنْ كُنْ لم يجز أن يلحقهم بالجوهر والصفة، فمثله الذي به العالم. على أنه جاز في الشاهد إثبات ما لا يُدرك ولا يحاط به نحو السمع والبصر والروح والعقل والهوى ونحو ذلك، وما يدرك نحو الأجسام الكثيفة، فلو كانت هي قاديمة الأصل فيجب أن يكون كل نوع يتولد ويحدث من جوهره: العقل من العقل، وكذلك البصر والسمع، ومعلوم الإختلاف بين كل جوهر والمتولد منه في ذلك، فلزم الكون والحدث إن كان مختلفا، وفى تثبيت الإختلاف بطلان أن يكون الذي فى وصف القدم عالما، أو على ما عليه صفته، وفي ذلك إثبات حدث العالم بمن ليس كمثله فمثله العالم
And what he claimed of origin and branch is inverted; because the ancient and eternity existed, and there was not that by which inference is made, therefore it is not necessary to make it a branch of this, rather it is the origin because this exists through it. Then everything that exists through another by way of reason - emerges from its substance in the seen, like building and writing, and all types of actions and sayings that are changes for whom it was, it is not permissible to attach them to the substance and attribute, so its like is that by which the world exists. However, it is permissible in the seen to affirm what is not perceived and not encompassed, such as hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, and the like, and what is perceived like dense bodies. For if they were ancient in origin, then it would be necessary that every type is generated and comes to be from its substance: intellect from intellect, and likewise sight and hearing. But the difference between every substance and what is generated from it in that is known, so contingency and temporality are necessitated if it is different. And in confirming the difference is the invalidity of that which is in the description of eternity being a knower, or in the state of His attribute. And in that is the proof of the temporality of the world by Him of whom there is nothing like Him, so the world is with Him.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected arguments: a refutation of the inversion of origin and branch, and an argument from generation (tawallud). Maturidi begins by criticizing those who claim that the ancient (qadim) is a branch (far') of the originated (hadith), calling this inverted. Since the ancient and eternity existed without needing inference, it cannot be a branch; rather, it is the origin because everything originated exists through it. Then he moves to the seen world: everything that exists through another (kullu ka'in bi-ghayrihi) emerges from its substance (jawhar) in the observed world, like building from a builder or writing from a writer. Such acts and qualities are changes (aghyar) and cannot be attributed to the substance or attribute in the same way. The world's like is that by which it exists, i.e., a creator. He then notes that in the seen, we affirm imperceptible things like hearing, sight, soul, intellect, desire, alongside perceptible dense bodies. If these were ancient in origin, then each type would have to generate from its own substance (e.g., intellect from intellect). But we observe clear differences between substances and what is generated from them, which necessitates that such generated things are contingent and temporal (kawn wa hadath). This difference disproves that an eternal being could be a knower or have that attribute in the same way. Thus, this proves the temporality of the world and the existence of a creator unlike anything ('adam mithlihi). The argument uses the diversity of generated things to show they cannot be eternal but require a creator.

Key Terms
أصل
فرع
قديم
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the eternal (God) can be considered a branch of the originated world.
Correction: Maturidi argues this is inverted; the ancient is the origin because the originated depends on it, and the ancient existed without need of inference.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the claim of origin and branch is inverted?
A) Because the ancient is a branch of the originated.
B) Because the ancient and eternity are the origin, and the originated is dependent on them.
C) Because both are equal in being branches of something else.
D) Because the branch can become an origin in another context.

Key Takeaway
The ancient is the origin, not a branch, because it is self-sufficient while the originated depends on it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself treating God as a branch of your life—something you turn to only after you've arranged your own world? If God is the self-sufficient origin, what would it look like for Him to be the origin of your day today?
?
Reflect on one thing—a desire, a person, an achievement—that you have made the 'origin' of your security. How does Maturidi's correction of the inverted claim challenge you to reorder that relationship? What step can you take today to treat God as the true origin?
?
The originated world exists only through the ancient. Is there an area of your life where you are acting as if you are the origin—trying to sustain yourself by your own power? What would change if you honestly acknowledged that your very existence is through Him? What would you need to let go of?

Argument from Generation and Contingency
Maturidi argues from generation to prove contingency and a unique creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the diversity of generated things to prove that they are contingent and require a creator unlike themselves.

Core Idea
The difference between generated things and their substances proves they are not eternal but contingent.

Maturidi extends his argument by examining the seen world: everything that exists through another emerges from its substance, yet we observe clear differences between substances and what is generated from them. This difference proves that generated things are contingent and not co-eternal with their substances. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the examples
Read the Arabic from 'ثم كل كائن...' to the end. Note the examples: building, writing, actions, words, and the list of imperceptibles (hearing, sight, etc.) and perceptibles (dense bodies).
The passage mentions both perceptible and imperceptible things to show the argument's breadth.
2
Understand the generation argument
Maturidi argues that in the seen world, generated things (like building from builder) are changes (aghyar) and cannot be equated with the substance or attribute of the generator. This shows they are not co-eternal.
The phrase 'تخارج من جوهرة' likely means 'emerges from its substance'.
3
Apply the argument to the claim of eternity
If these generated things were ancient in origin, each type would have to generate from its own kind (e.g., intellect from intellect). But we see differences between substance and product, which necessitates contingency.
The difference (ikhtilaf) between substance and generated thing implies that the generated thing is not co-eternal with the substance.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that this proves the temporality of the world (hadath al-alam) and the existence of a creator unlike anything, through whom the world exists.
The final phrase 'فمثله فمثله العالم' is dense: 'so His likeness is that by which the world exists'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعم من الأصل والفرع فمقلوب؛ لما كان القديم والقدم ولم يكن ما به استدلال، فلذلك لم يجب جعله فرعا لهذا، بل هو الأصل لكون هذا به. ثم كل كائن بغيره من طريق العقل - تخارج من جوهرة في الشاهد كالبناء والكتابة، وكل أنواع الأفعال والأقوال التى هى أغيار لَمَنْ كُنْ لم يجز أن يلحقهم بالجوهر والصفة، فمثله الذي به العالم. على أنه جاز في الشاهد إثبات ما لا يُدرك ولا يحاط به نحو السمع والبصر والروح والعقل والهوى ونحو ذلك، وما يدرك نحو الأجسام الكثيفة، فلو كانت هي قاديمة الأصل فيجب أن يكون كل نوع يتولد ويحدث من جوهره: العقل من العقل، وكذلك البصر والسمع، ومعلوم الإختلاف بين كل جوهر والمتولد منه في ذلك، فلزم الكون والحدث إن كان مختلفا، وفى تثبيت الإختلاف بطلان أن يكون الذي فى وصف القدم عالما، أو على ما عليه صفته، وفي ذلك إثبات حدث العالم بمن ليس كمثله فمثله العالم
And what he claimed of origin and branch is inverted; because the ancient and eternity existed, and there was not that by which inference is made, therefore it is not necessary to make it a branch of this, rather it is the origin because this exists through it. Then everything that exists through another by way of reason - emerges from its substance in the seen, like building and writing, and all types of actions and sayings that are changes for whom it was, it is not permissible to attach them to the substance and attribute, so its like is that by which the world exists. However, it is permissible in the seen to affirm what is not perceived and not encompassed, such as hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, and the like, and what is perceived like dense bodies. For if they were ancient in origin, then it would be necessary that every type is generated and comes to be from its substance: intellect from intellect, and likewise sight and hearing. But the difference between every substance and what is generated from it in that is known, so contingency and temporality are necessitated if it is different. And in confirming the difference is the invalidity of that which is in the description of eternity being a knower, or in the state of His attribute. And in that is the proof of the temporality of the world by Him of whom there is nothing like Him, so the world is with Him.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected arguments: a refutation of the inversion of origin and branch, and an argument from generation (tawallud). Maturidi begins by criticizing those who claim that the ancient (qadim) is a branch (far') of the originated (hadith), calling this inverted. Since the ancient and eternity existed without needing inference, it cannot be a branch; rather, it is the origin because everything originated exists through it. Then he moves to the seen world: everything that exists through another (kullu ka'in bi-ghayrihi) emerges from its substance (jawhar) in the observed world, like building from a builder or writing from a writer. Such acts and qualities are changes (aghyar) and cannot be attributed to the substance or attribute in the same way. The world's like is that by which it exists, i.e., a creator. He then notes that in the seen, we affirm imperceptible things like hearing, sight, soul, intellect, desire, alongside perceptible dense bodies. If these were ancient in origin, then each type would have to generate from its own substance (e.g., intellect from intellect). But we observe clear differences between substances and what is generated from them, which necessitates that such generated things are contingent and temporal (kawn wa hadath). This difference disproves that an eternal being could be a knower or have that attribute in the same way. Thus, this proves the temporality of the world and the existence of a creator unlike anything ('adam mithlihi). The argument uses the diversity of generated things to show they cannot be eternal but require a creator.

Key Terms
كائن بغيره
جوهر
تولد
حدث
اختلاف
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That generated things could be co-eternal with their substances or arise from them without contingency.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the observed difference between substance and generated thing necessitates contingency and temporality, so they cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the difference between a substance and what is generated from it imply?
A) That the generated thing is eternal.
B) That the generated thing is contingent and temporal.
C) That the substance is non-existent.
D) That the generated thing is identical to the substance.

Key Takeaway
The observed difference between generated things and their sources proves their contingency and points to a unique creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your intellect, hearing, and desires daily. If these are contingent and generated through another, how does that affect your trust in your own abilities? Will you consciously depend on the Creator of these faculties today?
?
The argument shows that everything generated is different from its source. Consider something you have created—a project, a relationship, an idea—that you treat as permanent. How does this lesson expose that as a false sense of permanence? What would it look like to attribute its existence to Allah alone?
?
You affirm that Allah is unlike His creation, yet you still measure Him by your understanding of generated things. In what specific area of your belief or action are you equating the Creator with the created? How will you realign your worship to the One who is 'not like anything'?

The World's Temporality and Need for a Creator
Maturidi presents evidence for the world's temporality and need for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that the world's temporality and inability to sustain itself prove it requires a creator.

Core Idea
The world's change and dependence show it is created, not eternal.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi begins by stating that the way the world indicates its creator varies according to what is indicated. He then presents the first line of evidence: the world's possibility of non-existence, its passing away, and the co-existence of opposites in a single entity at different times demonstrate its temporality. Moreover, its ignorance of its own beginnings and its inability to repair corruption show it did not come to be by itself. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first a general principle, then two specific indicators.
The Arabic uses 'دل' (it indicates) twice, marking two separate points.
2
Identify the first indicator
Maturidi says that the possibility of non-existence (إستحالة) and passing away (زوال), along with the gathering of opposites (اجتماع الأضداد) in one entity, indicate temporality (حدث). Ask yourself: why does change prove that something had a beginning?
An eternal being cannot undergo change from one state to another.
3
Identify the second indicator
The world's ignorance of its own beginnings and its inability to repair corruption show it did not come to be by itself. This is an argument from need: if something cannot sustain itself, it needs a sustainer.
This is similar to the later argument from need in lesson 3.
4
Understand the conclusion
From these two indicators, Maturidi concludes that the world is temporal (hadith) and that it has a creator. The world is not self-sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل أن دلالة مختلفة على اختلاف دل احتماله الإستحالة والزوال واجتماع الاضداد في عين في حال على حدثه ثم دل جهله بمباديه وعجزه عن إصلاح ما فسد منه أنه لم يكن بنفسه، ثم دل اجتماع الأحوال المتضادة واتساق جواهر الخلق على الاستقامة على أن مدير الكل ومحدثه واحد، ويدل أيضا اتساقه واستقامته وحفظ الاضداد في عين على قدرة مديره وحكمته وعلمه، فاختلفت جهات الدلالة فيها عليه دلالات العيان فصار دليل إثبات المحدث عجز المحدث، ودليل علمه لما اتسبق جهله بنفسه، فصار وجود الدلالة به علی الخلاف لا الوفاق: وأصل آخر أيضا: أن الضروريات والحاجات هي التي دلت على غير. فلم يجز أن يحتمل ما احتمل هو لما يحوج إلى غير ثم ذلك إلى آخر إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك فاسد، والله أعلم.
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle is that indication differs according to differences. It indicates the impossibility of [eternity] and passing away and the gathering of opposites in one entity in a state upon its temporality. Then it indicates its ignorance of its beginnings and its inability to repair what has become corrupt from it, that it did not come to be by itself. Then it indicates the gathering of contradictory states and the consistency of the substances of creation upon uprightness that the director of all and its originator is one. And also its consistency, uprightness, and preservation of opposites in one entity indicate the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. So the aspects of indication in it vary upon it as indications of the eye. So the proof of affirming the originator became the inability of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge became that His ignorance of itself was left behind. So the existence of indication came about upon difference, not agreement. And another principle: that necessities and needs are what indicate the other. So it is not permissible that what is itself possible needs another, then that to another ad infinitum, and that is invalid. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage is a summary statement by Imam Maturidi outlining the principles of theistic evidence from the world. He begins by stating that the indication (dalala) varies according to the differences in what it indicates. He lists three lines of evidence: First, the world's possibility of non-existence and passing away, and the co-existence of opposites in one subject (like heat and cold at different times), prove its temporality (huduth). Second, the world's ignorance of its own beginnings and its inability to repair its own corruption show that it did not come to be by itself (i.e., it requires a creator). Third, the gathering of contradictory states and the consistent order of created substances upon uprightness demonstrate that the director and originator of all is one. Moreover, the consistency, uprightness, and preservation of opposites in one entity prove the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. Thus, the multiple aspects of indication all point to a single creator. Maturidi then notes that the proof of the originator is the inability of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge is that the world lacks knowledge of its own origin. Therefore, the existence of indication is based on difference, not agreement (i.e., the world is different from its creator, so it points to Him). Finally, he adds another principle: necessities and needs indicate that which is other than themselves. For if a dependent being itself could satisfy its own need, it would not require another. But if it requires another, that chain cannot go to infinity (tasalsul), which is invalid. Thus, there must be a self-sufficient being that ends the regress. This argument from need and infinite regress is a classic kalam proof for the existence of God.

Key Terms
دلالة
حدث
استحالة
زوال
اضداد
جهل
عجز
مبادئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the world changes, it could still be eternal with cycles.
Correction: Maturidi argues that change (especially the gathering of opposites) is impossible for an eternal being, because an eternal being cannot undergo alteration or cease to exist.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the world's ignorance of its beginnings and its inability to repair corruption indicate?
A) That the world is eternal.
B) That the world did not come to be by itself.
C) That the world is self-sufficient.
D) That the world has multiple creators.

Key Takeaway
The world's temporality and helplessness prove that it is created and requires a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The world is ignorant of its own beginning. You too do not know where you came from. Does this ignorance humble you enough to seek the One who does know?
?
You experience opposites in your own self: health and sickness, joy and sorrow. If these changes prove the world is temporal, what do they prove about your own dependence on God every moment?
?
The world cannot repair its own corruption. Look at your own life: where have you tried to fix yourself without acknowledging your Creator? What is holding you back from admitting that you need Him absolutely?

The Unity and Attributes of the Creator from Cosmic Order
Maturidi presents arguments for divine unity and attributes from cosmic order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues from the consistency and preservation of opposites in the universe to the oneness and attributes (power, wisdom, knowledge) of the creator.

Core Idea
The world's orderly unity and balance point to a single, wise, all-powerful director.

Maturidi continues by observing that the gathering of contradictory states and the consistent arrangement of created substances upon uprightness indicate that the director and originator of all is one. Additionally, the preservation of opposites in one entity shows the power, wisdom, and knowledge of that director. This lesson focuses on these cosmic signs of divine unity and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and English translation. Note the parallel structure: 'then it indicates... and also it indicates...'.
This part follows directly from the previous passage.
2
Identify the argument for oneness
Maturidi says that the gathering of contradictory states (الأحوال المتضادة) and the consistency (اتساق) of substances (جواهر) upon uprightness show that the director (مدير) and originator (محدث) is one (واحد). How does order imply a single director?
If there were multiple directors, there would be conflict or inconsistency.
3
Identify the argument for attributes
The consistency, uprightness, and preservation of opposites in one entity indicate the power (قدرة), wisdom (حكمة), and knowledge (علم) of the director. What does each attribute correspond to?
Preserving opposites requires power and wisdom; the order itself shows knowledge.
4
Understand the concluding remark
Maturidi says that the proof of the originator is the inability of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge is the world's ignorance. He notes that the existence of indication is based on difference (between creator and creation), not agreement.
This highlights the via negativa aspect: the world points to God by being different from Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

If not, could it be that you haven't truly acknowledged your inability? Ask yourself: Am I living as if I am self-sufficient?
?

The Argument from Need and the Impossibility of Infinite Regress
Maturidi presents the argument from need and the impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the existence of needs in the world necessitates a self-sufficient being, and that an infinite chain of causes is impossible.

Core Idea
If everything depended on something else, there would be no first cause—so a self-sufficient being must exist.

Maturidi introduces another principle: necessities and needs are what point to something other than themselves. If a thing itself could satisfy its own need, it would not require another. But since things depend on others, that chain cannot continue without end (tasalsul). An infinite regress is invalid, so there must be a self-sufficient being that stops the regress. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. This is a self-contained argument about need and infinite regress.
The phrase 'فلم يجز' means 'it is not permissible'—logically impossible.
2
Understand the principle
Maturidi states: 'necessities and needs indicate the other'—that is, a thing in need points to something outside itself that can satisfy that need.
This is a fundamental premise: dependent beings point to an independent cause.
3
Follow the regress argument
If a dependent being (A) needs another (B), and B also needs another (C), then we have a chain. But if this chain goes on infinitely (to infinity), there would be no first cause to satisfy the need. Therefore, infinite regress is invalid (فاسد).
The conclusion is that there must be a self-sufficient being that ends the chain.
4
Apply the reasoning
Think of any contingent thing in the world: it owes its existence to something else. That something else, if also contingent, needs another, and so on. This cannot go on forever, so there must be a necessary being (God).
This is a classic argument in Islamic theology (kalam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل أن دلالة مختلفة على اختلاف دل احتماله الإستحالة والزوال واجتماع الاضداد في عين في حال على حدثه ثم دل جهله بمباديه وعجزه عن إصلاح ما فسد منه أنه لم يكن بنفسه، ثم دل اجتماع الأحوال المتضادة واتساق جواهر الخلق على الاستقامة على أن مدير الكل ومحدثه واحد، ويدل أيضا اتساقه واستقامته وحفظ الاضداد في عين على قدرة مديره وحكمته وعلمه، فاختلفت جهات الدلالة فيها عليه دلالات العيان فصار دليل إثبات المحدث عجز المحدث، ودليل علمه لما اتسبق جهله بنفسه، فصار وجود الدلالة به علی الخلاف لا الوفاق: وأصل آخر أيضا: أن الضروريات والحاجات هي التي دلت على غير. فلم يجز أن يحتمل ما احتمل هو لما يحوج إلى غير ثم ذلك إلى آخر إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك فاسد، والله أعلم.
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle is that indication differs according to differences. It indicates the impossibility of [eternity] and passing away and the gathering of opposites in one entity in a state upon its temporality. Then it indicates its ignorance of its beginnings and its inability to repair what has become corrupt from it, that it did not come to be by itself. Then it indicates the gathering of contradictory states and the consistency of the substances of creation upon uprightness that the director of all and its originator is one. And also its consistency, uprightness, and preservation of opposites in one entity indicate the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. So the aspects of indication in it vary upon it as indications of the eye. So the proof of affirming the originator became the inability of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge became that His ignorance of itself was left behind. So the existence of indication came about upon difference, not agreement. And another principle: that necessities and needs are what indicate the other. So it is not permissible that what is itself possible needs another, then that to another ad infinitum, and that is invalid. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage is a summary statement by Imam Maturidi outlining the principles of theistic evidence from the world. He begins by stating that the indication (dalala) varies according to the differences in what it indicates. He lists three lines of evidence: First, the world's possibility of non-existence and passing away, and the co-existence of opposites in one subject (like heat and cold at different times), prove its temporality (huduth). Second, the world's ignorance of its own beginnings and its inability to repair its own corruption show that it did not come to be by itself (i.e., it requires a creator). Third, the gathering of contradictory states and the consistent order of created substances upon uprightness demonstrate that the director and originator of all is one. Moreover, the consistency, uprightness, and preservation of opposites in one entity prove the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. Thus, the multiple aspects of indication all point to a single creator. Maturidi then notes that the proof of the originator is the inability of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge is that the world lacks knowledge of its own origin. Therefore, the existence of indication is based on difference, not agreement (i.e., the world is different from its creator, so it points to Him). Finally, he adds another principle: necessities and needs indicate that which is other than themselves. For if a dependent being itself could satisfy its own need, it would not require another. But if it requires another, that chain cannot go to infinity (tasalsul), which is invalid. Thus, there must be a self-sufficient being that ends the regress. This argument from need and infinite regress is a classic kalam proof for the existence of God.

Key Terms
ضروريات
حاجات
تسلسل
فاسد
غير
آخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an endless chain of causes is possible (infinite regress).
Correction: Maturidi argues that an infinite regress is invalid (fasid) because it would never provide a complete explanation; the chain must terminate in a self-sufficient being.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do necessities and needs (الضروريات والحاجات) indicate?
A) They indicate themselves.
B) They indicate something other than themselves.
C) They indicate an infinite regress is valid.
D) They indicate the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Dependent beings cannot form an infinite chain; there must be a self-sufficient creator to end the regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You depend on countless things every day: food, air, family, health. Have you ever honestly examined what your ultimate reliance truly rests on? What would it mean for your life if that ultimate support were itself dependent? How does that thought make you feel—and what will you do about it?
?
Maturidi says an infinite chain of needs is invalid. But you live as if your needs are met by finite things. Is your heart anchored to something that is itself in need? What would it take for you to consciously place your trust only in the Self-Sufficient?
?
The argument shows that 'وذلك فاسد' (that is invalid). Does your daily worry reflect a belief in an infinite regress of problems? How can this truth reshape your outlook on your current struggles, and what concrete step can you take today to break that cycle?

The World as Writing Points to a Creator
Maturidi uses the analogy of writing to prove a Creator without revealing His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the analogy of writing to argue for the existence of a Creator without claiming to know His essence.

Core Idea
The world's order points to a Maker, not His inner nature.

Maturidi begins with a simple observation: writing implies a writer, but doesn't tell us what that writer is like. He extends this to the entire universe—its intricate design demands a wise Creator, yet does not reveal His essence. This lesson unpacks that powerful analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source and its English translation to get the flow of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice how Maturidi compares writing to the world—both point to an agent without specifying its nature.
3
Understand the limits of inference
The passage stresses that we can know the Creator's existence and attributes of wisdom and knowledge, but not His essence or quality.
4
Reflect on the purpose of qiyas
Maturidi uses analogy (qiyas) as a necessary method to establish the Maker, but it has clear boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن الكتابة تدل على الكاتب، ومن لا يدل على كيفيته أو مثله لا يجوز أن يكون ملكا أو بشرا أو جنا، فتكون الكتابة غير دالة على مائية الكاتب وكيفيته ولا على مثلها، وهي تدل على كاتب ما، العالم بما فيه يدل على محدث ما لا يدل على كيفيته ومائيته، وكذلك البناء والنسج والنجر والصناعات؛ لذلك لزم القياس في إثبات صانع العالم بالعالم بما فيه من العجائب والأشياء التي لا يُحتمل كونها إلا بحكيم عليم، ولا يجب به تعرف الكيفية له والمائية، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And moreover, writing indicates the writer, and that which does not indicate his quality or likeness cannot be a king, a human, or a jinn. Thus writing does not indicate the essence (māhiyya) of the writer nor his quality, nor their likeness; rather it indicates a writer. The world with what is in it indicates an originator (muḥdith) without indicating his quality or essence. Similarly, building, weaving, carving, and crafts. Therefore analogy (qiyās) is necessary in establishing the Maker of the world through the world and the wonders and things in it that cannot exist except through a wise, knowing (Ḥakīm ʿAlīm) being. And it is not necessary that His quality and essence be known. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the analogy of writing to prove the existence of a Creator. The author first establishes that writing points to a writer without specifying the writer's nature (whether king, human, or jinn). Similarly, the world with its intricate order points to an originator (muḥdith) but does not reveal His essence (māhiyya) or quality (kayfiyya). This analogy is extended to other human crafts like building, weaving, and carving. The author then concludes that by analogy (qiyās), the wonders and order in the world necessitate a wise and knowing Maker (Ḥakīm ʿAlīm), but we are not required to comprehend His essence or quality. The passage ends with a reminder of reliance on Allah's power.

Key Terms
كتابة
كاتب
مائية
كيفية
محدث
قياس
حكيم عليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the analogy fully defines God's essence in detail.
Correction: Maturidi's analogy only proves existence and attributes of wisdom and knowledge, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's analogy, what does writing indicate about the writer?
A) His exact form and nature
B) That he is a king
C) Only that there is a writer
D) That he is a jinn

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's analogy shows that the world's order points to a wise Creator without revealing His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The world is like a book pointing to its Author. When I look at the sky, the mountains, or even my own existence, do I immediately recognize the Author, or do I stay absorbed in the book itself without looking to its Writer? What would change if I started seeing everything as a sign that demands a response to the Wise and Knowing Creator?
?
Maturidi teaches that the analogy of writing only proves that there is a writer, not his essence or qualities beyond wisdom and knowledge. In my relationship with God, am I content to know Him through what He has revealed of Himself in creation and scripture, or do I try to delve into what He has not disclosed, risking speculation? How can I humble myself to accept the limits of my knowledge?
?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.' If the entire proof of God's existence depends on recognizing our dependence on His power, how often do I live as if I am self-sufficient? Where in my daily life do I most forget that every breath is a sign of His power? What one action can I take today to actively remind myself of that dependence?

Proof from Finitude of Parts
Maturidi argues from the finitude of observed parts to the temporal origination of the whole world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the finitude of each observed part implies the finitude and thus temporal origination of the whole world.

Core Idea
If every part is finite, the whole must be finite — and therefore originated.

Maturidi begins his proof for the world's creation by pointing to a simple observation: everything we see has a limit or end. If each part of the world is finite, how can the whole be infinite? This logical puzzle forces us to accept that the world as a whole must have a beginning. In this lesson, we trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Note the key terms: 'نهاية' (finitude) and 'متناه' (finite).
The text has OCR uncertainties; focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify the main premise and objection
Maturidi states that every observed thing is finite. He anticipates an objection: perhaps the whole is not finite even though parts are. Understand why he considers this inconsistent.
He uses a parallel: if part can be finite while whole is not, then part could come from something while whole does not.
3
Analyze the additional spatial argument
Maturidi notes that parts serve as places for each other, but the whole cannot be in a place. This implies the whole is dependent and thus originated.
This reinforces the conclusion that the world must have a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إذ كل مشاهد ذو نهاية، وجعلتموه ذليل العالم، لم لا كان الكل كذلك؟ وإلا لو جاز كون شيء منه متناه، وجملته لا، لم لا جاز كون شيء منه عن شيء وجملته لا؟ وكذلك نرى بعضه لبعضه مكانا، ولا يحتمل جملته المكان لزوال الحمل، ولا قوة إلا بالله، وفي ذلك لزوم الحدث وما ذكرنا من البقاء قد بيناه فيما تقدم. بعد ذلك وما ذكر من التوهم فكذلك ما من وقت يتوهم إلا وأمكن توهم كونه ما إذا لم يجعل لأوليته وقت يبطل كله العالم أو أصله عما يحتمل من الحوادث الجاز أيضا وبعد، فإنه لو جاز إخلاء العالم قلب كل معقول، من جواز حي وميت في حال، فثبت حدث الكلية بما لا خلو عنه ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is said to them: Since every observed thing has an end, and you made it the proof of the world, why is the whole not likewise? Otherwise, if it is permissible for some of it to be finite while its entirety is not, why is it not permissible for some of it to be from something while its entirety is not? And similarly we see that some of it is a place for others, but its entirety cannot be in a place due to the cessation of bearing. And there is no power except with God. And in that is the necessity of temporal origination, and what we mentioned of permanence we have already clarified earlier. After that, and what was mentioned from imagination is likewise: there is no time imagined except that it is possible to imagine its existence if no time is made for its beginning, that would annul the whole world or its origin from what can bear accidents? Also, and after, if the world's being empty of [that] were permissible, it would overturn every rational [judgment], like the permissibility of a living and a dead in one state. So the temporal origination of the whole is established by that from which it cannot be empty, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments for the temporal origination (حدث) of the world. First, Maturidi argues that since every observed thing is finite (ذو نهاية), the whole world should also be finite. If a part can be finite while the whole is not, then why cannot a part come from something while the whole does not? Additionally, parts serve as places for other parts, but the whole cannot be in a place because it would have no container. This implies that the world is originated (حدث). Second, regarding imagination (توهم), at any time one can imagine the world's non-existence if no beginning is assigned; otherwise, if the world could be empty of accidents (حوادث), that would violate rationality (قلب كل معقول) by allowing impossible combinations like a living and dead in the same state. Therefore, since the world cannot be empty of accidents, it must have a beginning. Both arguments conclude that the whole world is originated.

Key Terms
نهاية (finitude)
متناه (finite)
حدث (temporal origination)
ذليل (likely 'دليل' proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The finitude of parts does not necessitate the finitude of the whole; the whole could be infinite even if parts are finite.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if such a disparity were allowed, then causality too could be inconsistent, and the spatial argument shows the whole's dependence, so the whole must be finite and originated.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of Maturidi's first argument?
A) The world is eternal because parts are finite.
B) The world must have a beginning because parts are finite and dependent.
C) The world is composed of infinite parts.
D) The world's finitude is irrelevant to its origin.

Key Takeaway
Because every observed part of the world is finite, the world as a whole must also be finite and thus had a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every part of your life is finite — your years, your health, your relationships — why do you often live as if the whole of your existence will never end? How will you orient yourself today in light of the undeniable finitude that this argument forces you to acknowledge?
?
The world cannot contain the world; it requires a Creator beyond it. In what area of your life are you trying to be self-contained, as if you need no Creator beyond your own strength? What would it look like to genuinely depend on God in that area?
?
Maturidi's argument shows that if the whole were infinite while its parts are finite, absurdity follows. Are there absurdities in your own life where you accept a whole (like your ego or ambitions) that is infinite in claim while its parts are clearly limited? Will you let this logical consistency reshape your self-assessment?

Proof from the Necessity of Accidents
Maturidi uses the concept of imagination and the necessity of accidents to prove the world's temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that since the world cannot be empty of temporal accidents, it must itself be temporal.

Core Idea
The world cannot exist without accidents, and accidents are temporal, so the world must have a beginning.

Maturidi presents a second, complementary proof: the world is never free from accidents (temporary events), and everything that never is free from what is temporal must itself be temporal. He also uses a thought experiment about imagining a time before the world to show its contingency. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the terms 'توهم' (imagination) and 'خلو' (emptiness).
The text has OCR issues; focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the imagination argument
Maturidi says that for any imagined point in time, one can imagine the world not existing if no beginning is assigned. This shows the world's contingency.
This leads to the possibility of the world being empty of accidents, which is impossible.
3
Grasp the conclusion from impossibility of emptiness
If the world could be empty of accidents, it would allow absurdities like a living and dead thing in the same state. Therefore, the world cannot be empty of accidents, so it must be originated.
The phrase 'ما لا خلو عنه' (that from which it is not empty) mandates a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إذ كل مشاهد ذو نهاية، وجعلتموه ذليل العالم، لم لا كان الكل كذلك؟ وإلا لو جاز كون شيء منه متناه، وجملته لا، لم لا جاز كون شيء منه عن شيء وجملته لا؟ وكذلك نرى بعضه لبعضه مكانا، ولا يحتمل جملته المكان لزوال الحمل، ولا قوة إلا بالله، وفي ذلك لزوم الحدث وما ذكرنا من البقاء قد بيناه فيما تقدم. بعد ذلك وما ذكر من التوهم فكذلك ما من وقت يتوهم إلا وأمكن توهم كونه ما إذا لم يجعل لأوليته وقت يبطل كله العالم أو أصله عما يحتمل من الحوادث الجاز أيضا وبعد، فإنه لو جاز إخلاء العالم قلب كل معقول، من جواز حي وميت في حال، فثبت حدث الكلية بما لا خلو عنه ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it is said to them: Since every observed thing has an end, and you made it the proof of the world, why is the whole not likewise? Otherwise, if it is permissible for some of it to be finite while its entirety is not, why is it not permissible for some of it to be from something while its entirety is not? And similarly we see that some of it is a place for others, but its entirety cannot be in a place due to the cessation of bearing. And there is no power except with God. And in that is the necessity of temporal origination, and what we mentioned of permanence we have already clarified earlier. After that, and what was mentioned from imagination is likewise: there is no time imagined except that it is possible to imagine its existence if no time is made for its beginning, that would annul the whole world or its origin from what can bear accidents? Commentary
This passage contains two arguments for the temporal origination (حدث) of the world. First, Maturidi argues that since every observed thing is finite (ذو نهاية), the whole world should also be finite. If a part can be finite while the whole is not, then why cannot a part come from something while the whole does not? Additionally, parts serve as places for other parts, but the whole cannot be in a place because it would have no container. This implies that the world is originated (حدث). Therefore, since the world cannot be empty of accidents, it must have a beginning. Both arguments conclude that the whole world is originated.

حوادث (accidents)
خلو (emptiness)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could be eternal even if it always contains accidents, because accidents could be eternal too.
Correction: Maturidi holds that accidents are inherently temporal (they change), so whatever is never without them must also be temporal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue would happen if the world could be empty of accidents?
A) The world would become eternal.
B) Rational judgments would be overturned, allowing contradictions.
C) Accidents would cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
