Argument from Food's Transformation Requires a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues that dead food becoming living requires a wise manager.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the transformation of dead food into living tissue to argue for the existence of a wise Creator and the origination of the world.

Core Idea
Dead food becoming alive cannot happen without a wise manager.

Maturidi presents a concrete example from everyday life—food turning into living flesh—to demonstrate that such a change cannot be random or self-generated. He argues that only the management of a wise agent can produce this transformation, which in turn proves the origination of the world. Let us examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source passage once without analysis. Then compare it to the literal translation to grasp the surface meaning.
Notice the key terms: موات (dead matter) and تدبير (management).
2
Identify the key premise
Maturidi states that food is from dead matter and cannot become what it is (living tissue) without the management (تدبير) of a wise manager. Identify this as the core observation.
3
Trace the argument to its conclusion
From the impossibility of such change without management, Maturidi concludes that the world must be originated (حدث العالم) because a part of it undergoes change. Ask yourself: why does a change in a part imply the whole is created?
The argument is based on the idea that if a part of the world is contingent upon a cause, then the whole world is also contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن الأغذية من موات، لا يُحتمل أن تصير كذلك إلا بتدبير مدبر عليم لا أن استفاد ذلك المعنى من غيره بلا تدبير، وفى ذلك لزوم القول بالذي قلنا يجب أو إن كان حدث شيء منه أو بعضه - لا أن كان في شيء مما ذكر القول بحدث العالم بما لزم في بعضه
That food is from dead matter; it cannot become so except by the management of a wise manager, not that it gained that meaning from something else without management. And in that is the necessity of the statement which we have said: it must be, or if something or some part of it originated - not that in something of what we mentioned is the statement of the origination of the world by what is necessary in some of it.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the transformation of food (dead matter) into living tissue. Maturidi states that such a change cannot occur without the wise management (تدبير) of a manager (مدبر). The alternative—that the food gained life without deliberate management—is impossible. Therefore, the origination of the world (حدث العالم) is necessitated, because the change in a part implies origination in the whole.

Key Terms
موات
تدبير
مدبر عليم
حدث العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transformation of dead food into living flesh happens spontaneously or by natural forces alone without a guiding agent.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that such a transformation cannot occur without the deliberate management (تدبير) of a wise manager, indicating the necessity of a Creator.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the transformation of dead food into living matter?
A) It happens by nature.
B) It requires a wise manager.
C) It is impossible.
D) It proves the eternity of matter.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Finitude of Parts Necessitates the Origination of the Whole
Maturidi argues that the world's parts are finite, so the whole must be originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that if the parts of the world are finite, then the whole must also be finite and therefore originated.

Core Idea
If every part is finite, the whole cannot be infinite.

Maturidi presents a concise logical argument against those who accept that the world's parts are finite but deny that the whole is originated. He shows that such a position leads to absurd consequences, such as allowing a part to come from nothing while the whole does not. Understanding this argument strengthens the rational foundation for the world's contingency and need for a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the key terms: متناه, جملة, حدث, مكان. Then read the literal translation.
The text has some OCR issues, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Maturidi addresses someone who admits parts are finite but denies the whole is originated. Identify this position in the passage.
Look for the conditional 'ولو جاز...'.
3
Follow the analogy
Maturidi uses an analogy: if a part can be finite while the whole is not, then by the same logic a part could come from something while the whole does not. Trace this reasoning.
4
Consider the spatial argument
Observe that parts occupy space within each other, but the whole cannot occupy a place. This inconsistency shows the whole must be originated.
The phrase 'ولا يحتمل جملته المكان' is key.
5
Conclude with the lesson
Maturidi concludes that the world is necessarily originated. Reflect on how this argument supports the existence of a Creator.
This connects to the broader theme of divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إذ كل مشاهد ذو نهاية، وجعلتموه ذليل العالم، ليم - لا كان الكل كذلك؟ وإلا لو جاز كون شيء منه متناه، وجملته لا، لم لا جاز كون شيء منه عن شيء وحملته لا؟ وكذلك نرى بعضه لبعضه مكانا، ولا يحتمل جملته المكان لزوال الحمل، ولا قوة إلا بالله، وفى ذلك لزوم الحدث وما ذكرنا من البقاء قد بيناه فيما تقدم.
Then it is said to them: Since every observed thing has an end, and you have made it the humble of the world? - is not the whole thus? Otherwise if it is possible that something of it is finite and its totality not, why is it not possible that something of it is from something and its totality not? Likewise we see some of it a place for some of it, and its totality does not bear the place due to the removal of bearing, and there is no power except with Allah. And in that is the necessity of origination, and what we mentioned of remaining we have explained previously.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
Maturidi addresses an opponent who accepts that parts of the world are finite but denies the whole is originated. He argues: if a part is finite (متناه), then the whole must also be finite and thus originated. Otherwise, the same logic would allow a part to come from nothing while the whole does not—which is absurd. He also notes that parts occupy place within each other, but the whole cannot occupy a place because that would imply removal. This inconsistency proves that the world is originated (حادث).

Key Terms
متناه
جملة
حدث
مكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The whole could be eternal even if all its parts are finite and originated.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if every part is finite, then the whole composed of them must also be finite. Otherwise, one could inconsistently claim that a part can come from nothing while the whole does not.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Maturidi draw from the finitude of the world's parts?
A) The whole must also be finite and originated.
B) The whole can be eternal.
C) Parts are not truly finite.
D) The world is not real.

Key Takeaway
If every part of the world is finite, then the world as a whole must also be finite and therefore originated, implying a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are made of finite cells, atoms, and moments. If every part of you is finite, how can you pretend that your time on earth is infinite or that your existence is self-sufficient? What does this say about your need for the Creator every moment, and what concrete step will you take today to acknowledge that dependence?
?
This passage shows that the whole cannot be in a place. Your body occupies space, yet its totality is not self-contained. Are you aware that your very existence is dependent on a power beyond you? How would you live differently today if you truly felt that your being is originated every instant—will you pause to thank Allah for your next breath?

Proof from Life and Death that the World is Originated
Maturidi uses the accidentality of life and death to prove the world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the alternation of life and death demonstrates that the world is originated.

Core Idea
Life and death are accidents that prove the world's origination.

Maturidi presents a concise rational proof that the world cannot be eternal. By examining the common experience of life and death, he shows that both are originated events that occur in a subject. This leads to the conclusion that the subject itself—the world—must also be originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the proof is very short and direct.
2
Understand the argument about life
The passage states that life cannot exist in a dead thing because life gives life. Therefore, life must be an originated accident.
3
Understand the argument about death
Death occurs after life, so it too is an originated accident.
4
Connect both to the world's origination
Since both life and death are originated accidents that inhere in the world, the world itself must be originated and not eternal.
This is a standard kalam argument: changing accidents prove the contingency of the substance.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If life and death come and go, can the world be eternal? What does this say about the need for a Creator?
This connects to the broader discussion of divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدث العالم إحالة كون حياة فى ميت لأنه به يحيى، ثبت أن حياة الأشياء حدث، فكذلك مونها؛ إذ قد يكون بعد الحياة
And the proof of the origination of the world is the impossibility of life being in a dead thing because it [life] gives life to it. It is established that the life of things is originated, and likewise their death; since it [death] may occur after life.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
Maturidi presents a concise proof: life cannot inhere in something already dead because life itself gives life. Therefore, the life that appears in things is originated (حادث). Similarly, death is also originated, as it occurs after life. Since both life and death are accidents that occur in a subject, that subject (the world) must itself be originated (not eternal). This is a standard kalam argument: the changing accidents prove the contingency of the substance.

Key Terms

موت
حادث
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think life is an eternal property of living things.
Correction: Maturidi shows life is an accident that comes and goes, proving it is originated.

Checkpoint
What does the alternation of life and death prove according to Maturidi?
A) That the world is eternal.
B) That life and death are both originated.
C) That death is not real.
D) That life is self-subsisting.

Key Takeaway
The alternation of life and death proves that both are originated accidents, demonstrating that the world itself is originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience life every day, but does the fact that life is an accident—a temporary gift—change how you value the time you have? What would you do differently starting today if you truly believed your next breath is not guaranteed?
?
Maturidi proves that death is an originated accident that comes after life. Have you ever genuinely contemplated that your own death is not a distant possibility but an inevitable, near event? How does that realization demand a reordering of your priorities right now?
?
If life and death are both accidents that prove the world's contingency, then your entire existence hangs on the Will of the Necessary Being. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or are you actively acknowledging your complete dependence on Allah in every moment—and what one concrete act today would prove that?

Proof of the World's Createdness from Life and Death
Maturidi presents a proof from life and death for the world's createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the impossibility of life in a dead thing to prove that the world is created.

Core Idea
Life cannot arise from mere death, proving it is created—so too the world.

Maturidi presents a concise proof for the createdness of the world, grounded in a simple observation about life and death. He argues that since life cannot exist in a dead thing, life itself must be originated. By analogy, the world—which comes after non-existence—is also created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the opening statement about God as the maker of examples.
2
Identify the premise
The proof rests on the impossibility of life existing in a dead thing. Why is that impossible? Because life gives life to others; a dead thing cannot produce life.
3
Trace the conclusion
From the premise, Maturidi concludes that life is created (hadith). Then he extends the argument: 'likewise its [?]'—either its death or existence—showing that the world is also created.
The word 'مونها' is ambiguous but the analogy is consistent: the world comes after non-existence as life comes after death.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Ask yourself: How does the createdness of life imply the createdness of the world? The underlying principle is that anything that is preceded by non-existence must be created.
This connects to the broader kalam argument for the world's contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردت المثال، جل ربنا عن ذلك، بل هو الجاعل لكل ذي المثال مثلا وهو منشى ذلك. ودليل حدث العالم إحالة كون حياة في ميت لأنه به يحيى، ثبت أن حياة الأشياء حدث، فكذلك [مونها]؛ إذ قد يكون بعد الحياة.
And if you want an example—exalted is our Lord above that—rather He is the maker for every possessor of an example of an example, and He is the originator of that. And the proof of the creation of the world is the impossibility of existence of life in a dead thing, because by it it lives; so it is established that the life of things is created, and likewise its [?] (existence? death?) ; for it may come after life.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the createdness (hadath) of the world. It begins by affirming that God is the creator of all examples and likenesses. Then it provides the proof: it is impossible for life to exist in a dead thing, because life brings life to others; thus life itself must be created (hadith). Similarly, the world (or its existence) is created, because it can come after non-existence, just as life comes after death. The proof relies on the observed fact that dead things do not generate life, so the life in living things must be originated. This implies that the world, which contains life and death, is also originated.

Refuting Dualism: Two Eternal Principles Imply Contingency
Maturidi argues that two eternal principles require external causes for separation and mixing, proving their contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the logical implications of mixing and separation to refute the idea of two co-eternal principles.

Core Idea
Two eternal principles cannot change or interact without an external cause, proving they are temporal.

Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument against dualism. He shows that if two principles are eternal and self-subsistent, their mixing and separation would require an external cause, contradicting their self-sufficiency. This passage demonstrates how logical reasoning cuts to the heart of theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the compressed style and key terms: تباين (separation) and إمتزاج (mixing).
The text has an ambiguous phrase marked with [؟], but the overall argument is clear.
2
Follow the argument about contradictory attributes
The passage begins by listing contradictory attributes (moving/still, alive/dead, etc.) that would apply if both principles are self-subsistent. Identify the logical problem: such attributes cannot coexist without an external cause.
3
Understand the analogical reasoning
Maturidi draws an analogy: just as states (e.g., movement) change in entities and require an external cause, so separation and mixing require an external cause. This demonstrates that the principles are not self-sufficient and thus temporal.
The conclusion is that their temporality (حدث) is necessitated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكونا متحركين بأنفسهما ساكنين حيين ميتين قاعدين قائمين ما يفسد مع أن يكون التباين لنفس يقع ، ثم امتزاج بما كان به التباين . ألا ترى أن الأحوال تتغير بالأعيان لم يجز وجودها إلا بغير ، فكذلك التباين والإمتزاج ، فثبت أن ما بغير همنا[؟] امتزجا وبغيرهما كانا متباينين وذلك يوجب حدثهما .
[If] they be moving by themselves, still, alive, dead, sitting, standing, what corrupts [the idea] is that separation occurs for itself, then mixing with what separation was by. Do you not see that states change with the entities, their existence is not permitted except by another, so likewise separation and mixing. So it is established that what by other than them[؟] mixed, and by other than them were separate, and that necessitates their temporality.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the dualist claim of two eternal principles (light and darkness) is untenable. The argument proceeds by considering the logical consequences of their separation and mixing. The author begins by listing contradictory attributes (moving/still, alive/dead, sitting/standing) that would apply if both principles are self-subsistent and similar, pointing out that such a situation is corrupt. The key point is that if separation occurs for no external reason but for itself, then mixing also would be by something other than the principles. The author then makes an analogical argument: just as states (like movement, stillness) change in entities and require an external cause for their existence, so too separation and mixing require an external cause. Therefore, the fact that two principles mix or separate due to something other than themselves proves that they are not self-sufficient and thus not eternal; they are temporal (hadath).

Key Terms
التباين
الإمتزاج
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God can create opposites or that opposites can coexist in the same subject.
Correction: The argument specifically targets the dualist claim of two co-eternal principles, not the coexistence of attributes in creation. Maturidi is showing that self-sufficient eternal beings cannot depend on external causes for their states.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why does the fact that two principles mix due to something else prove they are temporal?
A) Because mixing indicates they are composed of parts.
B) Because relying on an external cause for a state implies contingency, not eternity.
C) Because mixing implies they are not light and darkness.
D) Because mixing is a change, and change implies time.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by demonstrating that two eternal principles would depend on an external cause for their mixing and separation, proving they are temporal and not truly eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If two eternal principles are impossible because they would need an external cause, what does that mean for your own belief in the Oneness of God? Do you truly affirm that nothing shares in His eternity, or do you sometimes act as if there are other forces independent of Him? How can you root out hidden dualisms in your heart that make you rely on created things as if they were eternal?
?
Consider the analogical argument: just as changes in the world require a cause, so too separation and mixing require a cause. When you experience changes in your own life—shifts in mood, circumstance, or belief—do you acknowledge that they too come from God alone, or do you attribute them to other causes? What does this passage teach you about attributing causality to anything other than Allah?
?
The source challenges the idea of self-sufficient eternal principles. In your daily life, what do you treat as if it were eternal and self-sufficient—your wealth, health, relationships? How does Maturidi's argument expose the fragility of these things and call you to rely solely on the One? Make a concrete plan to break one attachment that you have wrongly eternalized.

Refuting Dualist Justification for Slaughter
Maturidi critiques dualist reasoning on slaughter and exposes contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi critiques the dualist argument linking slaughter to separation of light and darkness, and exposes contradictions in their view on good and evil.

Core Idea
If evil is never from light, then confession of murder exposes dualists' incoherence.

The dualists claimed that slaughter separates the luminous soul from the dark body, making it permissible. Maturidi turns this argument back on them, showing that their own premises about good and evil lead to logical absurdities regarding murder and confession.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualist claim
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi presents the dualist reasoning for permitting slaughter: it separates light from darkness.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice how Maturidi shows that if the murderer is from the principle of evil, his confession is truthful but admits sin; if from good, he lies but speaks evil. This proves dualism leads to incoherence.
3
Reflect on the wisdom point
Consider Maturidi's remark that inability to see wisdom in creating evil does not force dualism. This is a key epistemological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم و بعد ، فإنهم يقولون بحرمة الذبائح، وأحق من يحل هم ؛ إذ بها التفريق بين الجسد المظلم وبين الروح المضئ ، وبين النور الجلى والظلمة الستارة ، ذلك / وصفوا النور بأنه رقيق دراك، وبالروح ذلك لا بالظلمة ، فيجب حل الذبح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وأصله أنهم ينكرون الشر من جوهر الخير ، والخير من جوهر الشر، هذا الذي حملهم على القول باثنين قد أثبتوا الإقرار بالقتل ، وبما هو عندهم معصية ، فلو كان من غير الذي منه القتل فقد كذب، وهو شر ، ولو كان منه فقد صدق بالاقرار بالمعصية، ثون أن العجز عن إدراك المحكمة فى خلق الشر لا يضطر إلى القول باثنين . فيه تحقيقه أيضا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنهم أحق الخلق في الإمتناع عن النطق بالحكمة أو طلب العلم لأن قولهم : إن جوهر النور لا يجى منه شر قط. والجهل شر ، فإن كان من ذلك الجوهر فهو عالم جوهرة حكيم به، لا يحتمل الجهل ولا السفه، والتعلم وطلب الحكمة حق الجهال بهما ، وإن كان من جوهر الشر فإنه لا ينجع فيه ؛ لأن جوهره لا يقبل ولا يحتمل الخير. وإذا كان كذلك بطلت مناظرتهم ودعواهم و العالم ؛ لأن مناظرتهم في ذلك لو كانت جوهر النور كان هو عالماً جوهر الظلمة كان غير قابل ولا قبل المناظرة فلا معنى لها ، ولو كانت مع مستمع له فهو عبث . فلا بد من تحقيق الجهل والعلم في جوهر [كل] منها ليصح ذلك المعنى ، وفى ذلك جمع الأمرين في أحدهما ، وذلك المعنى ألزمهم القول باثنين ، فبطل بحمد الله والأصل فيه أن التكلم منهم بالحكمة لا بالحكمة لا يعدو إما أن تكلموا إما أن تكلموا بجوهرهم وهو يعلم فيخرج مخرج العبث ، أو يجهله المكلَّم، وإيهما كان ففيه ثبات الأمرين من غير جوهره، فإنه لا يخلو أيضا من قبول أو عبث ، وأيهما كان من واحد أو ففى ذلك ما قلنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم يقال : إذ القول بأن لا / يجوز أن يكون واحد يجي منه خير وشير ؛ ومن هذا قوله كيف كان منها العالم الذي كل واحد منهم هذا وصفه ، فينتقض عليها فعلها ما لذلك أدعى لما ذلك . أترى سفها أعظم مما عملاهما بأنفسها أو جهلا أبين من ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then after that, they say regarding the prohibition of slaughter, and who has more right to permit it? Because by it is the separation between the dark body and the luminous spirit, and between the clear light and the covering darkness. That/they described light as subtle and penetrating, and by the spirit that, not by darkness, so slaughter is permitted. And there is no power except with God. And its origin is that they deny evil from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil; this is what led them to say there are two. They have established the confession to murder, and what is in their view disobedience. If the murderer is from other than that which murder comes from, then they have lied, and it is evil; and if he is from it, then they have spoken truth by confessing to disobedience. Then that the inability to perceive wisdom in the creation of evil does not force [one] to say there are two. In it is its verification as well, and no power except with God. Moreover, they are the most deserving of creation to refrain from speaking wisdom or seeking knowledge, because their saying: indeed the essence of light never produces evil. And ignorance is evil. If the speaker is from that essence, then he is a knower, a wise essence, it does not admit ignorance or foolishness, and learning and seeking wisdom is the right of the ignorant to both. If he is from the essence of evil, then it is not effective in him, because his essence does not accept nor admit good. If that is so, their debate and claim and the world are invalidated; because their debate in that, if it were the essence of light - he is knowing, the essence of darkness is not accepting nor before the debate, so it has no meaning; and if it were with a listener, it is frivolous. So it is necessary to verify ignorance and knowledge in the essence [each] of them to make that meaning correct, and in that is the combining of both matters in one of them, and that meaning obliged them to say there are two, so it is invalidated by the praise of God. And the basis of it is that the speaker among them regarding wisdom - not regarding wisdom? - does not exceed either they speak by their essence while he knows, so it comes out as frivolous, or the speaker is ignorant of it, and whichever it is, in it is the establishment of both matters without his essence. For it is not free from acceptance or frivolity, and whichever it is from one or? then in that is what we said, and no power except with God. Then it is said: because the saying that it is not permissible that one [source] produce good and evil; and from this is his saying: how is it that from it the world is such that each one of them has this description? So their act is refuted by what they claim for that. Do you see a greater foolishness than what they both did by themselves, or a more obvious ignorance than that, and no power except with God.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several dualist arguments. First, the author addresses the dualist justification for animal slaughter: that it separates the dark body from the luminous soul. He then points out that their position on good and evil leads to contradictions in confession of murder. If the murderer is from the principle of evil, his confession is truthful but admits sin; if from the principle of good, he lies but speaks evil. The author then argues that the inability to understand the wisdom in creating evil does not force dualism. Next, he critiques the claim that the essence of light never produces evil: if so, a person speaking wisdom must be from light, and if from darkness, he cannot accept wisdom, making their debates pointless. Therefore, they must combine both principles in one essence, invalidating dualism. The second part examines the act of speaking wisdom: if the speaker speaks from his essence knowingly, it is frivolous; if ignorantly, it involves both knowledge and ignorance, again requiring a single source. Finally, the author refutes the assumption that one source cannot produce both good and evil, calling it foolishness, as their own actions demonstrate.

Key Terms
الذبيحة
النور
الظلمة
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the inability to see wisdom in evil proves dualism.
Correction: Maturidi argues that this inability does not necessitate two principles; it only reflects human limitation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the dualist argument that slaughter separates light from darkness?
A) He agrees with it.
B) He refutes it by showing contradictions in their view on good and evil.
C) He remains silent.
D) He argues that slaughter is prohibited.

Key Takeaway
Dualism collapses because it cannot coherently account for confession of murder or the existence of evil without internal contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness or experience evil, have you ever found yourself doubting God's unity or attributing evil to a source separate from Him? How does Maturidi's exposure of dualist contradictions challenge you to instead trust in God's wisdom, even when you cannot fully comprehend it? This week, when a troubling event occurs, will you consciously reject the temptation to blame a 'second power' and instead turn to God in submission?
?
Maturidi argues that our inability to understand the wisdom behind evil does not justify false beliefs about God. Are there areas in your life where you have used confusion or lack of understanding as an excuse to adopt a comfortable but false explanation? Commit today to seek knowledge and to accept that your limited intellect cannot encompass all of God's wisdom, and let that recognition deepen your reliance on Him.

The Impossibility of Dualism: Light and Knowledge
Maturidi refutes dualist claim that light never produces evil, showing it forces a single source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the dualist claim about light never producing evil undermines their ability to speak wisdom and forces a single source.

Core Idea
If light produces no evil, dualists cannot even debate without combining good and evil in one essence.

Maturidi continues his refutation by analyzing the dualist assertion that the essence of light never produces evil. He shows that if this were true, then anyone speaking wisdom must be from light, and anyone from darkness cannot accept wisdom, making their debates pointless. This forces them to combine both principles in one source, invalidating dualism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the light premise
The dualists say light never produces evil, and ignorance is evil. Read how Maturidi uses this to examine who can speak wisdom.
2
Follow the debate logic
If a speaker is from light, he already knows wisdom, so his speech is frivolous. If from darkness, he cannot accept wisdom, so debate is meaningless. Thus dualism makes rational discourse impossible.
3
Consider the final refutation
Maturidi concludes that the assumption that one source cannot produce both good and evil is foolish, as dualists' own actions show they combine both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم و بعد ، فإنهم يقولون بحرمة الذبائح، وأحق من يحل هم ؛ إذ بها التفريق بين الجسد المظلم وبين الروح المضئ ، وبين النور الجلى والظلمة الستارة ، ذلك / وصفوا النور بأنه رقيق دراك، وبالروح ذلك لا بالظلمة ، فيجب حل الذبح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وأصله أنهم ينكرون الشر من جوهر الخير ، والخير من جوهر الشر، هذا الذي حملهم على القول باثنين قد أثبتوا الإقرار بالقتل ، وبما هو عندهم معصية ، فلو كان من غير الذي منه القتل فقد كذب، وهو شر ، ولو كان منه فقد صدق بالاقرار بالمعصية، ثون أن العجز عن إدراك المحكمة فى خلق الشر لا يضطر إلى القول باثنين . فيه تحقيقه أيضا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنهم أحق الخلق في الإمتناع عن النطق بالحكمة أو طلب العلم لأن قولهم : إن جوهر النور لا يجى منه شر قط. والجهل شر ، فإن كان من ذلك الجوهر فهو عالم جوهرة حكيم به، لا يحتمل الجهل ولا السفه، والتعلم وطلب الحكمة حق الجهال بهما ، وإن كان من جوهر الشر فإنه لا ينجع فيه ؛ لأن جوهره لا يقبل ولا يحتمل الخير. وإذا كان كذلك بطلت مناظرتهم ودعواهم و العالم ؛ لأن مناظرتهم في ذلك لو كانت جوهر النور كان هو عالماً جوهر الظلمة كان غير قابل ولا قبل المناظرة فلا معنى لها ، ولو كانت مع مستمع له فهو عبث . فلا بد من تحقيق الجهل والعلم في جوهر [كل] منها ليصح ذلك المعنى ، وفى ذلك جمع الأمرين في أحدهما ، وذلك المعنى ألزمهم القول باثنين ، فبطل بحمد الله والأصل فيه أن التكلم منهم بالحكمة لا بالحكمة لا يعدو إما أن تكلموا إما أن تكلموا بجوهرهم وهو يعلم فيخرج مخرج العبث ، أو يجهله المكلَّم، وإيهما كان ففيه ثبات الأمرين من غير جوهره، فإنه لا يخلو أيضا من قبول أو عبث ، وأيهما كان من واحد أو ففى ذلك ما قلنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم يقال : إذ القول بأن لا / يجوز أن يكون واحد يجي منه خير وشير ؛ ومن هذا قوله كيف كان منها العالم الذي كل واحد منهم هذا وصفه ، فينتقض عليها فعلها ما لذلك أدعى لما ذلك . أترى سفها أعظم مما عملاهما بأنفسها أو جهلا أبين من ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then after that, they say regarding the prohibition of slaughter, and who has more right to permit it? Because by it is the separation between the dark body and the luminous spirit, and between the clear light and the covering darkness. That/they described light as subtle and penetrating, and by the spirit that, not by darkness, so slaughter is permitted. And there is no power except with God. And its origin is that they deny evil from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil; this is what led them to say there are two. They have established the confession to murder, and what is in their view disobedience. If the murderer is from other than that which murder comes from, then they have lied, and it is evil; and if he is from it, then they have spoken truth by confessing to disobedience. Then that the inability to perceive wisdom in the creation of evil does not force [one] to say there are two. In it is its verification as well, and no power except with God. Moreover, they are the most deserving of creation to refrain from speaking wisdom or seeking knowledge, because their saying: indeed the essence of light never produces evil. And ignorance is evil. If the speaker is from that essence, then he is a knower, a wise essence, it does not admit ignorance or foolishness, and learning and seeking wisdom is the right of the ignorant to both. If he is from the essence of evil, then it is not effective in him, because his essence does not accept nor admit good. If that is so, their debate and claim and the world are invalidated; because their debate in that, if it were the essence of light - he is knowing, the essence of darkness is not accepting nor before the debate, so it has no meaning; and if it were with a listener, it is frivolous. So it is necessary to verify ignorance and knowledge in the essence [each] of them to make that meaning correct, and in that is the combining of both matters in one of them, and that meaning obliged them to say there are two, so it is invalidated by the praise of God. And the basis of it is that the speaker among them regarding wisdom - not regarding wisdom? - does not exceed either they speak by their essence while he knows, so it comes out as frivolous, or the speaker is ignorant of it, and whichever it is, in it is the establishment of both matters without his essence. For it is not free from acceptance or frivolity, and whichever it is from one or? then in that is what we said, and no power except with God. Then it is said: because the saying that it is not permissible that one [source] produce good and evil; and from this is his saying: how is it that from it the world is such that each one of them has this description? So their act is refuted by what they claim for that. Do you see a greater foolishness than what they both did by themselves, or a more obvious ignorance than that, and no power except with God.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several dualist arguments. First, the author addresses the dualist justification for animal slaughter: that it separates the dark body from the luminous soul. He then points out that their position on good and evil leads to contradictions in confession of murder. If the murderer is from the principle of evil, his confession is truthful but admits sin; if from the principle of good, he lies but speaks evil. The author then argues that the inability to understand the wisdom in creating evil does not force dualism. Next, he critiques the claim that the essence of light never produces evil: if so, a person speaking wisdom must be from light, and if from darkness, he cannot accept wisdom, making their debates pointless. Therefore, they must combine both principles in one essence, invalidating dualism. The second part examines the act of speaking wisdom: if the speaker speaks from his essence knowingly, it is frivolous; if ignorantly, it involves both knowledge and ignorance, again requiring a single source. Finally, the author refutes the assumption that one source cannot produce both good and evil, calling it foolishness, as their own actions demonstrate.

الظلمة
الجهل
العلم
الحكمة
المناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That light never producing evil justifies dualism.
Correction: Maturidi shows this premise leads to absurdities in discourse and forces a single source.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the dualist claim that light never produces evil invalidate their debates?
A) Because debates require both good and evil.
B) Because if a speaker is from light, he is already wise, making speech frivolous; if from darkness, he cannot accept wisdom, so debate is meaningless.
C) Because light and darkness cannot interact.
D) Because dualists are not intelligent.

Key Takeaway
Dualism collapses because it renders rational discourse impossible and forces the combination of opposites in a single source.

In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to separate your 'good self' from your 'bad self', and what would it mean to acknowledge that both come from the same source? ?

Where in your own life do you try to compartmentalize your faith—perhaps attending to 'spiritual' matters on one hand and 'worldly' affairs on the other—as if they don't intermingle? How does this lesson challenge you to integrate every part of your life under the single authority of Allah? Commit to one concrete change that brings your daily actions under that unity.
?

When you speak about Islam, are your words rooted in genuine knowledge and sincerity, or are they empty rhetoric that fails to transform your heart? Two Usages of 'Thing'
Maturidi enumerates two usages of 'thing' and illustrates that names do not imply similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways 'thing' is used in theological discussion and why agreement in names does not imply similarity.

Core Idea
Names do not bind realities; shared names do not imply shared attributes.

Maturidi begins by clarifying the term 'thing' (shayʾ), which is central to affirming God's existence. He distinguishes two usages: one where 'thing' functions as a mere name, and another where it signifies existence. This distinction is crucial because some mistakenly think that applying the same name to God and creation implies similarity. Maturidi uses the example of 'unique' to show that agreement in naming can coexist with complete dissimilarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on two usages
Read the first part of the source where Maturidi states there are two expressions concerning 'thing'. Focus on the distinction between using it as a name and as an affirmation.
2
Understand the principle of name agreement
Note that agreement in names does not necessitate similarity. Maturidi gives the example of calling someone 'unique' even though others are also called 'unique' in a different sense.
This refutes the idea that sharing a name implies shared attributes.
3
Reflect on other examples
Consider the examples of 'unbelief' and 'Islam' – they share the grammatical category 'name' but have opposite meanings. The same applies to movements and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في القول بالشيء عبارتان : إحداهما أن يجعل الشيء اسما ، والموافقة في الاسماء لا توجب التشابه ، لما قد يستعمل فى موضع نفى الموافقة فى المعنى نحو أن يقال : 'فلان' واحد عصره . وواحد قومه على نفى أن يكون له فيهم نظير أو شبيه من الوجه الذى أريد ، وإن كانوا جميعا في تسمية الواحد شركاء . ولو كانت الموافقة في الاسم توجب التشابه لا يحتمل استعماله فى موضع إرادة نفي الموافقة ، وكذلك تجد قول كفر وإسلام على تحقيق الاسم لكل واحد منها والموافقة من حيث القول ولكن المعنى متناقض ، وكذا ذلك في الحركات والأفعال ونحو ذلك. ودليل إثبات القول بالشيء وجهان : أحدهما السمع من قوله : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ، ولو لم يكن هو شيئا لم عنه شيئية الأشياء باسم الشيئية ؛ إذ الشيء في التحقيق خلاف ما لا يحتمل القول بالشيء ، وكذلك قوله : قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَادَةً قُلْ اللَّهُ شَهِيدٌ فلو لم يكن يقع عليه اسم الشيء لكان لا يحتمل تضمنه ذلك القول حتى ينسب إليه ، وأما العقل فهو أن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غير في العرف ؛ إذ القول بلا شيء نفى لم يرد له عن التصغير [؟] ، فثبت أنه اسم الإثبات ونفى التعطيل ، فإن كان قوم لا يعرفون أن معنى الشيء الإثبات والخروج من التعطيل يتقى ذلك كراهة أن يعتقد قلوبهم معنى مكروها ويقولون بالهستيه ، فإنه بإنتهم أوضح في معنى الإثبات ، وإن كانا واحدا عند أهل العلم بهذا اللسان.
And we have two expressions concerning the statement about 'thing': One of them is to make 'thing' a name, and agreement in names does not necessitate similarity, because it may be used in the context of negating agreement in meaning, such as when it is said: 'So-and-so is the unique of his age and the unique of his people' to negate that he has a likeness or similitude in them from the aspect intended, even though they all share in the naming of 'unique'. And if agreement in name necessitated similarity, it would not be permissible to use it in the context of intending negation of agreement. Similarly, you find the saying 'unbelief' and 'Islam' for the verification of the name for each of them, and agreement in terms of speech, but the meaning is contradictory. And likewise for movements, actions, and the like. And the proof for affirming the statement about 'thing' has two aspects: The first is transmitted (samʿ) from His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11). And if He were not a thing, He would not have negated from Him the 'thingness' of things by the name 'thingness'; for 'thing' in reality is the opposite of what does not admit the statement about 'thing'. And similarly His saying: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is Witness' (Quran 6:19). For if the name 'thing' did not apply to Him, it would not be possible for it to contain that statement until it is attributed to Him. As for reason (ʿaql), it is that 'thingness' is the name of affirmation only in convention; for the statement 'without thing' is a negation that has not been intended for the diminutive [؟]. So it is established that it is the name of affirmation and negation of nullification (taʿṭīl). If some people do not know that the meaning of 'thing' is affirmation and exemption from nullification, that is avoided lest their hearts believe an objectionable meaning, and they say 'hastī' (existence) in Persian, for it is clearer in the meaning of affirmation, even though they are the same for the people of knowledge of this language.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage classifies and proves. First, Maturidi gives two ways 'thing' is used: as a mere name (where agreement in name does not imply similarity—illustrated by 'unique' applied to a person but negating any equal), and as an affirmation of existence. He emphasizes that shared names do not necessitate shared realities. He then provides two proofs for affirming 'thing' for God: (1) Scriptural proof: Quran 42:11 negates any likeness to Him, which would be meaningless if He were not a 'thing'; also Quran 6:19 asks 'what thing is greatest in testimony?' and answers 'Allah', so the name 'thing' applies to Him. (2) Rational proof: In common usage, 'thing' means affirmation; saying 'no thing' is negation. Thus, calling God 'thing' affirms His existence and negates denial (taʿṭīl). He notes that some use Persian 'hastī' to avoid misunderstanding, but the meaning is the same for knowledgeable Arabic speakers.

Key Terms
شيء
تشابه
اسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That calling God and creation by the same name (e.g., 'thing') implies they share attributes or are similar.
Correction: Maturidi shows that agreement in names does not necessitate similarity. Names are conventional; the same name can apply to completely different realities.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi use to show that agreement in names does not necessitate similarity?
A) The term 'unique' applied to a person
B) The term 'profession' applied to different jobs
C) The term 'father' applied to different men
D) The term 'book' applied to different texts

Key Takeaway
The first usage of 'thing' is as a mere name, and sharing a name does not imply sharing attributes or reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'al-'Alim' (the All-Knowing) and yourself 'knowledgeable', what prevents you from inadvertently imagining a scaled-up version of your own knowledge? How can Maturidi's insight reshape your understanding of divine names?
?
Have you ever used a shared attribute name between yourself and Allah to justify a sense of self-importance? How will you now actively remind yourself that the name does not imply sharing the attribute?
?
In what specific part of your life—prayer, du'a, or daily speech—do you most easily slip into assuming that shared names mean shared realities? What concrete step will you take this week to correct that?

Two Proofs for Affirming 'Thing' for God
Maturidi presents scriptural and rational proofs for affirming 'thing' for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational proofs that affirm calling God 'thing' and refute the denial of His existence.

Core Idea
Affirming 'thing' for God is essential to avoid nullification and affirm His existence.

After clarifying the usage of 'thing', Maturidi now presents two proofs that God is rightly called 'thing'. The first is from scripture, citing two Quranic verses that would be meaningless if God were not a 'thing'. The second is rational, showing that 'thing' in common usage simply means affirmation, and denying it leads to negation (taʿṭīl). He also notes a practical precaution: some use the Persian term 'hastī' to avoid misunderstanding, but the core meaning is the same for scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs
Read the second part of the source, starting from 'Daleel ithbat al-qawl...'. Identify the scriptural proof (samʿ) and the rational proof (ʿaql).
2
Understand the scriptural proof
Maturidi cites Quran 42:11 and 6:19. Reflect on how each verse presupposes that God is a 'thing'.
If God were not a thing, negating likeness or asking 'what thing' would be meaningless.
3
Understand the rational proof
Notice that 'thingness' (al-shay'iyya) is the name of affirmation; saying 'no thing' is pure negation. Thus, calling God 'thing' affirms His existence and rejects nullification.
4
Note the Persian alternative
Maturidi mentions that some use 'hastī' to avoid possible confusion, but the meaning is identical for those who know Arabic.

The Four Meanings of 'One' (Wahid)
Maturidi classifies 'wahid' into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's fourfold classification of 'wahid' and how the fourth type uniquely applies to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness defies all ordinary categories of unity.

Maturidi responds to a question about the meaning of 'al-Wahid' (the One) by distinguishing four types. The first three apply to created things; the fourth is utterly transcendent and belongs only to Allah. This lesson unpacks that classification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the four categories
Find each of the four types of 'wahid' described. Note the characteristics of the first three and how they differ from the fourth.
The first three are ordinary categories; the fourth is transcendent.
3
Understand the fourth type
Reflect on why the fourth type is described with negatives and silence. Why does Maturidi say it is more hidden than 'it is'?
This is the key to tawhid.
4
Connect to Allah's oneness
Consider how this classification shows that Allah's oneness is not like any created unity. How does this protect against anthropomorphism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وسئل واحد عن معنى الواحد؛ قال : ينصرف على أربعة : كُل لا يحتمل التضعيف، و وجزء لا يحتمل التنصيف، والذي بينهما يحتمل الوجهين : كارتفاعه عما لا يتنصف وانحطاطه عما لا يتضعف؛ إذ لا شيء وراء الكل"، والرابع هو الذي قام به الثلاثة هو ولا هو هو أخفى من هو، والذى الخرس عنه اللسان، وانقطع دونه البيان، وانحسرت عنه الأوهام، وحارت فيه الأفهام، فذلك الله رب العالمين . ومن أحب أن يقول في الله بالجسم على التحقيق مما بينا من معانى الأجسام التي هي محل الأعراض المحتملة للنهايات ونحو ذلك يكلم في معانى خلق الأجسام المشاهدة، إن أمكن تثبيته من كل جهة من جهاته من حيث تلك الجهة فالقول به فى الله محال فاسد؛ لأنه وصف له بما قام دليل حدثه، وإن كان لا يتهيأ إيجابه فحقه التسمية، وإن ثبت قبل به، وإلا لا، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Someone asked about the meaning of 'the One'; he said: It applies to four kinds: a whole that does not admit of duplication, and a part that does not admit of division, and what is between them admits both aspects: like being above what is not divisible and below what is not duplicable; for there is nothing beyond the whole. And the fourth is that by which the three subsist – it is and it is not, it is more hidden than 'it is'; that before which the tongue falls silent, beyond which speech ceases, minds are bewildered, and intellects are confounded – that is Allah, Lord of the worlds. And whoever wishes to say about Allah 'body' in reality – according to what we have explained of the meanings of bodies which are loci of accidents, receptive to ends, and the like – he is speaking about the meanings of created observable bodies. If it is possible to affirm it from every aspect of its aspects from the perspective of that aspect, then saying it of Allah is impossible and corrupt, because it is describing Him by what the proof of temporality has been established. And if it is not possible to affirm it, then it is not right to name [Him with it]; and if it is affirmed, one accepts it, otherwise not. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct but related teachings. First, Maturidi answers a question about the meaning of 'the One' (al-wāḥid) by classifying it into four types. The first type is a whole that cannot be doubled (e.g., something undividable as a unity). The second is a part that cannot be divided further (a smallest unit). The third is something between these two, which can be both whole and part depending on perspective. The fourth type is unique: it is that by which the three other types subsist, yet it is neither fully existent nor non-existent in a comprehensible way; it defies language, intellect, and imagination. This fourth is Allah. Second, the passage refutes the claim that Allah can be called 'body' (jism) in a literal sense. The reasoning is that bodies are defined as loci of accidents, having ends and dimensions – all signs of temporality (ḥudūth). Describing Allah with such attributes implies He is originated. If one cannot verify such a description from every aspect, it is invalid to apply it. The argument closely aligns with the kalam proof of God's existence and attributes.

Key Terms
واحد
كل
جزء
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The oneness of Allah is like the numerical unity of created objects.
Correction: The fourth type of 'one' is beyond all categorization; Allah's oneness is unique and indescribable.

Checkpoint
According to the fourfold classification of 'wahid', which type corresponds to Allah?
A) The whole that does not admit duplication
B) The part that does not admit division
C) That which is between whole and part
D) The fourth, which defies description

Key Takeaway
Allah's oneness is of a unique, transcendent category beyond ordinary conceptions of unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Ahad' in your prayers, do you ever pause to consider that your mind cannot truly grasp what 'Ahad' means? How would your khushu' (focus) change if you remembered that your intellect falls short before His oneness? What will you do today to remind yourself of this limitation?
?
The passage describes the fourth meaning as 'that before which the tongue falls silent'. When was the last time you felt speechless in awe of Allah? If never, what is holding you back from contemplating His greatness to the point of silence? What can you change in your daily routine to create space for such awe?
?
You often use the word 'one' to describe Allah, but this lesson shows that His oneness is unlike any unity you know. Are you comfortable with not fully understanding your Lord? Does that discomfort drive you to seek more knowledge or to accept your limitation with humility? How will you practice surrendering your intellect to the truth that He is beyond it?

Refuting 'Body' (Jism) for Allah
Maturidi refutes applying 'jism' to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that describing Allah as a 'body' is impossible because bodies imply temporality.

Core Idea
Allah is not a body; bodies are temporal, Allah is eternal.

After affirming Allah's unique oneness, Maturidi addresses those who might describe Allah as a 'body'. He argues that the concept of body carries with it signs of temporality and contingency, making it incompatible with the eternal and necessary existence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the definition of 'body' given.
Maturidi defines bodies as loci of accidents.
2
Understand the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: if a body can be affirmed from every aspect, then it is temporal; describing Allah as temporal is impossible.
The key is the implication of temporality.
3
Apply the criterion
The passage gives a condition: if affirmation from all aspects is impossible, then it is not a valid attribute. Either way, calling Allah 'body' fails.

World's Origination Proves a Willful Creator
Maturidi argues that world's origination from nothing proves a willful creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's kalam argument that the world's creation from nothing necessitates a creator with free will, not nature.

Core Idea
Creation from nothing can only come from a willful Creator, not blind nature.

Maturidi presents a distinctive kalam argument for God's existence. He argues that the very fact the world came into being from nothing shows it must be the act of a being with perfect choice, because nature acts by compulsion. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step, drawing on both rational analysis and universal human practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms: hadath, ikhtiyar, tab', idtirar.
The passage uses concise, argumentative style.
2
Identify the key contrast
Maturidi contrasts creation by choice (ikhtiyar) with creation by nature (tab'). Understand why he thinks nature implies compulsion and deficiency.
3
Examine the argument structure
The argument has two main premises: (1) The world originated from nothing, (2) Only a willful being can originate from nothing. From this, it follows that the creator is willful, not natural.
Follow how he eliminates nature as a possibility.
4
Reflect on human practice
Maturidi uses the universal practice of supplication as evidence that people instinctively turn to a willful God, not a compelled force. Consider why this is a powerful proof.
This shows the argument is not purely abstract but grounded in human experience.
5
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the world is by God's choice. Summarize the logical flow in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنا قد بيَّنا حَدَثَ العالم لا من شيء شيء، وذلك نوع ما لا يبلغه إلا فِعل من هو في غاية معنى الاختيار، وما يكون بالطبع فحقه الاضطرار، ومحال أن يكون من يبلغ شأنه إلى إنشاء الأشياء لا من شيء ثم يكون ذلك بالطبع. ما كان وقوع الشيء بالطبع هو تحت قهر آخر، وجعله بحيث يسقط عنه الإمكان، وذلك آية الحدث وأمارة الضعف، جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى. ما جرى التعارف المتوارث من الخلق بالدعوات والتضرع إلى الله تعالى بالفرج، وأنه قهر كذا ونصر كذا، وأعان فلاناً وخذل فلاناً، وأن كل ذي قوة يفعل بقوة أنشأها، ولا ينال شيء من ذلك بالمضطر، ولا يرغب فيه؛ دلَّ ذلك على أن العالم باختياره.
And also, we have already explained the origination of the world from nothing, which is a type that only the act of one who is at the utmost degree of choice can achieve. And what occurs by nature is necessarily compelled, and it is impossible that one whose status reaches to the origination of things from nothing would then do that by nature. The occurrence of a thing by nature is under the compulsion of another, and it renders it such that possibility falls away from it, and that is a sign of origination and a mark of weakness—exalted is our Lord above that. And what has been the inherited custom among people of supplications and earnest prayers to God Almighty for relief, and that He overcomes such-and-such and grants victory to such-and-such, and helps so-and-so and abandons so-and-so, and that every possessor of power acts by a power that He has created, and nothing of that is attained by the compelled, nor is it desired—all that indicates that the world is by His choice.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam proof for the existence of a willful creator. Maturidi begins by restating a prior demonstration: the world originated from nothing (ḥadath al-ʿālam lā min shayʾ). Such an origination can only be the act of one who possesses perfect choice (ikhtiyār), because if it occurred by nature (ṭabʿ), it would be compelled (muḍṭarr) and involuntary. He then argues that a natural process is always subject to external compulsion, which implies deficiency and contingency—attributes incompatible with God. He further draws on the universally observed practice of humans turning to God in supplication for relief and victory, acknowledging that help comes only from a powerful agent, not from a compelled one. This demonstrates that the world is created by God's free choice, not by necessity.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
لا من شيء
الاختيار
الطبع
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from creation ex nihilo is purely philosophical and abstract, unrelated to human experience.
Correction: Maturidi grounds the argument in the universal human practice of supplication, showing it is both rational and experiential.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the world's origination from nothing cannot be by nature?
A) Because nature produces things imperfectly

C) Because nature cannot create from nothing

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof shows that creation from nothing, together with the universal practice of supplication, necessitates a willful Creator, not blind nature.

Choice Necessitates Power and Will
Maturidi establishes that choice necessitates power and will, using the perfection of creation as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of choice (ikhtiyār) in God necessarily implies that He possesses power (qudra) and will (irāda), and how this is derived from the perfection of creation.

Core Idea
Choice without power is impossible; perfect acts prove both power and will.

This passage from Imām al-Māturīdī begins by establishing a foundational principle: if choice is affirmed for God, then power and will must also be affirmed. He argues that a being lacking power produces disordered outcomes and cannot control opposites. The perfection of creation—in the witnessed realm—serves as evidence for the unseen Creator's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Maturidi connects choice to power and will.
Pay attention to the terms 'قدرة' and 'إرادة'.
2
Understand the argument from opposites
Maturidi argues that one without power cannot control a thing and its opposite. Why does this matter?
This shows that God must have both power and will to create opposites.
3
Connect the witnessed to the unseen
The perfect act in the witnessed world (shāhid) is a sign for the unseen (ghā'ib). Reflect on how this analogy works.
This is a key principle in kalām.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'nor indeed might but through God.' Consider why Maturidi adds this phrase.
It is a reminder of human limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا ثبت الاختيار ثبتت له القدرة على الخلق، والإرادة لكونه على ما هو عليه؛ لأن من لا قدرة له يخرج الذي يكون منه مضطرباً فاسداً، ولا يملك الشيء وضده، فثبت أن ما كان منه بقدرة كان واختيار، وذلك أمارات الفعل المتقن الحقيقية في الشاهد الذي هو أصل للعلم بالغائب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ما ذكرنا من تواصل الفعل - أعني الواقع به بالفعل - وتتابعه محكماً هو الدليل أنه كان فعله على العلم به، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً أنه إذ خرج كل الجواهر التي لا يمتحن في مصالح الممتحنين، وخلق كل شيء أريد به البقاء، مع خلق ما به بقاؤه، علم أنه كان بمن يعلم كيفية كل شيء وحاجته وما به القوام والمعاش، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً أن الله سبحانه خلق الخلق خلقاً دلَّ على حدثه، وعلى أن له محدثاً، وعلى وحدانية محدثه، فلولا أن علم بالخلق - يعلم أنه إذا خلق على ما خلق كان فيه دليل العلم به وبخلقه - لا يحتمل أن يخرج على ذلك خلقه، وبالله التوفيق.
So when choice is established, power over creation is established for Him, and will for His being as He is; because one who has no power produces that which comes from him as disordered and corrupt, and he does not possess a thing and its opposite. So it is established that what comes from Him by power is by choice, and that is the mark of a perfect act in the witnessed realm, which is the basis for knowledge of the unseen. And based on what we mentioned of the continuity of the act—I mean the one actually occurring—and its sequential occurrence as well-ordered is the proof that His act was based on knowledge of it. And also, when He brought forth all the substances that are not tested in the interests of the tested ones, and created everything intended to endure, together with creating that by which it endures, it is known that it was by one who knows the quality of everything and its need and that by which subsistence and livelihood are sustained. And also, that God Almighty created creation as a creation that indicates its own origination, and that it has an originator, and the oneness of its originator. Were it not that He knew about creation—that He knows that when He creates in the manner He created, there is in it evidence of knowledge of Him and of His creation—it would not be possible that His creation emerge in that manner.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage presents multiple proofs for God's attributes. First, Maturidi argues that choice (ikhtiyār) entails power (qudra) and will (irāda), because a being lacking power produces disordered and corrupt outcomes, such that it cannot control a thing and its opposite. The fact that creation is perfectly ordered (mutqan) in the witnessed world (shāhid) serves as evidence for the unseen (ghāʾib) Creator. Second, he points to the continuity and sequential perfection of acts as proof that they were done with knowledge (ʿilm). Third, he notes that the emergence of all substances (jawāhir)—which are not tested for the benefit of those tested—and the creation of everything intended to endure, along with the means of its endurance, indicate a Creator who knows every thing's quality and need. Fourth, he states that creation itself points to its own origination, its creator, and the creator's oneness; if God did not know that such creation would be evidence, He would not have created it that way. All this establishes that God has power, will, and knowledge.

Key Terms
الاختيار
القدرة
الإرادة
الفعل المتقن
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will is separate from His power, or that power alone suffices for action.
Correction: Maturidi shows that true perfect action requires both power and will; otherwise the act would be disordered.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the existence of choice in God necessarily imply?
A) Only power
B) Only will
C) Both power and will
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Choice (ikhtiyār) in God necessitates both power (qudra) and will (irāda), as evidenced by the perfect order of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life do you see disorder and corruption that might stem from relying on your own power instead of submitting to God's will? What one area will you consciously surrender to His power and choice today?
?
This lesson teaches that perfect acts come only from one with power and will. When you look at the perfect order of creation, does it stir genuine awe and gratitude in your heart, or have you become numb to it? What will you do this week to reawaken that awe?
?
The passage says one without power produces disordered outcomes. Think of a relationship or responsibility that is chaotic. Are you trying to manage it by your own strength? What would it look like to invite God's power and will into that situation right now?

Creation's Order Proves God's Knowledge
Maturidi provides three arguments from creation to prove God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three arguments from the order of creation that prove God possesses knowledge (ʿilm): the continuity of perfect acts, the creation of substances and their sustenance, and creation's inherent evidential structure.

Core Idea
The ordered nature of creation demands a Creator with perfect knowledge.

In this continuation, Imām al-Māturīdī presents multiple proofs for God's attribute of knowledge. He first points to the continuity and sequential perfecting of acts, then to the creation of all substances and their means of enduring, and finally to the fact that creation itself is structured to point to its Creator. Each argument underscores that only a knowing Creator could produce such a cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic and translation. Notice the repeated phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' as a refrain.
This passage has three distinct proofs.
2
Identify the first proof: continuity
The continuity and well-ordered sequence of acts proves knowledge. Why would disorder imply lack of knowledge?
This is a teleological argument.
3
Identify the second proof: substances and sustenance
God created substances that are not subject to testing, and everything intended to endure along with its means of survival. This requires knowledge of each thing's nature and needs.
Think about the intricacy of creation.
4
Identify the third proof: creation as evidence
Creation itself indicates its own origination, its originator, and oneness. If God did not know that it would serve as evidence, He would not have created it that way.
This is a meta-argument about the purpose of creation.
5
Synthesize
How do these three proofs together establish knowledge? What is the common thread?
They all point to design and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا ثبت الاختيار ثبتت له القدرة على الخلق، والإرادة لكونه على ما هو عليه؛ لأن من لا قدرة له يخرج الذي يكون منه مضطرباً فاسداً، ولا يملك الشيء وضده، فثبت أن ما كان منه بقدرة كان واختيار، وذلك أمارات الفعل المتقن الحقيقية في الشاهد الذي هو أصل للعلم بالغائب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى ما ذكرنا من تواصل الفعل - أعني الواقع به بالفعل - وتتابعه محكماً هو الدليل أنه كان فعله على العلم به، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً أنه إذ خرج كل الجواهر التي لا يمتحن في مصالح الممتحنين، وخلق كل شيء أريد به البقاء، مع خلق ما به بقاؤه، علم أنه كان بمن يعلم كيفية كل شيء وحاجته وما به القوام والمعاش، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً أن الله سبحانه خلق الخلق خلقاً دلَّ على حدثه، وعلى أن له محدثاً، وعلى وحدانية محدثه، فلولا أن علم بالخلق - يعلم أنه إذا خلق على ما خلق كان فيه دليل العلم به وبخلقه - لا يحتمل أن يخرج على ذلك خلقه، وبالله التوفيق.
So when choice is established, power over creation is established for Him, and will for His being as He is; because one who has no power produces that which comes from him as disordered and corrupt, and he does not possess a thing and its opposite. So it is established that what comes from Him by power is by choice, and that is the mark of a perfect act in the witnessed realm, which is the basis for knowledge of the unseen. And based on what we mentioned of the continuity of the act—I mean the one actually occurring—and its sequential occurrence as well-ordered is the proof that His act was based on knowledge of it. And also, when He brought forth all the substances that are not tested in the interests of the tested ones, and created everything intended to endure, together with creating that by which it endures, it is known that it was by one who knows the quality of everything and its need and that by which subsistence and livelihood are sustained. And also, that God Almighty created creation as a creation that indicates its own origination, and that it has an originator, and the oneness of its originator. Were it not that He knew about creation—that He knows that when He creates in the manner He created, there is in it evidence of knowledge of Him and of His creation—it would not be possible that His creation emerge in that manner.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage presents multiple proofs for God's attributes. First, Maturidi argues that choice (ikhtiyār) entails power (qudra) and will (irāda), because a being lacking power produces disordered and corrupt outcomes, such that it cannot control a thing and its opposite. The fact that creation is perfectly ordered (mutqan) in the witnessed world (shāhid) serves as evidence for the unseen (ghāʾib) Creator. Second, he points to the continuity and sequential perfection of acts as proof that they were done with knowledge (ʿilm). Third, he notes that the emergence of all substances (jawāhir)—which are not tested for the benefit of those tested—and the creation of everything intended to endure, along with the means of its endurance, indicate a Creator who knows every thing's quality and need. Fourth, he states that creation itself points to its own origination, its creator, and the creator's oneness; if God did not know that such creation would be evidence, He would not have created it that way. All this establishes that God has power, will, and knowledge.

العلم
تواصل الفعل
الجواهر
الممتحنين
القوام
المعاش
حدثه
وحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may argue that random processes can produce order, so knowledge is unnecessary.
Correction: Maturidi argues that order, especially the provision of sustenance, requires specific knowledge of needs and causes.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the continuity and well-ordered sequence of acts prove?
A) God's power
B) God's will
C) God's knowledge
D) God's oneness

Key Takeaway
The orderly and purposeful nature of creation—from the sequence of events to the intricate provision for all beings—demonstrates that the Creator possesses perfect knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the order in your daily life—the precise sequence of events that led to your current situation—do you truly see it as evidence of God's detailed knowledge, or do you take it for granted? How would your awareness change if you constantly reminded yourself that every provision and every trial is known by Him in its minutest detail? What step will you take today to act on this belief?
?
Consider the countless things that sustain you—food, water, air—that have no will, no trial of their own, yet they exist perfectly suited to your needs. If God knows so specifically what each creature requires, how could you ever doubt that He knows what you truly need, even when your own desires are confused? In what area of your life are you failing to trust His knowledge and instead relying solely on your own planning?
?
Your very existence as a human being is a sign pointing back to its Originator, as the passage states. But are you living in a way that reflects this sign? When people observe your life, do they see evidence that you are created by a knowing Creator, or do they see someone who acts as if he is self-sufficient? What specific habit or attitude would you need to abandon today to make your life more transparent to the One who knows all?
