The Scriptural Proof for God's Speech
Maturidi presents the scriptural evidence for God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to establish that God is described with real speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is proven by revelation itself.

Maturidi begins by stating that God's attribute of speech is established by both revelation and reason. He first turns to the Quran, citing verses that affirm God's speech directly and showing that even disbelievers acknowledged it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the verses Maturidi quotes. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to how he uses the verbal noun 'takliman'.
2
Identify the scriptural arguments
List the three verses or references Maturidi uses: the verse about Moses, the disbelievers' question, and the party hearing God's speech.
Each verse serves a different purpose.
3
Understand the consensus point
Note that Maturidi says there is agreement that God has speech in reality, even if opinions differ on its nature. This shows the foundational nature of this attribute.
This sets up the rational argument to come.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : الأصل أن الله عز وجل قد ثبت وصفه بالكلام بحجة السمع والعقل، فالسمع قوله : وَكَلَّمَ اللهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا، ذكره بالمصدر مع غير تمانع بين الخلق بكلام الله، وقد وجد الاتفاق على أنه متكلم وأن له كلاما في الحقيقة وإن اختلفت الآراء في مائيته : ولا أنكر على الذين قالوا : لَوْلا يُكَلَّمُنَا الله ، إلا بوصف التكبر والجهل بمنزلة أنفسهم ، وكذلك قوله : وَقَدْ كَانَ فريقٌ مِنْهُم يَسْمَعُونَ كَلَامَ اللهِ.
وأما العقل : إن كل عالم قادر لا يتكلم فعن آفة ، يكون من عجز أو والله عنه متعال ، ثبت أنه متكلم . على أن الذى لا يتكلم في الشاهد إنما لا يتكلم بالم الذي لا يسمع ولا يبصر من الآفة ، والله منزه عن المعنى الذى يقتضى الصمم والعمى ، وكذلك البكم ، وهو أولى ؛ إذ هو أجل ما يحمد به في الشاهد و به ينفصل البشر من سائر الحيوان ، مع ما كان كل محتمل الكلام. فعن عجز لا يتكلم أو عن سكوت .
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The foundation is that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is established as being described with speech by the evidence of transmission (sam') and reason ('aql). As for transmission, His saying: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' — He mentioned it with the verbal noun despite no contestation among creation about the Speech of Allah. And agreement has been found that He is speaking (mutakallim) and that He has speech in reality, even if opinions differ about its quiddity (māhiyya). And He did not denounce those who said: 'Why does not Allah speak to us?' except by describing them as arrogant and ignorant of their own station. Similarly, His saying: 'And indeed a party of them used to hear the Word of Allah.' 
As for reason: Every knowledgeable, powerful being who does not speak does so due to a defect, whether from incapacity or [something else]. Allah is exalted above that. So it is established that He is speaking. Moreover, that which does not speak in the witnessed world only does not speak due to the meaning that it does not hear or see — from defect — and Allah is transcendent beyond the meaning that would necessitate deafness and blindness, and likewise muteness. And He is more deserving [of speech]; for speech is the noblest thing by which one is praised in the witnessed world, and by it humans are distinguished from other animals, along with the fact that everything capable of speech [does so except due to incapacity or silence?]
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational proof that God is described with the attribute of speech (kalam). Maturidi begins by stating the principle: it is established by both revelation and reason. First, he gives the scriptural evidence: the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' (4:164) uses the verbal noun 'taklim' strongly affirming speech, and there is no disagreement among creation about God's speech — all agree He has real speech, though they differ about its nature. He also notes that when the disbelievers said 'Why does not Allah speak to us?' (2:118), Allah rebuked them not for asking for speech but for their arrogance and ignorance. Another verse (2:75) mentions that a party heard the Word of Allah, proving that God's speech can be heard. Second, the rational proof: A being that has knowledge and power but does not speak must have a defect, like incapacity or something else. Since Allah is free from defect, He must speak. In the witnessed world, whoever does not speak is either deaf/mute or incapacitated. Allah is transcendent above such defects, so He is necessarily speaking. Moreover, speech is the noblest praise-worthy attribute in humans, distinguishing us from animals, and God is more deserving of that perfection.

Key Terms
كلام
متكلم
السمع
مائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's speech is metaphorical or created.
Correction: Maturidi asserts that God has speech in reality (fi al-haqiqa), though its exact nature (māhiyya) is unknown.

Checkpoint
Which verse does Maturidi use to argue that God's speech can be heard?
A) 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly'
B) 'Why does not Allah speak to us?'
C) 'And indeed a party of them used to hear the Word of Allah'
D) 'Allah is the Creator of all things'

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses Quranic verses to establish that God has real speech, and that speech can be heard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah were to speak to you directly as He spoke to Moses, would you be prepared to listen, or would your heart be hard? Examine your daily habit of reading the Quran: do you treat it as the living speech of the One who spoke to Moses, or as just another book? What would change if you truly believed that every word you recite is His real speech?
?
The disbelievers were rebuked not for wanting to hear from Allah, but for their arrogance. When you pray, do you come with humility, truly expecting Allah to respond, or have you become complacent, treating prayer as a routine? Ask yourself: am I like those who demand signs but lack sincerity? How can you make your next prayer a genuine plea for divine conversation?
?
Maturidi proves that Allah's speech is real and can be heard. If you truly internalized that the Quran is Allah's actual speech, how would it change the way you read, memorize, or teach it? Would you still rush through your recitation, or would you pause, reflect, and let it transform your heart? Commit to one concrete change in your relationship with the Quran starting today.

The Rational Proof for God's Speech
Maturidi gives a rational argument for God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that God must possess speech because silence would imply a defect.

Core Idea
God speaks because He is perfect; silence is a defect.

After presenting the scriptural evidence, Maturidi provides a rational proof. He argues that any knowledgeable and powerful being who does not speak must have a defect. Since Allah is free from defects, He must be speaking.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the logical structure: premise, analogy, conclusion.
Look for the phrase 'إن كل عالم قادر لا يتكلم فعن آفة'.
2
Understand the analogy from the witnessed world
Maturidi uses the example of beings who cannot speak due to deafness, blindness, or muteness to show that silence is linked to defect.
God is free from such defects.
3
Grasp the perfection of speech
Speech is the noblest quality in humans. Therefore, Allah, who is most perfect, must possess it.
This reinforces the rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Two Proofs: No Self-Creation and Parents' Inability
Maturidi argues against self-creation and parents as creators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nothing can create itself and why parents cannot be true creators, pointing to a higher Creator.

Core Idea
Nothing creates itself, and parents are powerless—pointing to Allah alone.

Maturidi uses logical reasoning and human experience to demonstrate that every contingent thing depends on an external cause. The first argument shows the contradiction in self-creation, while the second observes the limitations of parents in bringing forth children. Both lead to the conclusion that there is a Creator beyond creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct arguments presented.
The passage begins with a logical proof and then shifts to an empirical observation.
2
Analyze the self-creation argument
Maturidi argues that if something could bring itself into existence, it could also annihilate itself. Why is that contradictory? Reflect on the idea of a thing having power over its own existence and non-existence simultaneously.
This is a classic contradiction: a thing cannot both cause and be subject to its own annihilation.
3
Examine the parents' example
Parents cannot fully control or plan their children's beginnings. They often hope for outcomes that do not materialize, and they cannot fix things if they go wrong. What does this reveal about human agency?
It shows that human power is limited and that a greater Power must be responsible.
4
Combine the arguments
How do these two arguments together point to the existence of Allah? The first establishes that nothing can be its own cause; the second shows that humans are not the ultimate cause. Therefore, the first cause is the Creator.
Together they form a cumulative case for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لو جاز أن يوجد نفسه لجاز في ذلك الوقت أن يعدمه، وذلك متناقض، فثبت الوجود بغيره، وبالله التوفيق. وأيضا أن جميع ما يذكر إنما هو نوع الموات إلا من ذكر من الآباء والأمهات مما يعلمون أن تدبيرهم لا يبلغ مبادى الأولاد، وأنهم يكونون على غير ما يأملون، وأنهم لو فسدوا لا يمكن إصلاحهم، وأن وسعهم لا يبلغ ما استتر من الأشياء فضلا من تقديرها هنالك، وغير ذلك من الوجوه التي يبطل كون ذلك بهم.
For if it were permissible for it to bring itself into existence, it would be permissible at that time to annihilate it, and that is contradictory. So it is established that existence is from another, and with Allah is success. And also, all that is mentioned is but a kind of inanimate thing except those mentioned from among the fathers and mothers, who know that their management does not reach the beginnings of children, and that they are not as they hope, and that if they were corrupted, it would not be possible to reform them, and that their capacity does not reach what is hidden of things, let alone their estimation there, and other aspects that nullify that this could be from them.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage presents a twofold argument for the existence of a Creator. First, Maturidi argues that nothing can bring itself into existence, because if it could, it would also be able to annihilate itself, which is a logical contradiction—a thing cannot have power over its own existence and nonexistence simultaneously. Therefore, every contingent thing’s existence must be from another. Second, he directs attention to the case of parents and children: parents clearly cannot fully control or even know the intricacies of their children’s development, and they often fail to achieve their plans for them. If humans were the sole agents, they would have complete power over the process, but they do not. This demonstrates that there is a higher, all-knowing Creator who arranges matters beyond human capacity.

Misconception: Some think that parents are the true creators of their children.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this by showing parents' profound ignorance and lack of control over their children's development.

B) Parents can fully manage their children's development.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The passage says parents cannot even know the hidden things about their children. Are there areas of your life where you still trust your own knowledge and power more than Allah's? What specific step can you take today to relinquish that control?

Proof from Limits and Decline
Maturidi argues from limits and decline to a wise, powerful creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observation of fixed limits and inevitable decline to prove that the world depends on an all-knowing, all-powerful Creator.

Core Idea
Limits and decline show the world is not self-sufficient but directed by a wise Creator.

Maturidi presents a profound argument from everyday observation: every created thing has a fixed limit, and once reached, it cannot exceed it. Despite continuous nourishment and care, everything eventually declines. This cannot be explained by the thing itself or by natural forces alone, because if it were self-sufficient, it would either remain constant or improve. Maturidi argues this points to an external, all-knowing, and all-powerful being who ordains such limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage up to 'جل ثناؤه'. Understand the observation and conclusion.
The passage uses concise language; take time to parse each phrase.
2
Identify the key observation
Notice that Maturidi says everything has a limit and then declines despite nourishment and upbringing. What does this imply about self-sufficiency?
3
Understand the conclusion
He deduces that this indicates a Creator who knows all and is powerful, because natural causes cannot explain this pattern.
The phrase 'by One who knows by Himself' shows the agent must be self-knowing.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument is not based on revelation but on reason and common experience. It establishes a rational foundation for tawhid.
Connects to the broader aim of Ilm al-Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن كل شيء له حده إذا بلغ ذلك الحد لا يزداد له طول ولا عقل، بل يأخذ كل شيء من ذلك بالانتقاص على قيام الأغذية ودوام التربية، علم أن ذلك كذلك لا بما ذكر، ولكن بمن هو عالم بذاته حتى لا يعرب عنه شيء، قادر بنفسه فلا يعجزه شيء جل ثناؤه. وأيضا أنه ما من شيء مما ذكر من أنواع الجواهر إلا وقد يحتمل الإفساد والإصلاح جميعا، وذلك أيضا كله متضاد متدافع لا يحتمل الاجتماع للتعاون، ثبت أنها كانت على ما عليه بغيرها؛ إذ كل شيء على جهة بنفسه لا يحتمل التغير ما دامت نفسه، وبالله العصمة.
And after that, every thing has its limit; when it reaches that limit, it does not increase in length nor in intellect, but rather everything takes from that by decreasing despite the continuation of nourishment and the persistence of upbringing. It is known that it is thus not by what was mentioned, but by One who knows by Himself so that nothing escapes Him, powerful by Himself so that nothing incapacitates Him—exalted is His praise. And also, there is nothing among what was mentioned of the types of substances except that it may bear both corruption and reform together, and that too is all contradictory and repelling, not admitting combination for cooperation. It is established that they were as they are by other than themselves; since every thing in its own manner by itself does not admit change as long as its self exists, and with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage advances two further proofs. First, every created thing has a fixed limit (ḥadd)—once reached, it cannot exceed it in size or capacity, and despite continuous nourishment and care, it eventually declines. This cannot be explained by the thing itself or by natural processes, because if it were self-sufficient, it would either remain constant or improve, but it does not. Therefore, the cause must be a wise, all-knowing, and all-powerful Creator who ordains these limits. Second, every substance can undergo both corruption and reform, and these two states are fundamentally opposed and cannot cooperate. Since opposites do not naturally come together, the fact that things exist in one state rather than another is determined by an external agent. That agent must be self-subsisting and immutable, for what is self-subsisting does not change as long as it exists. The phrase 'with Allah is protection' concludes the argument, seeking divine safeguarding against error.

Key Terms
حده
انتقاص
قيام الأغذية
دوام التربية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think things naturally have limits and decline without any external cause; Maturidi argues this pattern itself proves a wise controller.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the consistency of limits and decline across all entities requires a knowing and powerful being, not just natural processes.

Checkpoint
What observation does Maturidi use to prove that things are not self-sufficient?
A) Things never change or decline.
B) Things have limits and eventually decline despite nourishment.
C) Things can create themselves.
D) Things are eternal and do not need a creator.

Key Takeaway
The existence of fixed limits and inevitable decline in creation proves it is not self-sufficient but depends on an all-knowing, all-powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen your own body grow, plateau, and now feel its decline. You continue to eat and exercise, yet you cannot stop aging. What does this undeniable limit teach you about your utter dependence on Allah? Will you let this truth humble you today, or will you continue to pretend you are in control?
?
Consider something you have been striving for—a goal, a relationship, a skill—that has reached its limit and is now declining despite your efforts. Why do you think it is declining? Could it be that Allah is reminding you that He alone is the one who gives and takes, and that your efforts are meaningless without His will? How will you realign your heart to accept His decree?
?
You have witnessed the decline of loved ones, even with the best care. Did that moment make you turn to Allah in recognition of His power, or did it make you resent the natural order? What does the proof from limits and decline demand from you right now in terms of your reliance on Allah?

Proof from Corruption and Reform
Maturidi argues from the coexistence of corruption and reform to an immutable external agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that since everything can be corrupted and reformed, and these are opposites that cannot cooperate, there must be an external agent determining their state.

Core Idea
Opposites like corruption and reform cannot coexist naturally, proving an external controller.

Maturidi extends his proof by noting that every substance can undergo both corruption and reform. These two states are fundamentally opposed and repelling, yet they can occur in the same thing at different times. Since opposites cannot cooperate to bring about change, the fact that things exist in one state rather than another must be determined by an external agent. Maturidi concludes that this agent is self-subsisting and immutable.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Start from 'وأيضا أنه ما من شيء' to the end. Understand the observation that everything can be corrupted and reformed.
2
Identify the key point
Maturidi notes that corruption (ifsād) and reform (iṣlāḥ) are opposites (mutḍādd) and repel each other (mutadāfi‘). They cannot come together to cooperate.
3
Understand the implication
Since they cannot cooperate, something external must decide when a thing is corrupted or reformed. This external agent is not subject to change.
The phrase 'by other than themselves' (bi-ghayrihā) indicates dependence.
4
Connect to previous argument
This reinforces the need for a wise, powerful Creator who controls all changes.
Together with the limit argument, it forms a complete rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن كل شيء له حده إذا بلغ ذلك الحد لا يزداد له طول ولا عقل، بل يأخذ كل شيء من ذلك بالانتقاص على قيام الأغذية ودوام التربية، علم أن ذلك كذلك لا بما ذكر، ولكن بمن هو عالم بذاته حتى لا يعرب عنه شيء، قادر بنفسه فلا يعجزه شيء جل ثناؤه. وأيضا أنه ما من شيء مما ذكر من أنواع الجواهر إلا وقد يحتمل الإفساد والإصلاح جميعا، وذلك أيضا كله متضاد متدافع لا يحتمل الاجتماع للتعاون، ثبت أنها كانت على ما عليه بغيرها؛ إذ كل شيء على جهة بنفسه لا يحتمل التغير ما دامت نفسه، وبالله العصمة.
And after that, every thing has its limit; when it reaches that limit, it does not increase in length nor in intellect, but rather everything takes from that by decreasing despite the continuation of nourishment and the persistence of upbringing. It is known that it is thus not by what was mentioned, but by One who knows by Himself so that nothing escapes Him, powerful by Himself so that nothing incapacitates Him—exalted is His praise. And also, there is nothing among what was mentioned of the types of substances except that it may bear both corruption and reform together, and that too is all contradictory and repelling, not admitting combination for cooperation. It is established that they were as they are by other than themselves; since every thing in its own manner by itself does not admit change as long as its self exists, and with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage advances two further proofs. First, every created thing has a fixed limit (ḥadd)—once reached, it cannot exceed it in size or capacity, and despite continuous nourishment and care, it eventually declines. This cannot be explained by the thing itself or by natural processes, because if it were self-sufficient, it would either remain constant or improve, but it does not. Therefore, the cause must be a wise, all-knowing, and all-powerful Creator who ordains these limits. Second, every substance can undergo both corruption and reform, and these two states are fundamentally opposed and cannot cooperate. That agent must be self-subsisting and immutable, for what is self-subsisting does not change as long as it exists. The phrase 'with Allah is protection' concludes the argument, seeking divine safeguarding against error.

Correction: Maturidi shows that since corruption and reform are opposites that cannot work together, change must come from outside the thing itself.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about corruption and reform?
A) They are the same thing.
B) They are opposites that can cooperate.
C) They are opposites that cannot work together.
D) They are impossible for substances.

Key Takeaway
The presence of opposing states like corruption and reform in the same thing shows an external, immutable agent controls change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the changes in your life—your moments of faith and doubt, strength and weakness—are you quick to attribute them to your own doing, or do you acknowledge that an external, immutable Agent is turning your heart? This passage forces you to ask: who or what do you credit for your spiritual states? Are you practicing reliance (tawakkul) or self-reliance?
?
Consider a specific area of your life where you see both corruption (sin, bad habits) and reform (repentance, improvement). According to Maturidi's argument, these opposites cannot come from your own unchanging self. So who is responsible for the change? How does that realization affect your sense of accountability and your hope for reform?
?
The passage ends with 'with Allah is protection' (وَبِٱللَّهِ ٱلْعِصْمَةُ). Are you truly convinced that only Allah can safeguard you from corruption and grant lasting reform? In what specific situation today are you relying on your own plan rather than seeking His protection and guidance?

Needs and Desires Prove an External Creator
Maturidi argues that needs and desires prove an external Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of needs and desires in living beings necessitates an external Creator who implanted them and provided their fulfillment.

Core Idea
Needs and desires cannot arise from a self-sufficient being; they prove an external Provider.

Maturidi begins his argument from the observable fact that all living beings have needs and desires that compel them to seek nourishment. He examines whether these needs could originate from the beings themselves and concludes they cannot, because a self-sufficient essence cannot be inherently needy. This points to an external Creator who created them with needs and also provides the means to satisfy them.

Guided Path
1
Observe the premise
Notice that all living beings are built upon needs and desires that overwhelm them. Without these, they would not need nourishment. This is an observable fact.
2
Consider the impossibility of inherent need
If a being had needs by its own essence, it would not be self-sufficient. But self-sufficiency (al-ghina) is a sign of standing by oneself (al-qiyam bi al-dhat). So needs cannot be intrinsic.
The key phrase: 'al-qiyam bi al-dhat daleel al-ghina'.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Since needs are not self-caused, there must be an external Creator who created beings with needs and also created the means to fulfill them. This establishes a directing Knower (al-mudir al-‘alim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن كل حي فما يعاين مبنى على الحاجات والشهوات التي تغلبهم وتقهرهم، ولولا ذلك ما احتاجوا إلى الأغذية، ثم كانت هي أسبابا للغنا، لم يحتمل أن يكون منها تهيج الشهوات وحدوث الحاجات، ولا يجوز أن تكون لأنفسها شهوات وحاجات؛ لأوجه: أحدها أن القيام بالذات دون الغير دليل الغنا، لا يجوز أن يصير حاجة، ولأن ما كان لذاته على جهة لا يحتمل زواله، وقد يقع لها الغنا بالغير، أو للإحالة أن يكون الشيء من حيث نفسه محوجا إلى غير، بل إذا أحوج إلى غير من حيث نفسه بدوم الحاجة ما بقيت نفسه، فثبت أن هنالك غير أنشأهم على الحاجات وركب فيهم الشهوات، ثم أنشأ لهم ما به الغنا وقضاء الشهوات، فيكون بما أريد به ثبت المدير العالم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما لا يوجد شيء من أعيان العالم وصفاتها إلا مسخرا مذللا بما لولا ذلك كان أهون عليه وألذ، نحو القرار الدائم والسير المتتابع مما كان به معاش أحد وما له المعاش، فصار العالم بكليته بالمعنى الذي ذكرت. ولا يجوز أن يكون المسخر المذلل بمالك التدبير حتى يكون به غنى الغير وقيامه، ولا يملك إزالة الذلة نفسه والسخرة، ثبت أن لكل ذلك مديرا عليها علم وجوه حاجاتهم وغناهم، فخلقهم على ذلك مع ما أحوج بعضنا إلى بعض في القيام والبقاء على جهل كل منهم بالوجه الذي أحوج إلى غيره، وعجزه عن صرف وجه الحاجة عن نفسه، ثبت أن لذلك كله مديرا عليها، على تدبيره جرى أمرهم. وبعد، لو خلى بين أعقل الخلق وأعظمهم تدبيرا وبين تقدير أحواله وأفعاله من الزمان والمكان فيما لطف منها لما احتمل وسعهم، فمن دونه أحق بذلك. صرف قهر الزمان عن نفسه ولا إحاطة المكان، ثبت أن لا يملك أحد تصريف العالم على ما هو عليه لا يجوز كونه به دون خارج من معناه في إحاطة الحاجة به بل بالقيام بذاته عالما قادرا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, every living being is seen to be built upon needs and desires that overwhelm and overpower them, and were it not for that, they would not need nourishment; then those [nourishments] were causes for sufficiency. It is not possible that from them [the nourishments] the incitement of desires and the occurrence of needs should arise, nor is it permissible that they have desires and needs by themselves, for reasons: one of them is that standing by oneself without another is evidence of sufficiency; it is not permissible that it become a need. And because what is by itself in a manner does not admit its disappearance, and sufficiency may occur to it through another, or due to the impossibility that a thing from itself would need another; rather, if it needs another from its own self, as long as the need persists, its self would not remain. So it is established that there is another who created them upon needs and installed desires in them, then created for them that through which sufficiency and fulfillment of desires come, so it is thereby intended to establish the directing Knower, and there is no power except with Allah. Along with the fact that there is nothing among the entities of the world and their attributes except that it is subjugated and humiliated—for if it were otherwise, it would be easier and more pleasurable for it—such as constant rest and continuous movement, through which one’s livelihood and sustenance are obtained. So the entire world is as I described. And it is not permissible that the subjugated and humiliated thing have the ownership of management such that through it the sufficiency of others and their existence occur, nor can it remove humiliation and subjugation from itself. It is established that all of that has a director who knows the aspects of their needs and sufficiency, so He created them upon that, along with making some of us need others in existence and survival, with each of them ignorant of the aspect by which he needs another, and unable to turn away the aspect of need from himself. It is established that all of that has a director over them; on His planning their affair runs. And after that, if the most intelligent of creation and the greatest in planning were entrusted with the estimation of his states and actions of time and place, in what is subtle of them, his capacity would not bear it. So those below him are more deserving of that. He cannot remove the domination of time from himself nor the encompassment of place. It is established that no one can dispose of the world as it is; it is not permissible for it to be by itself without an external [agent] in encompassing the need with it, but rather by One who stands by Himself, knowing, capable, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This extensive passage presents a cumulative argument based on three main observations. First, living beings are characterized by needs and desires that compel them to seek nourishment. These needs and desires cannot originate from the being itself, because a self-sufficient essence (qa'im bil-dhat) cannot be needy—if it were inherently needy, its need would never cease and its existence would be impossible. Hence, an external Creator must have implanted these needs and also provided the means to satisfy them. Second, everything in the world is subjugated (musakhkhar) and humiliated (mudhallal), meaning it serves a purpose beyond itself; if it were left to itself, it would prefer constant rest or continuous motion, but it is forced into a state that benefits others. This subjugation proves a Director who knows the needs of all things. Third, Maturidi points to the limitation of human planning: even the most intelligent person cannot fully control his own states of time and place, let alone manage the whole world. Therefore, the world cannot be run by any creature; it must be governed by a self-subsistent, all-knowing, and all-powerful Creator. Each stage leads to the conclusion that there is a wise Director (al-mudir al-‘alim) who manages everything.

Key Terms
الحاجات والشهوات
القيام بالذات
الغنا
المدير العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the world could be self-organizing or that needs are just natural phenomena without a creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that needs cannot arise from a self-sufficient essence, so they must be implanted by an external Creator.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that needs and desires cannot originate from the living being itself?
A) Because a self-sufficient essence cannot be needy.
B) Because needs are purely physical.
C) Because desires are illusions.
D) Because living beings are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Needs and desires in living beings cannot be self-caused; they prove an external directing Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a deep need for something—whether for food, love, or purpose—do you immediately recognize that this need is a sign pointing you to the One who created it and who alone can truly satisfy it? Or do you forget Him and rely on secondary causes? What would change in your daily life if you saw every need as a divine summons?
?
Your very existence is built upon needs and desires that you did not choose and cannot escape. How much gratitude do you show to the One who not only placed these needs in you but also provides for them every moment? Start right now: list three needs you took for granted today and thank Allah for them.
?
If needs and desires are not random but purposeful indicators of a directing Creator (al-mudir al-‘alim), then what does it say about the way you pursue your desires? Are you using them as leads to draw closer to Him, or are you chasing them as ends in themselves, ignoring the One who put them there? Commit to one change today in how you respond to a recurring desire.

5 min
Universal Subjugation Points to a Director
Maturidi argues that universal subjugation proves a wise Director.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal subjugation of all things in the world indicates a wise Director who knows their needs and purposes.

Core Idea
Everything in the world is subjugated and humiliated; this proves a Director who manages it all.

Maturidi extends his argument by observing that every entity and attribute in the world is subjugated (musakhkhar) and humiliated (mudhallal)—it serves a purpose beyond itself. Nothing can remove this subjugation from itself, and no creature has the power to manage the entire system. This points to a Director (mudir) who knows all needs and provides for them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of subjugation
Note that nothing in the world exists purely for itself; everything is subjugated to serve some other purpose. For example, the sun, moon, and elements are forced into patterns that benefit living beings.
2
See the impossibility of self-management
If a thing is subjugated, it cannot own the management of itself or of others. Therefore, the subjugation of all things cannot be self-caused but must be imposed by an external Director.
3
Realize the need for a knowing Director
The Director must know the needs of all things and how to supply them. This is because the subjugation is purposeful and orderly, not random.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second, everything in the world is subjugated (musakhkhar) and humiliated (mudhallal), meaning it serves a purpose beyond itself; if it were left to itself, it would prefer constant rest or continuous motion, but it is forced into a state that benefits others. This subjugation proves a Director who knows the needs of all things. Third, Maturidi points to the limitation of human planning: even the most intelligent person cannot fully control his own states of time and place, let alone manage the whole world. Therefore, the world cannot be run by any creature; it must be governed by a self-subsistent, all-knowing, and all-powerful Creator. Each stage leads to the conclusion that there is a wise Director (al-mudir al-‘alim) who manages everything.

Key Terms
مسخرا مذللا
المدير العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the world runs on its own through natural laws without a director.
Correction: Maturidi shows that universal subjugation implies an external Director, as nothing can manage itself.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that everything in the world is subjugated?
A) That the world runs by accident.
B) That there exists a Director who knows all needs.
C) That subjugation is an illusion.
D) That creatures can manage themselves.

Key Takeaway
Universal subjugation proves that a wise Director manages all creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sun rising each day, serving your life without complaint, do you see it as a subjugated servant or just a natural process? How can you train your heart today to recognize the Director behind its constant service?
?
You depend on countless things for your next breath, meal, or heartbeat—yet you often feel self-sufficient. How does Maturidi's argument expose that feeling as a delusion? Will you today thank the Director for each of those subjugated blessings?
?
If you cannot remove even a small need from yourself (like hunger, thirst, or fatigue), what does that say about your control over your own life? Are you willing to submit to the Director who does have full control?

Human Limitation Proves a Creator
Maturidi argues that human limitation proves a self-subsistent Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human inability to control even their own states of time and place points to a self-subsistent, all-knowing Creator who governs the world.

Core Idea
Even the most intelligent human cannot master their own time and place; this proves a greater Creator must govern all.

Maturidi concludes his argument by considering the limitation of human planning. Even the wisest person cannot fully estimate or control the subtle details of their own life – such as time and place – let alone manage the entire world. This shows that no creature can run the world; it must be governed by a self-subsistent (qa'im bi dhatihi), all-knowing, and all-powerful Creator.

Guided Path
1
Consider the limit of human planning
Reflect on how even the most intelligent and experienced person cannot perfectly plan all their actions and circumstances, especially the subtle aspects of time and place.
2
Recognize the implication
If a human cannot manage even their own limited sphere, then no human or creature can manage the entire world. The world's order must come from a being beyond creation.
3
Identify the necessary attributes
The one who manages the world must be self-subsistent (not needing anything), all-knowing, and all-powerful. Only such a being can oversee the immense complexity of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن كل حي فما يعاين مبنى على الحاجات والشهوات التي تغلبهم وتقهرهم، ولولا ذلك ما احتاجوا إلى الأغذية، ثم كانت هي أسبابا للغنا، لم يحتمل أن يكون منها تهيج الشهوات وحدوث الحاجات، ولا يجوز أن تكون لأنفسها شهوات وحاجات؛ لأوجه: أحدها أن القيام بالذات دون الغير دليل الغنا، لا يجوز أن يصير حاجة، ولأن ما كان لذاته على جهة لا يحتمل زواله، وقد يقع لها الغنا بالغير، أو للإحالة أن يكون الشيء من حيث نفسه محوجا إلى غير، بل إذا أحوج إلى غير من حيث نفسه بدوم الحاجة ما بقيت نفسه، فثبت أن هنالك غير أنشأهم على الحاجات وركب فيهم الشهوات، ثم أنشأ لهم ما به الغنا وقضاء الشهوات، فيكون بما أريد به ثبت المدير العالم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما لا يوجد شيء من أعيان العالم وصفاتها إلا مسخرا مذللا بما لولا ذلك كان أهون عليه وألذ، نحو القرار الدائم والسير المتتابع مما كان به معاش أحد وما له المعاش، فصار العالم بكليته بالمعنى الذي ذكرت. ولا يجوز أن يكون المسخر المذلل بمالك التدبير حتى يكون به غنى الغير وقيامه، ولا يملك إزالة الذلة نفسه والسخرة، ثبت أن لكل ذلك مديرا عليها علم وجوه حاجاتهم وغناهم، فخلقهم على ذلك مع ما أحوج بعضنا إلى بعض في القيام والبقاء على جهل كل منهم بالوجه الذي أحوج إلى غيره، وعجزه عن صرف وجه الحاجة عن نفسه، ثبت أن لذلك كله مديرا عليها، على تدبيره جرى أمرهم. وبعد، لو خلى بين أعقل الخلق وأعظمهم تدبيرا وبين تقدير أحواله وأفعاله من الزمان والمكان فيما لطف منها لما احتمل وسعهم، فمن دونه أحق بذلك. صرف قهر الزمان عن نفسه ولا إحاطة المكان، ثبت أن لا يملك أحد تصريف العالم على ما هو عليه لا يجوز كونه به دون خارج من معناه في إحاطة الحاجة به بل بالقيام بذاته عالما قادرا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, every living being is seen to be built upon needs and desires that overwhelm and overpower them, and were it not for that, they would not need nourishment; then those [nourishments] were causes for sufficiency. It is not possible that from them [the nourishments] the incitement of desires and the occurrence of needs should arise, nor is it permissible that they have desires and needs by themselves, for reasons: one of them is that standing by oneself without another is evidence of sufficiency; it is not permissible that it become a need. And because what is by itself in a manner does not admit its disappearance, and sufficiency may occur to it through another, or due to the impossibility that a thing from itself would need another; rather, if it needs another from its own self, as long as the need persists, its self would not remain. So it is established that there is another who created them upon needs and installed desires in them, then created for them that through which sufficiency and fulfillment of desires come, so it is thereby intended to establish the directing Knower, and there is no power except with Allah. Along with the fact that there is nothing among the entities of the world and their attributes except that it is subjugated and humiliated—for if it were otherwise, it would be easier and more pleasurable for it—such as constant rest and continuous movement, through which one’s livelihood and sustenance are obtained. So the entire world is as I described. And it is not permissible that the subjugated and humiliated thing have the ownership of management such that through it the sufficiency of others and their existence occur, nor can it remove humiliation and subjugation from itself. It is established that all of that has a director who knows the aspects of their needs and sufficiency, so He created them upon that, along with making some of us need others in existence and survival, with each of them ignorant of the aspect by which he needs another, and unable to turn away the aspect of need from himself. It is established that all of that has a director over them; on His planning their affair runs. And after that, if the most intelligent of creation and the greatest in planning were entrusted with the estimation of his states and actions of time and place, in what is subtle of them, his capacity would not bear it. So those below him are more deserving of that. He cannot remove the domination of time from himself nor the encompassment of place. It is established that no one can dispose of the world as it is; it is not permissible for it to be by itself without an external [agent] in encompassing the need with it, but rather by One who stands by Himself, knowing, capable, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This extensive passage presents a cumulative argument based on three main observations. First, living beings are characterized by needs and desires that compel them to seek nourishment. These needs and desires cannot originate from the being itself, because a self-sufficient essence (qa'im bil-dhat) cannot be needy—if it were inherently needy, its need would never cease and its existence would be impossible. Hence, an external Creator must have implanted these needs and also provided the means to satisfy them. Second, everything in the world is subjugated (musakhkhar) and humiliated (mudhallal), meaning it serves a purpose beyond itself; if it were left to itself, it would prefer constant rest or continuous motion, but it is forced into a state that benefits others. This subjugation proves a Director who knows the needs of all things. Third, Maturidi points to the limitation of human planning: even the most intelligent person cannot fully control his own states of time and place, let alone manage the whole world. Therefore, the world cannot be run by any creature; it must be governed by a self-subsistent, all-knowing, and all-powerful Creator. Each stage leads to the conclusion that there is a wise Director (al-mudir al-‘alim) who manages everything.

Key Terms
صرف قهر الزمان
القيام بالذات
عالما قادرا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that humans can eventually control everything through science and planning.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even the best human planning is limited, so the world cannot be run by humans; it requires a perfect Creator.

B) That no one can manage the world except a self-subsistent, knowing, capable Creator.
C) That the world runs by chance.
D) That humans should try harder.

Key Takeaway

The Proof from Human Needs and Appetites
Maturidi argues from human needs and appetites to a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the existence of human needs and appetites to prove a wise Creator who manages all affairs.

Core Idea
Needs and appetites are not self-caused; they point to a Director who knows and provides.

Maturidi presents a profound argument from the very fabric of human existence. He observes that we have needs and appetites that drive us, yet these cannot originate from the objects themselves. This leads him to reason that a knowing Director must have created us with these needs and also provided the means to fulfill them. The argument culminates in the recognition that everything in the world is subjugated for our benefit, further confirming the existence of a single Manager.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze every detail. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of Maturidi's reasoning settle in before you dive into the commentary.
Notice how Maturidi uses everyday human experience as a starting point.
2
Identify the main claim
Identify the central thesis: that needs and appetites cannot arise from the things themselves that supposedly satisfy them. Look for the reasons Maturidi gives to support this.
The reasons are introduced by 'لأوجه'.
3
Understand the three reasons
Maturidi gives three reasons why things cannot have needs by themselves. The first is about self-sufficiency, the second about immutability, the third about essential causation. Work through each one with the commentary.
These reasons are subtle; take your time.
4
Follow the extension to all of creation
Maturidi then extends the argument: every entity in the world is subjugated and humbled, meaning it cannot be the ultimate manager. This points to a single director over all.
Consider the examples of constant rest and uninterrupted movement.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final conclusion is that there is a director who knows the aspects of needs and sufficiencies, and upon whose management all affairs proceed. This is the affirmation of the Creator.
How does the interdependence of humans further support this?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولولا ذلك ما احتاجوا إلى الأغذية ، ثم كانت هي أسبابا - للغنا ، لم يحتمل أن يكون منها تهيج الشهوات وحدوث الحاجات ، ولا يجوز أن تكون لأنفسها شهوات وحاجات؛ لأوجه : أحدها أن القيام بالذات دون الغير دليل الغنا ، لا يجوز أن يصير حاجة ، ولأن ما كان لذاته على جهة لا يحتمل زواله ، وقد يقع لها الغنا بالغير، أو للإحالة أن يكون الشيء من حيث نفسه محوجا إلى غير ، بل إذا أحوج إلى غير من حيث نفسه بدوم الحاجة ما بقيت نفسه ، فثبت أن هنالك غير أنشأهم على الحاجات وركب فيهم الشهوات ، الغنا / وقضاء الشهوات ، فيكون بما أريد به نفى المدير العالم ثم أنشأ لهم ما به الغنا تثبيته، ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما لا يوجد شيء من أعيان العالم وصفاتها إلا مسخرا مذللا بما لولا ذلك كان أهون عليه وألذ ، نحو القرار الدائم والسير المتتابع مما كان به معاش أحد وما له المعاش ، فصار العالم بكليته بالمعنى الذي ذكرت . ولا يجوز أن يكون المسخر المذلل بمالك التدبير حتى يكون به غنى الغير وقيامه ، ولا يملك إزالة الذله نفسه والسخرة ، ثبت أن لكل ذلك مديرا عليها علم وجوه حاجاتهم وغناهم ، فخلقهم على ذلك مع ما أحوج بعضنا إلى بعض فى القيام والبقاء على جهل كل منهم بالوجه الذى أحوج إلى غيره، وعجزه عن صرف وجه الحاجة عن نفسه ، ثبت أن لذلك كله مديرا عليها ، على تدبيره جرى أمرهم
And were it not for that, they would not have needed nourishment; then they (the nourishment) were causes for sufficiency, yet it is not plausible that from them arise the incitement of appetites and the occurrence of needs. And it is not permissible that they (the things themselves) have appetites and needs by themselves, for several reasons: one is that subsistence by self without another is evidence of self-sufficiency; it is not permissible for it to become a need. And because what is by its essence in a certain state does not admit of its removal; and self-sufficiency may occur to it through another; or because it is impossible for a thing to be, from its own essence, need-inducing of another. Rather, if it makes another needy from its own essence, as long as the need continues, its own essence would not remain. So it is established that there is another who created them upon needs and embedded in them appetites, self-sufficiency and fulfilling appetites, so that by what is intended thereby, the knowing director is affirmed. Then He created for them that by which self-sufficiency is confirmed – and there is no power but in God. And with that, there is no entity among the essences of the world and their attributes except that it is subjugated and humbled by that which, were it not so, would have been easier and more pleasant for it, such as constant rest and uninterrupted movement, which were the livelihood of one and his means of living. Thus the whole world became in the sense I mentioned. And it is not permissible that the subjugated and humbled thing should possess the management (tadbir) so that through it another's sufficiency and subsistence occur; nor can it remove its own abasement and subjugation. So it is established that for all that there is a director who knows the aspects of their needs and sufficiencies. So He created them upon that, along with making some of us need others for subsistence and survival, while each of them is ignorant of the aspect by which he is made needful of another, and unable to turn away the aspect of need from himself. So it is established that for all that there is a director upon whose management (tadbir) their affairs proceed.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage is a proof from human needs and appetites for the existence of a wise Creator. Maturidi begins by stating that if not for need, humans would not require nourishment. However, the very things that cause sufficiency also incite appetites and create needs. He then argues that it is impossible for these things themselves to possess appetites and needs, giving three reasons: (1) A self-subsistent thing is inherently self-sufficient and cannot become needy. (2) What exists by its essence in a fixed state cannot lose that state; if it gains self-sufficiency from another, that shows its contingency. (3) It is impossible for a thing, from its own essence, to make another needy, because if it did, its own essence would not persist as long as the need continues. Therefore, there must be an external agent who created humans with needs and appetites. This agent also created means for sufficiency. Maturidi then notes that everything in the world is subjugated and humbled for human benefit, yet these subjugated things cannot themselves be the managers, because they cannot remove their own subjugation. Thus, the world requires a director who knows all needs and sufficiencies. This director also made humans interdependent while keeping them ignorant of the exact aspects of their needs, further proving that management is from another. The conclusion is that all affairs run according to His management.

Key Terms
الحاجة
الشهوة
الغنى
المدير
التسخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi is denying that food causes satiety or that natural processes are self-sufficient.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny causation; he argues that the very causal relationships (needs arising from food, etc.) cannot be explained by the objects themselves, but require a transcendent designer who set up these relationships.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one reason why things cannot have needs and appetites by themselves?
A) Because they are eternal and unchanging.
B) Because self-subsistent things are self-sufficient and cannot become needy.
C) Because they are created by God.
D) Because needs only arise in living beings.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves the existence of a wise Creator by showing that human needs and appetites cannot be self-caused and that all of creation is subjugated, requiring a single Director who knows and provides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong desire—whether hunger, thirst, or ambition—do you pause to recognize that this desire itself is a created thing, placed in you by Allah? How would acknowledging this change your relationship with your desires today? Will you commit to saying 'Alhamdulillah' the next time you feel a need, seeing it as a sign of His provision?
?
The lesson says you are ignorant of the aspect of your need to others. In what area of your life have you experienced this—where you relied on someone without fully understanding why? How does that dependence point you to the Director? Are you willing to thank Him for the people He has made a means for your sufficiency, even when you don't fully understand the arrangement?
?
Consider a time you felt completely helpless and needy. Did you turn to Allah as the Director, or did you try to control the situation yourself? The proof says you cannot remove your own need. What would it look like for you to surrender that area of your life to His management today? Will you make a firm intention to do so right now?

The Proof from the Impossibility of Self-Management
Maturidi argues from human incapacity to manage time and space to the existence of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues from human inability to manage personal affairs in time and space to the necessity of a self-subsistent Creator.

Core Idea
Our inability to manage ourselves points to a Director beyond us.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument: even the most intelligent person cannot fully control his own existence in relation to time and space. This limitation demonstrates that we do not own ourselves, and that the world requires an external, self-sufficient Being. In this lesson, we unpack this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the condensed style and the logical flow.
Pay attention to the duplicate phrase 'من الزمان' – it may be a scribal repetition.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify Maturidi's main premises: the inability of even the greatest human to manage subtle affairs, the impossibility of escaping time and space, and the conclusion that no one owns the world.
3
Reflect on the logical conclusion
Understand why the world cannot exist through a being that is itself in need, and how this forces the existence of a self-subsistent, knowing, powerful Being.
This is the core of the argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ، لو خلى بين أعقل الخلق وأعظمهم تدبيرا وبين تقدير أحواله وأفعاله من الزمان من الزمان والمكان فيما لطف منها لما احتمل وسعهم ، فمن دونه أحق . صرف قهر الزمان عن نفسه ولا إحاطة المكان ، ثبت أن منهم لا ثم لا يملك أحد العالم على ما هو عليه لا يجوز كونه به دون خارج من معناه فى إحاطة الحاجة به بل بالقيام بذاته عالما قادرا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And moreover, if the most intelligent of creation and the greatest in management were left alone to estimate his own states and actions in terms of time and place, even in the subtlest matters, it would not be within his capacity, let alone those below him. To remove from himself the dominance of time and encompassment of place is impossible. So it is established that none of them owns the world as it is; it is not permissible for it to be through him without an external being, in the sense of encompassing need through him; rather, it is through a being subsistent by itself, knowing, powerful. And there is no power but in God.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof: the inability of even the most intelligent and well-managing person to fully plan his own affairs in terms of time and space. Maturidi argues that if such a person were left to himself, he could not manage even the subtle details, let alone those less capable. Moreover, no one can free themselves from the constraints of time and place. Therefore, it is established that no one possesses the world as it is; the world cannot exist through a contingent being that is itself subject to need. Rather, it must be through a being that subsists by itself, knowing and powerful. This being is God.

Key Terms

الإحاطة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is denying human free will in daily affairs.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying free will; he is arguing that ultimately, the entire framework of time and space is beyond human control, pointing to a higher Director.

B) Because time and space constraints are beyond human capacity.
C) Because God directly controls every action.
D) Because humans are not created with free will.

Key Takeaway
Human inability to manage time and space proves that the world depends on a self-sufficient, knowing, and powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Eternity of the World's Matter
Maturidi refutes eternity of world's matter via two scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that the world's primordial matter is eternal, and why both possible scenarios lead to the conclusion that matter requires a Creator.

Core Idea
Eternal matter is no escape from contingency.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles the argument that the world's primordial matter (tina) could be eternal. He shows that whether this matter is like the contingent world or unlike it, it still bears the marks of dependence and need. Read on to see how he dismantles this alternative to divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: Maturidi presents two scenarios.
The passage uses technical terms like 'tina' (clay/matter) and 'jawhar' (substance).
2
Identify the two scenarios
Look for the two possibilities Maturidi considers: either the matter is of the same substance as the world, or it is of a different, self-sufficient substance.
The text uses 'fa-amma in kanat' and 'aw in kanat' to introduce the scenarios.
3
Understand the conclusion of each scenario
For each scenario, notice how Maturidi argues that the matter cannot be independent of a Creator. The first scenario leads to temporality, the second to need for a wise director.
The conclusion is that both scenarios point to the same need for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من يقول بقدم طينة العالم ، فأما إن كانت من جوهر هذه المعانى فيلحقها ما يلحق العالم ويظهر جوهرها عجزها وحاجاتها ، وهما دليلا حدث العالم وكونه فيلزم فيها ما يلزم فى غيرها ، أو كانت خارجة من هذا الجوهر غنية قوية بغيره ، لا تمسها الحاجات ولا يعترضها الشهوات الباعثة على الحيل المفزعة إلى تأمل غناها وقوتها ، فأما إن كان العالم بها بأن اعترضت بها العوارض وانقلبت بجوهرها عما كانت عليه فصارت إلى هذه الحاجات والشهوات ، فصارت بجوهرها محتملة لكل حاجة ، محتملة لكل شهوة ، متمكنة للاستحالة والتغير ، فيبطل عنها جميع أوصاف الغنى والقوة ، وصارت أصل الحاجات وأم الشهوات ، فلزم انصراف تدبيرها إلى حكيم عليم ، على ما لزم ذلك في جميع العالم
Then regarding those who say that the world's clay (tina) is eternal: If it is of the substance of these meanings (i.e., contingent world), then what affects the world affects it, and its essence shows its incapacity and needs, which are the two proofs of the world's temporality and its coming-to-be, so what applies to others applies to it. Or if it is outside this substance, self-sufficient, powerful through another, untouched by needs and not assailed by appetites that drive to desperate stratagems to contemplate its self-sufficiency and power; but if the world is through it in that it is affected by accidents and its essence is altered from what it was, so it becomes subject to these needs and appetites, then its essence becomes susceptible to every need, susceptible to every appetite, capable of change and alteration, and all attributes of self-sufficiency and power are nullified from it, and it becomes the origin of needs and the mother of appetites. Then it becomes necessary that its management (tadbir) be directed to a wise, knowing one, just as that is necessary for the entire world.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that the world's primordial matter (tina) is eternal. Maturidi considers two scenarios. First, if this matter is of the same substance as the contingent world, then it shares the world's defects: incapacity and need, which prove the world's temporality. Thus, the matter itself is also temporal. Second, if the matter is of a different substance, self-sufficient and powerful through another, untouched by needs, then we must consider how the world came from it. If the world arises from it through change and alteration, then the matter's essence becomes subject to needs and appetites, losing its self-sufficiency. In that case, the matter itself requires a wise director, just as the world does. Either way, the eternal matter cannot be independent of a Creator.

Key Terms
طينة العالم
قدم
الجوهر
الحاجة
التغير
المدبر الحكيم
شهوات
عوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies the existence of primordial matter.
Correction: He argues that even if it exists, it cannot be eternal and independent of God.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw about the eternal matter if it is of the same substance as the contingent world?
A) It is eternal and self-sufficient.
B) It shares the world's incapacity and need, proving its temporality.
C) It is untouched by accidents.
D) It becomes the origin of needs.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the concept of eternal matter, in either scenario, fails to provide a self-sufficient cause and necessarily leads to the need for a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you habitually act as if your own 'matter'—your abilities, wealth, or status—is self-sufficient, ignoring that every change reveals your need for a wise Director? Where do you see yourself relying on your own power rather than acknowledging God's tadbir? What will you do today to shift that reliance?
?
The passage argues that even something that appears self-sufficient becomes needy when it interacts with the world. In what area of your life do you present a façade of self-sufficiency to others, while inwardly knowing it is full of needs? How will you bring that area under God's direction this week?
?
Maturidi shows that no matter how we conceive of the world's origin, it inevitably points to a wise Creator. How does this truth confront your tendency to explain away God's role in your personal story? What one intellectual or emotional barrier are you using to avoid submitting to His tadbir? What step can you take right now to break that barrier?

Refuting the Potentiality View: The Need for a Cause
Maturidi refutes the potentiality view of as-hab al-hayula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that the world existed in potentiality in eternal matter by showing that potentiality requires a cause for actualization.

Core Idea
Potentiality always needs a cause to become actual — matter is no exception.

Maturidi engages with the view of the proponents of hylé, who argued that the world pre-existed in potentiality in an eternal matter and then became actual. He uses examples from nature to show that this very reasoning proves that matter cannot actualize itself; it requires an external cause. In this passage, he dismantles their argument from within.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key terms: bi-quwwa (بالقوة) and bil-fi'l (بالفعل).
2
Identify the view being refuted
The passage states the view that the world existed in potentiality in eternal matter and then emerged in actuality. Underline who holds this view: as-hab al-hayula.
3
Analyze the examples
Maturidi lists examples like the human in the drop, the tree in the pit, etc. Ask yourself: what do these examples have in common? They all require an external cause to bring them from potential to actual.
These examples are used to draw an analogy with primordial matter.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi argues that if these natural potentialities need a cause, then the 'clay' and 'hylé' must also need a cause. The conclusion undermines the view that matter alone can account for the world's existence.
This refutes the idea of an eternal self-actualizing matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو كانت هي بحالها، لكن العالم كان فيها بقوة ، ظهر بالفعل ، وذلك هو قول أصحاب الهيولى ، ثم دل تلف جميع ما في العالم من كون شيء في شيء بالقوة إذا خرج منه بالفعل نحو ما يقولون من كون النسمة في النطقة ، وكل حيوان في النطف أو البيض ، والعصف في الحب ، والشجر فى النواة ، وكذلك كل الجواهر ، ومثله عندهم البقاء في الأغذية والنماء ونحو ذلك ، فيجب أن يكون أمر الطينة التي قالوا بها وأمر الهيولى كذلك ؛ إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم
Or that it (the matter) remains as it is, but the world was in it in potentiality (bi-quwwa) and appeared in actuality (bil-fi'l). That is the view of the proponents of hylé (as-hab al-hayula). Then evidence from all things in the world that something is in something in potentiality and then emerges from it in actuality, such as they say: the human being in the drop of fluid, every animal in the sperm or egg, the bird in the egg, the tree in the pit, and likewise all substances. And similarly, according to them, lastingness is in food, growth, and so on. So the matter of the clay (tina) they speak of and the matter of hylé must be likewise, since they are the origin of the entire world.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage refutes the view of those who hold to hylé (primordial matter), who claim that the world existed in potentiality in the eternal matter and then emerged in actuality. Maturidi points out that this analogy from natural processes (e.g., human from sperm, tree from pit) actually undermines their claim, because in all these examples, the potential becomes actual through an external agent or process. The primordial matter itself, if it remains unchanged, cannot account for the actualization of the world without a director. The conclusion is that their own analogy demands that the clay and hylé require a cause for actualization, just as other things do.

Key Terms
القوة
الفعل
الهيولى
النطفة
النواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That potentiality in matter means the world can emerge without a cause.
Correction: Maturidi shows that potentiality always requires a cause to become actual, as seen in natural examples. Therefore, primordial matter also needs a cause.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's point about the examples of potentiality (like human in sperm)?
A) They prove that matter is eternal.
B) They show that potentiality always requires a cause for actualization.
C) They demonstrate that the world is eternal.
D) They are irrelevant to the argument.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the very examples the hylé proponents use to support their view actually prove the need for a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence, do you ever trace it back to a mere potential in your parents? What does it mean for you personally that this potential needed an external cause to become actual, and that you are not self-caused? How does this shape your reliance on Allah every moment?
?
Maturidi shows that even the examples of natural generation (bird from egg, tree from pit) require a cause. What aspects of your life (health, wealth, relationships) do you subconsciously treat as self-caused or due to chance? Will you consciously acknowledge Allah as the cause behind them today?
?
The proponents of hylé thought matter could be the ultimate source. Is there something in your life—like your abilities, status, or possessions—that you rely on as if it were self-sufficient? How can you practically remind yourself that everything actual requires a Cause?

The World's Substance Shows Neediness
Maturidi sets up a disjunction about the world's substance to prove neediness and a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the inherent neediness of the world's substance (jawhar) to prove the necessity of a wise, knowledgeable Creator.

Core Idea
The world's substance is inherently needy, so it must be governed by a wise Creator.

Maturidi begins by examining the nature of the world's fundamental substance (jawhar). He sets up two possibilities: either the substance is self-sufficient or it is needy. Through observation, he shows it is needy, leading to the conclusion that its governance must come from a wise and knowledgeable Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the disjunction being presented.
Notice the two possibilities about the substance's nature.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Look for the two branches: either the substance is self-sufficient or it is needy. Maturidi uses these to frame his argument.
The transition 'أما إن كان العالم بها بأن...' signals the second branch.
3
Connect changeability to neediness
Maturidi argues that because the substance is subject to accidents and change (انقلبت بجوهرها), it becomes needy, and thus requires governance from a wise, knowledgeable being.
This is a standard kalam contingency argument but focused on jawhar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يلحق العالم ويظهر جوهرها عجزها وحاجاتها ، وهما دليلا حدث العالم وكونه فيلزم فيها ما يلزم فى غيرها ، أو كانت خارجة من هذا الجوهر غنية قوية بغيره ، لا تمسها الحاجات ولا يعترضها الشهوات الباعثة على الحيل المفزعة إلى تأمل غناها وقوتها ، فأما إن كان العالم بها بأن اعترضت بها العوارض وانقلبت بجوهرها عما كانت عليه فصارت إلى هذه الحاجات والشهوات ، فصارت بجوهرها محتملة لكل حاجة ، محتملة لكل شهوة ، متمكنة للاستحالة والتغير ، فيبطل عنها جميع أوصاف الغنى والقوة ، وصارت أصل الحاجات وأم الشهوات ، فلزم انصراف تدبيرها إلى حكيم عليم ، على ما لزم ذلك في جميع العالم ، أو كانت هي بحالها، لكن العالم كان فيها بقوة ، ظهر بالفعل ، وذلك هو قول أصحاب الهيولى ، ثم دل تلف جميع ما في العالم من كون شيء في شيء بالقوة إذا خرج منه بالفعل نحو ما يقولون من كون النسمة في النطقة ، وكل حيوان في النطف أو البيض ، والعصف في الحب ، والشجر فى النواة ، وكذلك كل الجواهر ، ومثله عندهم البقاء في الأغذية والنماء ونحو ذلك ، فيجب أن يكون أمر الطينة التي قالوا بها وأمر الهيولى كذلك ؛ إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم .. وكذلك يلزم القرامطة - في قولهم إن المبدع الأول فيه جميع العالم مبروزا تستمد منه النَّفْس الكل فيمد الهيولى ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول ؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل وقد صيروا جميعا الوجود للحال دليل الأولية ، لكن الأول جوهر الكل والثانى جوهر الجزء ، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء ؛ إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس ، وبالله التوفيق .
What attaches to the world and reveals the incapacity and needs of its substance (jawhar) — these are the two proofs of the world's origination (huduth) and its existence, so what is necessary in other things is necessary in it. Or [the substance] is outside this substance, rich and strong by other than itself, needs do not touch it, nor do desires that drive to devices making one rush to contemplate its richness and strength. But if the world is by it [the substance] in that accidents affect it and it is changed in its substance from what it was, and so it becomes subject to these needs and desires, then by its substance it becomes susceptible to every need, susceptible to every desire, capable of change and alteration, then all attributes of richness and strength are voided for it, and it becomes the source of needs and the mother of desires. So its governance must be directed to a wise, knowledgeable (Creator), as that is necessary in the whole world. Or [the substance] is as it is, but the world was in it potentially, and appeared actually — that is the saying of the proponents of hyle (ashab al-hayula). Then the perishing of all that is in the world from something being in something potentially, when it emerges from it actually, indicates — like their saying of the soul being in the sperm, every animal in sperm or eggs, the bird in the egg, the tree in the seed, and likewise all substances, and similarly according to them preservation in foods and growth and the like. So it must be that the matter of the clay (al-tina) they spoke of and the matter of hyle are likewise, since they are the origin of the whole world. Similarly, it is necessary for the Qaramitah — in their saying that the first originated being (al-mubdi' al-awwal) contains the whole world potentially, the universal soul derives from it, it furnishes hyle, and from it the composition of the world — that the first should perish, since this is the truth of everything that is in something potentially and appears actually. And they all made existence a proof of primacy (al-awwaliyya) at the moment, but the first is the substance of the whole, and the second is the substance of the part, and the substance of the whole is known by the substance of the part, since it is not something that anyone can perceive by sense. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the nature of the world's substance (jawhar) and its implications for the existence of a wise Creator, while refuting the theories of hyle and the Qaramitah. The author first presents a disjunction: either the world's substance is inherently weak and needy, which proves its origination and need for a Creator, or it is independent and strong, which would exempt it from needs. But observation shows the substance is subject to accidents and change, proving it is needy. Therefore, its governance must come from a wise, knowledgeable being. The author then addresses the view of 'ashab al-hayula (proponents of prime matter) who claim the world existed potentially in a substrate and appeared actually. He argues that since everything that exists potentially eventually perishes (as seen in sperm, seeds, etc.), the primordial substrate (hyle or clay) would also be perishable. He extends the same critique to the Qaramitah, who claim the first originated being contains the whole world potentially, and that the world emerges from it. If so, the first must perish like all potential things. The author concludes by noting that they all make existence evidence of primacy, but the first (the whole) is unknowable in itself, known only through its parts, and ultimately inaccessible to sense. The passage is a dense argument combining contingency, potentiality, and refutation of rival views.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar, substance)
حكيم عليم (hakim 'alim, wise knowledgeable)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi's argument is simply the standard kalam cosmological argument from contingency, but he specifically targets the nature of substance (jawhar) and its inherent changeability as the proof.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is distinctive in focusing on the substance's subjection to accidents and change, leading to neediness, rather than mere contingency of existence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to the world's substance when it is affected by accidents?
A) It becomes self-sufficient and strong.
B) It becomes subject to needs and desires, proving its neediness.
C) It remains unchanged and eternal.
D) It becomes the Creator itself.

Key Takeaway
The world's substance is inherently needy because it is subject to change, so its governance must be from a wise, knowledgeable Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an unexpected change in your life—like a loss or a new challenge—do you feel your own helplessness and turn to the One who governs all change, or do you rely on your own plans? What will you do today to consciously acknowledge your need for Al-Hakim in a specific situation?
?
This passage says the world's substance becomes 'the source of needs and the mother of desires.' Honestly, do you see yourself as self-sufficient or as constantly in need? How would admitting your complete neediness to Allah every morning change the way you live the rest of the day?
?
Imagine you truly believed that every need and desire in your heart is a sign that you are governed by a wise Creator. Would that change how you respond to your desires? Think of one desire you struggle with—how can you actively hand its governance to Allah today, trusting that He knows what you need?

Refuting the Hyle Theory of Potentiality
Maturidi refutes the hyle theory by showing potential existence implies perishability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the hyle theory, which posits that the world existed potentially in a substrate and emerged actually.

Core Idea
If the world existed potentially in hyle, then hyle itself would perish like all things that exist potentially.

Maturidi now addresses the view of the proponents of hyle (prime matter), who claim that the world was present potentially in a substrate and then emerged actually. He argues that everything that exists in something else potentially eventually perishes when it comes out actually, as seen in seeds and sperm. Therefore, the hyle itself would be perishable, contradicting its role as an eternal origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on hyle
Read the Arabic text from 'أو كانت هي بحالها' to 'إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم' and its translation.
Notice the examples Maturidi gives: soul in sperm, animal in egg, tree in seed.
2
Understand the hyle claim
The hyle theorists say the world was in hyle potentially (bi-quwwa) and emerged actually (bi'l-fi'l). Maturidi counters by showing that anything existing potentially does not remain potentially indefinitely.
3
Apply the same logic to hyle
Maturidi argues that since the world's constituents come out of their potential state and perish (e.g., the sperm ceases to be), the hyle itself, being the substrate for potential existence, would also perish. Thus hyle cannot be an eternal origin.
This undermines the hyle theory's claim to explain cosmic origins.

Source Text
