Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to the substrate when something emerges from potentiality to actuality, as in the example of the sperm?
A) The substrate becomes stronger.
B) The substrate perishes or changes.
C) The substrate continues to exist unchanged.
D) The substrate becomes the actual thing.

Key Takeaway
The hyle theory fails because potential existence always leads to the perishing of the substrate, so hyle cannot be an eternal origin of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that when potential becomes actual, the original substrate perishes. What 'potential' are you holding onto in your life—a good deed you keep postponing, a repentance you delay—thinking it can stay safe indefinitely? When will you let it become actual, knowing that the moment of delay itself perishes?
?
The hyle theorists sought an eternal origin to avoid acknowledging that everything (including their own existence) is contingent upon Allah. What 'eternal safety net' do you rely on in your heart—your health, youth, wealth, or even your own good deeds—as if it were truly permanent? How does this lesson expose that trust as misplaced?
?
If every substrate perishes when its potential is actualized, then every moment you do not act on your intention to obey Allah, that potential dies. Look at your last week: how many intentions to pray on time, to lower your gaze, to give charity never became actual? What do you think that says about the state of your heart's 'hyle'? Will you let today's intention perish too?

Refuting the Qaramitah's First Originated Being
Maturidi refutes Qaramitah cosmology by applying the potentiality-perishability argument and noting the imperceptibility of the first being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Qaramitah claim that the first originated being contains the world potentially, and his argument that such a being would perish like all potential things.

Core Idea
The Qaramitah's first originated being, if it contains the world potentially, would perish when the world becomes actual.

Maturidi extends his critique to the Qaramitah, who believe in a first originated being (al-mubdi' al-awwal) that contains the whole world potentially. From that being, the universal soul derives and supplies hyle, leading to the composition of the world. Maturidi applies the same logic: if the world was in the first being potentially, then that first being must perish when the world emerges actually, just like all other potential things. He also notes that the first being (the whole) is known only through its parts and is imperceptible, further undermining its alleged primacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qaramitah passage
Read the Arabic text from 'وكذلك يلزم القرامطة' to the end, along with the translation.
Notice how Maturidi connects the Qaramitah view to the same potentiality problem.
2
Understand the Qaramitah view
The Qaramitah say the first originated being (al-mubdi' al-awwal) contains the world potentially, the universal soul derives from it, and the soul supplies hyle.
This is a complex emanationist cosmology.
3
Apply the potentiality critique
Maturidi argues that since the world is in the first being potentially, the first being must perish when the world becomes actual, just like other potential substrates. Thus the first being cannot be eternal.
The conclusion: 'أن يتلف الأول'.
4
Note the epistemological point
Maturidi adds that the first being (the whole) is known only through its parts (the world) and is imperceptible, undermining any claim to know it directly.
This reinforces the argument that the first being is not self-sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يلحق العالم ويظهر جوهرها عجزها وحاجاتها ، وهما دليلا حدث العالم وكونه فيلزم فيها ما يلزم فى غيرها ، أو كانت خارجة من هذا الجوهر غنية قوية بغيره ، لا تمسها الحاجات ولا يعترضها الشهوات الباعثة على الحيل المفزعة إلى تأمل غناها وقوتها ، فأما إن كان العالم بها بأن اعترضت بها العوارض وانقلبت بجوهرها عما كانت عليه فصارت إلى هذه الحاجات والشهوات ، فصارت بجوهرها محتملة لكل حاجة ، محتملة لكل شهوة ، متمكنة للاستحالة والتغير ، فيبطل عنها جميع أوصاف الغنى والقوة ، وصارت أصل الحاجات وأم الشهوات ، فلزم انصراف تدبيرها إلى حكيم عليم ، على ما لزم ذلك في جميع العالم ، أو كانت هي بحالها، لكن العالم كان فيها بقوة ، ظهر بالفعل ، وذلك هو قول أصحاب الهيولى ، ثم دل تلف جميع ما في العالم من كون شيء في شيء بالقوة إذا خرج منه بالفعل نحو ما يقولون من كون النسمة في النطقة ، وكل حيوان في النطف أو البيض ، والعصف في الحب ، والشجر فى النواة ، وكذلك كل الجواهر ، ومثله عندهم البقاء في الأغذية والنماء ونحو ذلك ، فيجب أن يكون أمر الطينة التي قالوا بها وأمر الهيولى كذلك ؛ إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم .. وكذلك يلزم القرامطة - في قولهم إن المبدع الأول فيه جميع العالم مبروزا تستمد منه النَّفْس الكل فيمد الهيولى ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول ؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل وقد صيروا جميعا الوجود للحال دليل الأولية ، لكن الأول جوهر الكل والثانى جوهر الجزء ، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء ؛ إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس ، وبالله التوفيق .
What attaches to the world and reveals the incapacity and needs of its substance (jawhar) — these are the two proofs of the world's origination (huduth) and its existence, so what is necessary in other things is necessary in it. Or [the substance] is outside this substance, rich and strong by other than itself, needs do not touch it, nor do desires that drive to devices making one rush to contemplate its richness and strength. But if the world is by it [the substance] in that accidents affect it and it is changed in its substance from what it was, and so it becomes subject to these needs and desires, then by its substance it becomes susceptible to every need, susceptible to every desire, capable of change and alteration, then all attributes of richness and strength are voided for it, and it becomes the source of needs and the mother of desires. So its governance must be directed to a wise, knowledgeable (Creator), as that is necessary in the whole world. Or [the substance] is as it is, but the world was in it potentially, and appeared actually — that is the saying of the proponents of hyle (ashab al-hayula). Then the perishing of all that is in the world from something being in something potentially, when it emerges from it actually, indicates — like their saying of the soul being in the sperm, every animal in sperm or eggs, the bird in the egg, the tree in the seed, and likewise all substances, and similarly according to them preservation in foods and growth and the like. So it must be that the matter of the clay (al-tina) they spoke of and the matter of hyle are likewise, since they are the origin of the whole world. Similarly, it is necessary for the Qaramitah — in their saying that the first originated being (al-mubdi' al-awwal) contains the whole world potentially, the universal soul derives from it, it furnishes hyle, and from it the composition of the world — that the first should perish, since this is the truth of everything that is in something potentially and appears actually. And they all made existence a proof of primacy (al-awwaliyya) at the moment, but the first is the substance of the whole, and the second is the substance of the part, and the substance of the whole is known by the substance of the part, since it is not something that anyone can perceive by sense. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the nature of the world's substance (jawhar) and its implications for the existence of a wise Creator, while refuting the theories of hyle and the Qaramitah. The author first presents a disjunction: either the world's substance is inherently weak and needy, which proves its origination and need for a Creator, or it is independent and strong, which would exempt it from needs. But observation shows the substance is subject to accidents and change, proving it is needy. Therefore, its governance must come from a wise, knowledgeable being. The author then addresses the view of 'ashab al-hayula (proponents of prime matter) who claim the world existed potentially in a substrate and appeared actually. He argues that since everything that exists potentially eventually perishes (as seen in sperm, seeds, etc.), the primordial substrate (hyle or clay) would also be perishable. He extends the same critique to the Qaramitah, who claim the first originated being contains the whole world potentially, and that the world emerges from it. If so, the first must perish like all potential things. The author concludes by noting that they all make existence evidence of primacy, but the first (the whole) is unknowable in itself, known only through its parts, and ultimately inaccessible to sense. The passage is a dense argument combining contingency, potentiality, and refutation of rival views.

Key Terms
القرامطة (al-Qaramitah)
المبدع الأول (al-mubdi' al-awwal, the first originated being)
النَّفْس الكل (al-nafs al-kull, the universal soul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Qaramitah view avoids the problem because the first being is spiritual, but Maturidi shows that the same law of potentiality applies universally.
Correction: Even if the first being is non-material, the principle that potential existence leads to perishing of the substrate still holds, as Maturidi applies it to the Qaramitah's first being.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must the Qaramitah's first originated being perish?
A) Because it is composed of parts.
B) Because it contains the world potentially, and all potential things cause the substrate to perish when actualized.
C) Because the universal soul consumes it.
D) Because it is made of hyle.

Key Takeaway
The Qaramitah's first originated being, containing the world potentially, would necessarily perish like all potential substrates, and it remains unknowable through the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that anything existing potentially perishes when actualized. What is something in your own life—a skill, relationship, or ambition—that you hold only as a potential? If it becomes actual, are you prepared for what it might cost you? Will you cling to it or let it be transformed?
?
The Qaramitah claimed the first being is known through the existence of the world, but Maturidi says it is imperceptible. Have you ever assumed you understood God based solely on what you can see and sense? How might this passage expose a hidden reliance on sensory proof in your own faith? Commit today to seeking knowledge of the unseen through revelation and reason, not mere observation.

Contingency and Refutation of Eternal Potentiality
Maturidi establishes contingency and refutes Qaramitah potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi establishes that all contingent events point to a contingent origin and why the Qaramitah view of an eternal origin with potentiality is self-defeating.

Core Idea
If the origin contained all things potentially, then nothing could be known for certain.

Maturidi continues his argument for tawhid by examining the implications of contingency. He argues that if the world is made up of contingent events, then the origin itself must be contingent and could not be eternal as the Qaramitah claim. Moreover, their model of potentiality actually leads to ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Note the logical flow from contingency to refutation.
The passage is dense—slow down and follow each claim.
2
Identify the two key claims
Identify Maturidi's claim that the origin is contingent and the Qaramitah claim of an eternal origin. Notice how he uses the concept of potentiality to refute them.
Look for the phrase 'القوة' (potentiality) and its implication.
3
Understand the example of sperms and seeds
Maturidi uses the analogy of sperms and seeds to show that potentiality implies ignorance of future states. Reflect on why this applies to hyle and clay as well.
This example illustrates the absurdity of the Qaramitah view.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the Qaramitah claim lacks governance (تدبير) and is invalid. Ask yourself: Why does potentiality lead to ignorance?
This step solidifies the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت جميع ما بيننا من الحوادث والحاجات التي هي للحدث لذلك الأصل؛ إذ صار محتملا للتلف والفناء، وذلك أيضا يلزم القرامطة يجعلونه أبديا، وإن كانوا يقولون : كان بعد أن لم يكن بالإبداع، ولا قوة إلا بالله ودليل الجهل تمن فيه ما فيه بالقوة بأحوال ما فيه ومن فيه وما يكون ذكرت من جهل النطف والحبوب وغيرها، فيلزم كذلك في الهيولى والطينة وما قالوا، ولزم أن ليس لما قالوا تدبير، ولا كان شيء من ذلك به، ولكن إن كان فهو يمن علم ما يكون فيجعل أصله مبروزا فيه بالقوة، يظهر بالفعل بما جعل له من المواد والأمكنة التي بها تنمو في ذلك القول بالتوحيد، وأنه منشى ذلك كله ليكون به كل شيء يكون على ما قالوا، أو أن يكون محدثا لكل شيء يكون أبدا على ما يشاء أن يكون من أصول وابتداء، أو كيف شاء على ما يقوله أهل التوحيد. وإذ الله سبحانه قادر على إنشاء أصل فيه كل شيء مبروزا، هو قادر على ابتداء كل شيء كما شاء من غير بروز بالقوة ولا خروج بالفعل، ولكن بالتقدير والتكوين . وإن زعموا أن الأشياء كانت في الأصل مستجنّة بجوهرها فيظهر بالفعل فهي أيضا ترجع إلى ما قلنا؛ لأن ذلك قولهم فى النطف والحبوب، مع ما في هذا مما في العقل دفعه بما لا يحتمل تمكن أضعاف الشيء بجوهره فيه؛ للتناقض والفساد وتكذيب العيان .
The proofs: and when this is established, all the contingent events and needs that are for the contingent (al-hadith) are established for that origin, since it [the origin] becomes susceptible to perishing and annihilation. And that also is necessary for the Qaramitah who make it eternal, even though they say: it was after it had not been, by origination (ibda'). And there is no power except by God. And the proof of ignorance [is that] what is in it potentially is prevented [from knowledge]? by the states of what is in it, of it, and what will be, mentioned from ignorance of sperms and seeds and others. So likewise it is necessary in hyle and clay and what they said. And it is necessary that they have no governance [of their claim], nor was anything from that by it. But if it [the claim] is, then it is prevented? from knowledge of what will be, so it makes its origin potentially manifested, appearing actually by what is made for it of materials and places by which it grows in that statement of tawhid, and that He [God] is the originator of all that so that everything that will be exists according to what they said, or that He is the creator of everything that will be eternally as He wills to be from origins and beginnings, or how He wills according to what the people of tawhid say. And since God, exalted, is able to create an origin in which everything is manifested potentially, He is able to originate everything as He wills without potential manifestation nor actual emergence, but rather by estimation and creation (al-taqdir wa al-takwin). And if they claim that things were in the origin hidden in their substance and then appear actually, that also returns to what we said, because that is their saying concerning sperms and seeds, along with what in this the intellect can refute by that which does not allow the existence of multiples/weaknesses? of a thing in its substance, due to contradiction, corruption, and the falsification of direct observation.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the argument, now establishing tawhid by showing that the Qaramitah view leads to absurdities, and affirming God's direct creation. The author begins by stating that once the previous proof (the neediness and contingency of the world) is established, then all the contingent events and needs we observe are proven to belong to that original contingent substrate. Since that substrate is susceptible to perishing, the Qaramitah cannot claim it is eternal even if they say it was originated (ibda'). Their claim that the first originated being contains all things potentially actually prevents knowledge of future states, because potentiality is unknown. The same reasoning applies to hyle and clay (tina). Consequently, their claim has no governance, and if it did, it would preclude knowledge of what will be. The correct view, according to the people of tawhid, is that God is the originator of everything, creating each thing directly as He wills, without any pre-existent potentiality. God is able to create an origin containing everything potentially, but He is also able to create each thing individually as He wills, through estimation (taqdir) and creation (takwin). Finally, the author refutes the claim that things preexist hidden in a substance and then appear: this reduces to the same argument as with sperms and seeds, and reason rejects it because it implies contradictory states (multiple potentials in one substance), which contradicts both reason and direct observation.

Key Terms
الإبداع (ibda')
القوة (quwwa)
القرامطة (al-Qaramitah)
الهيولى (hayula)
الطينة (al-tina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qaramitah model that an eternal first being contains all things potentially is compatible with tawhid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that potentiality leads to ignorance and contingency, so the origin cannot be eternal; only direct creation by God is consistent with tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the Qaramitah view of an eternal origin with potentiality fail?
A) Because potentiality implies that the origin is not originated.
B) Because potentiality leads to ignorance of future states, as seen in sperms and seeds.
C) Because the origin cannot be eternal if it contains potentiality.
D) Because the Qaramitah deny the existence of hyle and clay.

Key Takeaway
The Qaramitah model of an eternal origin with potentiality is refuted because potentiality implies ignorance, contradicting the idea of a knowable, governed creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust in potential—your plans, your abilities, your future. But the passage warns that potentiality without God's governance leads to ignorance. Where in your life have you assumed that because something has potential, you can know its outcome without relying on the Creator? Will you repent of that subtle self-reliance today?
?
The Qaramitah held to a philosophy that seemed logical—yet it obscured their knowledge of God. Are there theological or philosophical ideas you cling to that sound sensible but actually blind you to the majesty and knowledge of Allah? Are you willing to abandon them even if they are comfortable?
?
The passage concludes that their claim lacks 'governance' (تدبير). Do you live as if every detail of your existence is under God's direct governance, or do you act as if some things are left to blind potential? What would it look like to truly believe 'لا قوة إلا بالله' in your next decision?

Affirmation of Direct Creation and Final Refutation
Maturidi affirms direct creation and refutes hidden substance model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi affirms that God creates directly by estimation and creation (al-taqdir wa al-takwin), and why the alternative model of things hidden in a substance is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
God creates each thing as He wills, without pre-existent potential or actual emergence.

Having refuted the Qaramitah model of potentiality, Maturidi now presents the correct understanding of tawhid: God's direct creation. He asserts that God is able to create things individually without relying on any pre-existing potential. He also refutes the claim that things preexist hidden in a substance, showing it leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the shift from refutation to positive affirmation.
This passage contains the core positive doctrine.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Maturidi contrasts two possibilities: either everything emerges from a potential-laden origin, or God creates directly. Identify the one he affirms.
Look for 'بالتقدير والتكوين' (estimation and creation).
3
Understand the refutation of hidden substance
Analyze the argument against things being hidden in a substance. Maturidi says it reduces to the same problem as sperms and seeds, and reason rejects it due to contradiction and falsification of observation.
This refutation uses logical impossibility.
4
Reflect on tawhid
Conclude that the proper tawhid is that God creates each thing directly as He wills, without any intermediate potentiality.
This affirms God's absolute power and knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the proper method of creation according to Maturidi in this passage?
A) By bringing things out of a hidden potential in a primordial substance.
B) By estimation and creation (al-taqdir wa al-takwin) without any potential or actual emergence.
C) By a process of emanation from a first principle.
D) By using pre-existing materials and shaping them.

Key Takeaway
God's direct creation by estimation and creation is the only coherent model of tawhid; any model involving pre-existent potential or hidden substances is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God creates everything directly, or do you secretly assume that some things 'just happen' through natural processes independent of His immediate will? What in your daily life—your reactions, worries, or thanks—reveals your actual belief? Will you today consciously attribute every small event to His direct creation?
?
When you witness the growth of a plant or the development of a fetus, do you attribute it to inherent potentials in matter, or do you see it as God's direct act of creation at every moment? How would your gratitude and reliance on Him change if you truly saw His hand in every detail?
?
The passage states that any model involving pre-existent potential is contradictory and falsified by observation. Are there areas in your understanding of the world where you unconsciously accept contradictory explanations—like attributing some outcomes to luck, chance, or nature? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your tawhid and live with complete trust in God's sole creativity?

God Existed Without Place – The Principle and Signs of Contingency
Maturidi states the principle and lists signs of contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's foundational principle that God's existence precedes all place, and how change, cessation, transformation, and annihilation are signs of contingency that do not apply to God.

Core Idea
God's timeless existence without place is proven by His immunity to the signs of contingency.

Maturidi begins his treatise by laying down a fundamental principle: God existed eternally before any place existed. He then lists the marks of created things—change, cessation, transformation, annihilation—to argue that these cannot apply to the Creator. This lesson walks you through that opening argument, showing how it establishes divine transcendence over time and space.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning. Let the author's own phrasing sink in.
Pay attention to the list of four signs—they are central.
2
Identify the core principle
Locate the phrase 'الأصل فيه أن الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان'. This is the thesis: God's existence is prior to all place.
The word 'مكان' (place) is the key term.
3
Spot the four signs of contingency
The passage enumerates 'التغير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان' as signs of createdness. Understand each one.
These are evidence that something is not eternal.
4
Connect signs to God's nature
Ask yourself: Since God is eternal, why can't these signs apply to Him? The passage argues that if God changed, His first state would not be essential—contradicting His necessity.
This links the signs to the argument for God's unchanging essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : الأصل فيه أن الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان وجائز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان ، فهو على ما كان ، وكان على ما عليه الآن ، جل عن التغير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان ؛ إذ ذلك أمارات الحدث التي بها عرف حدث العالم ، ودلالة احتمال الفناء ؛ إذ لا فرق بين الزوال من حال إلى حال ، ليعلم أن حاله الأولى لم تكن لذاته ؛ إذ لا يحتمل زوال ما لزم ذاته . وبين أنها ليست لذاته ، لما احتمل هو قبول الأعراض وانتقال الأحوال، ولا قوة إلا بالله . و بعد ، فإن فى تحقيق المكان له والوصف له بذاته فى كل مكان تمكين الحاجة له إلى ما به قراره على مثل جميع الأجسام والأعراض التي قامت بالأمكنة وفيها تقلبت وقرب، على خروج جملتها عن الوصف بالمكان ، فمن أنشأها وأمسك كيانها لا يماثلها، ويتعالى عن الحاجة إلى مكان ، أو الوصف بما عليه العالم أن كليته لا في مكان ، وأنه يجزئياته في المكان. إن الله تعالى لو جعل في مكان لجعل بحق الجزئية من العالم، وذلك أثر النقصان ، بل لما استقام قيام جميع العالم لا بالأمكنة للجملة ، فقيمه على ذلك أحق وأولى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is that Allah, glorified be He, existed and there was no place, and it is possible for places to cease to exist while He remains as He was. So He is as He was, and He was as He is now, exalted above change, cessation, transformation, and annihilation; for those are signs of contingency by which the contingency of the world is known, and an indication of the possibility of perishing; for there is no difference between passing from one state to another, so that one knows that His first state was not by His essence; for it is not possible for that which is necessary for His essence to pass away. And it is clarified that they are not by His essence, because He is subject to the reception of accidents and the transition of states. And there is no power except with Allah. And after that, indeed, in establishing place for Him and describing Him by His essence in every place is enabling need for Him for that by which He is established, like all bodies and accidents that subsist in places and in which they change and draw near, while their entirety is beyond description with place. So the One who originated them and holds their existence is not like them, and is exalted above need for a place, or the description that the world is such that its totality is not in a place, while its particulars are in a place. Indeed, if Allah were placed in a place, He would be placed by right of particularity from the world, and that is an effect of deficiency. Rather, since the existence of the entire world is established without places for the whole, then the One who establishes it is more worthy and deserving of that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage is a foundational argument for divine transcendence over place. Imam al-Maturidi starts with the principle (الأصل) that God existed eternally before any place existed, and even if all places were to cease, He would remain unchanged. Change, cessation, and transformation are signs of contingency (حدث) and potential annihilation. Since God is eternal and unchanging, He cannot undergo such changes. The author then argues that if God were described as being in a place, He would need that place to provide stability, just as bodies and accidents need places. But God is the Creator and Sustainer of all places, so He is unlike them and has no need for place. Moreover, if God were in a place, He would be a part of the world (جزئية), which is a deficiency. Since the whole world exists without needing a place for its totality (the world is not itself in a place), the Creator is even more entitled to be free from place. The passage concludes by affirming that God is exalted above all spatial limitations.

Key Terms
مكان
حدث
تغير
زوال
استحالة
بطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes (like being 'above') imply He can change location or state.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any change, including change of state or place, is a sign of contingency and cannot apply to the eternal God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the four signs (change, cessation, transformation, annihilation) indicate?
A) They are attributes of perfection
B) They are signs of contingency (حدث)
C) They are possible for God
D) They are irrelevant to creation

Key Takeaway
God's eternal existence without place is proven by the fact that change, cessation, transformation, and annihilation are signs of contingency, which are impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust in created things that change and perish every day—your health, your wealth, your relationships. How does knowing that God alone is unchanging and beyond all place affect where you will place your ultimate security today? What will you consciously hold onto differently after this lesson?
?
When you catch yourself fearing change or loss, does your heart turn to the One who cannot change? If not, what does that reveal about what you truly believe is reliable? Before you sleep tonight, will you spend five minutes thanking God for being the only unshakable reality in your life?
?
Every contingent thing in your life—including your own existence—is a sign pointing to its Maker. How do you habitually ignore that sign? What would it look like for you to stop anchoring your identity and worth in things that are subject to change, cessation, transformation, and annihilation, and instead anchor everything in the Eternal One?

Refuting the Need for Place – God's Transcendence Over Spatial Limitation
Maturidi refutes that God needs or occupies place, using need and particularity arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God cannot be in a place because place implies need, limitation, and deficiency, whereas God is the Originator of all places.

Core Idea
Placing God in a space would make Him dependent and a part of the world – an absurd deficiency.

Maturidi now tackles a common but mistaken theology: that God could be literally in a place. He argues that if God were in a place, He would need that place for stability, just as bodies and accidents do. Such need is incompatible with God's self-sufficiency. Moreover, being in a place would reduce God to a particular part of the world (جزئية), implying deficiency. This lesson unpacks that powerful argument, concluding with an analogy: the world as a whole is not itself in a place, so its Creator is even more free from place.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text beginning with 'و بعد، فإن في تحقيق المكان له' and follow the argument. Use the translation to check understanding.
The passage is dense – take it slowly.
2
Analyze the 'need' argument
Maturidi argues that if God were in a place, He would need that place for stability (تمكين الحاجة). Identify this reasoning.
This is similar to how bodies need places.
3
Examine the 'particularity' argument
He says that if God were in a place, He would be a 'juz'iyya' (particular) of the world, which is an effect of deficiency (أثر النقصان). Understand why.
Being confined to a specific location implies limitation.
4
Consider the analogy
Maturidi observes that the world as a whole is not in a place (كليته لا في مكان), yet its parts are. So the Creator is even more deserving of being beyond place.
This is a logical inference from the world's own structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : الأصل فيه أن الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان وجائز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان ، فهو على ما كان ، وكان على ما عليه الآن ، جل عن التغير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان ؛ إذ ذلك أمارات الحدث التي بها عرف حدث العالم ، ودلالة احتمال الفناء ؛ إذ لا فرق بين الزوال من حال إلى حال ، ليعلم أن حاله الأولى لم تكن لذاته ؛ إذ لا يحتمل زوال ما لزم ذاته . وبين أنها ليست لذاته ، لما احتمل هو قبول الأعراض وانتقال الأحوال، ولا قوة إلا بالله . و بعد ، فإن فى تحقيق المكان له والوصف له بذاته فى كل مكان تمكين الحاجة له إلى ما به قراره على مثل جميع الأجسام والأعراض التي قامت بالأمكنة وفيها تقلبت وقرب، على خروج جملتها عن الوصف بالمكان ، فمن أنشأها وأمسك كيانها لا يماثلها، ويتعالى عن الحاجة إلى مكان ، أو الوصف بما عليه العالم أن كليته لا في مكان ، وأنه يجزئياته في المكان. إن الله تعالى لو جعل في مكان لجعل بحق الجزئية من العالم، وذلك أثر النقصان ، بل لما استقام قيام جميع العالم لا بالأمكنة للجملة ، فقيمه على ذلك أحق وأولى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is that Allah, glorified be He, existed and there was no place, and it is possible for places to cease to exist while He remains as He was. So He is as He was, and He was as He is now, exalted above change, cessation, transformation, and annihilation; for those are signs of contingency by which the contingency of the world is known, and an indication of the possibility of perishing; for there is no difference between passing from one state to another, so that one knows that His first state was not by His essence; for it is not possible for that which is necessary for His essence to pass away. And it is clarified that they are not by His essence, because He is subject to the reception of accidents and the transition of states. And there is no power except with Allah. Proof for Seeing God from the Negation of Idrak
Maturidi affirms the vision of God and gives a proof from the verse negating idrak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that the negation of idrak in the Quran implies the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter without encompassing.

Core Idea
The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' proves we can see Him, but not encompass Him.

Maturidi opens a crucial topic: the beatific vision of Allah in the hereafter. He insists it is obligatory to believe in, yet clarifies it is not a comprehensive perception (idrak) nor does it require anthropomorphic interpretation (tafsir). Then he offers a striking proof from a well-known Quranic verse, turning the apparent negation into an affirmation of a real but non-encompassing vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi first states the doctrine, then provides the proof.
Pay attention to the terms 'ru'ya' (vision) and 'idrak' (comprehensive perception).
2
Analyze the proof structure
Identify the verse cited (6:103) and follow Maturidi's reasoning: If God were absolutely invisible, negating 'idrak' would be pointless because other things can be known without being seen. Therefore, the negation of 'idrak' implies that 'ru'ya' is possible but not encompassing.
The key is distinguishing between 'idrak' and 'ru'ya'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this tells us about the nature of seeing God: it is real, but unlike seeing physical objects, it does not grasp or encompass Him.
This preserves both God's transcendence and the reality of the vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمة الله : القول في رؤية الرب عز وجل عندنا لازم وحق من غير إدراك ولا تفسير . فأما الدليل على الرؤية فقوله تعالى: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ، ولو كان لا يرى لم يكن لنفي الإدراك حكمة؛ إذ يدرك غيره بغير رؤية ، فموضع نفى الإدراك - وغيره من الخلق لا يدرك إلا بالرؤية . لا معنى له ، وبالله التوفيق .
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The statement concerning the vision of the Lord, Mighty and Majestic, is necessary for us and true, without [literal] perception (idrak) or interpretation (tafsir). As for the proof for the vision, it is His statement: "No vision can grasp Him, but He grasps all vision." And if He were not to be seen, there would be no wisdom in negating idrak; for other than Him is perceived by other than vision. So the place of negating idrak – while other created beings are not perceived except by vision – would have no meaning. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's position on the beatific vision. He begins by stating that the belief in seeing God (in the hereafter) is obligatory and true, but it is not a perception that encompasses God (idrak) nor does it require interpretation in the sense of likening to physical sight. Then he provides a rational proof based on the verse "No vision can grasp Him" (6:103). He argues: If God were absolutely invisible, then negating idrak (which is a form of vision that encompasses) would be meaningless, because other things can be known without being seen. But the verse specifically negates idrak, implying that there is a form of seeing that does not encompass – hence vision is possible. The argument is concise: The verse does not say 'He cannot be seen' but 'no vision can grasp Him', leaving open the possibility of a vision without comprehension.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الإدراك
التفسير
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' proves that Allah cannot be seen at all, either in this world or the hereafter.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the verse only negates 'idrak' (comprehensive perception), not 'ru'ya' (vision). The negation of idrak implies that a form of vision (ru'ya) is possible but does not encompass Allah. This preserves the reality of the beatific vision while affirming God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main point in citing the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'?
A) To prove that God cannot be seen under any circumstances.
B) To prove that the verse is metaphorical and cannot be taken literally.
C) To prove that vision (ru'ya) is possible because only idrak is negated.
D) To prove that God's essence is visible to all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves the possibility of seeing God by showing that the Quran's negation of idrak actually implies a form of vision without comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the verse 'No vision can grasp Him,' does your heart feel distance or invitation? How would your daily worship change if you truly internalized that you will see Allah without fully grasping Him? This week, spend a moment after each prayer reflecting on the hope of that vision.
?
Maturidi argues that the negation of idrak actually affirms ru'ya. Is there any hidden doubt in your heart that the verse denies all seeing? If you are certain of seeing Allah, does that certainty move you to seek Him more earnestly through His names and attributes? What one step can you take today to cultivate that longing?
?
The wisdom (hikma) in the verse is to affirm vision without comprehension. Does your desire to know Allah prioritize grasping Him with your intellect, or are you content to see Him as He reveals Himself? Are you willing to let go of the need to fully understand, trusting that the vision will be enough? Set aside time this evening to pray for increase in certainty of meeting Him.

Mu'tazila Imply Eternity of the World and Agreement with Dahriyya
Maturidi shows the shay'iyya doctrine entails eternity of the world and aligns with Dahriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite doctrine of non-existent things leads to the conclusion that the world is eternal and aligns with atheistic materialism.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's 'things' doctrine makes the world eternal, contradicting monotheism.

Maturidi continues his critique of the Mu'tazila by exposing the logical consequences of their claim that non-existent things are eternal. He shows that this forces them to say the world is eternal, which all monotheists reject. Then he strengthens his point by comparing them unfavorably to the Dahriyya (materialists). In this passage, you will see how a seemingly minor philosophical shift leads to a major theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of Maturidi's argument.
Note that the Arabic has a textual variant 'وفيا' likely meaning 'وفيما'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise
The Mu'tazila hold that non-existent things (ma'dum) are eternal and distinct from God. Locate this in the passage.
The phrase 'والمعدوم أشياء سواه لم يزل' expresses this.
3
See how that implies world's eternity
Since the world is all that is not God, if non-existent things are eternal, then the world as a category must also be eternal. Maturidi argues this undermines creation ex nihilo.
4
Notice the comparison with Dahriyya
Maturidi says even the Dahriyya, who affirm an eternal creator, are closer to truth than the Mu'tazila. This highlights the severity of the Mu'tazilite error.
The Dahriyya at least avoid making the world co-eternal with God in a polytheistic sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيا قالوا قدم العالم ؛ لأنه الأشياء سوى الله ، والمعدوم أشياء سواه لم يزل، وفي ذلك مخالفة جميع الموحدين في إنشاء الله تعالى الأشياء من لا شيء، وعلى قبلهم إنما هو إنشاء بمعنى الإيجاد، وإلا فهي أشياء قبل الإنشاء، والله الموفق. فقول من يقول من الدهرية بالبارى على أنه لم يزل صانع الأشياء لتكون في الأزل أقرب من قول هؤلاء. وفيما قالوا أيضا إيجاب موافقة الدهرية في قولهم طيلة العالم قديمة.
And among what they said: the world is eternal because things other than God, and the non-existent is other than Him has never ceased, and in that is opposition to all monotheists in God's creation of things from nothing, and according to them it is only creation in the sense of bringing into existence, otherwise they are things before creation, and God grants success. So the saying of those who say from the Dahriyya about the Creator that He never ceased making things to be in pre-eternity is closer than the saying of these. And among what they said also is affirming agreement with the Dahriyya in their saying the duration of the world is eternal.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage argues that the Mu'tazilite position logically leads to the eternity of the world. If non-existents are eternal things distinct from God, then the world (which is all that is not God) is also eternal. This contradicts the consensus of monotheists that God creates from nothing. Maturidi even says that the Dahriyya (materialists) who posit an eternal creator constantly making the world are closer to truth than the Mu'tazila, because at least they admit a creator. The Mu'tazila, by affirming eternal non-existent things, effectively agree with the Dahriyya that the world is eternal.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
دهرية
إنشاء
إيجاد
موحدون
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazilite position is moderate and compatible with monotheism, since they affirm God's creation.
Correction: In reality, their doctrine of eternal non-existent things logically entails that the world is eternal and not created from nothing, aligning them with atheistic materialism (Dahriyya).

Checkpoint
Whose view does Maturidi say is closer to truth than the Mu'tazila?
A) The Christians
B) The Dahriyya
C) The Dualists
D) The Philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view on non-existent things ultimately leads to denying creation ex nihilo and aligns with materialism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life—your habits, your struggles, your identity—do you secretly assume these things have always been there? Do you act as if they are eternal 'things' that God will merely rearrange, or do you honestly confess that He brought them from nothing? How would your reliance on Him change if you truly believed each moment is a fresh creation?
?
The Mu'tazila went astray by granting pre-existence to non-existent things. Are there 'non-existent things' in your life—hopes, fears, possibilities—that you treat as real and permanent before God creates them? In what specific area do you need to repent of assuming something is 'already there' instead of waiting for God to create it?
?
Even the Dahriyya, with their error, are said to be closer to truth than those who deny creation ex nihilo. This shows how central this doctrine is. Do you truly live as though you are created out of nothing, completely dependent on God's will every second? What would change in your prayer, your work, your relationships if you meditated daily on the fact that you were once nothing and are sustained from nothing?

The World's Lack of Self-Sufficiency Refutes Deniers
Maturidi refutes the denial of the world as proof for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world cannot be self-sufficient and that denying it as proof for God leads to logical inconsistency.

Core Idea
Nothing in the world is self-caused; its existence demands a Creator.

Maturidi begins by establishing that the world has no inherent meaning or self-existence. Those who deny the world as evidence for God end up making the world self-caused, which is a contradiction. This lesson explores the initial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening refutation
Read the Arabic passage from 'فكان العالم' to 'حادثا' (first part). Note how Maturidi sets up the problem.
2
Understand the deniers' position
The deniers claim that existence does not necessitate a creator. Identify their two arguments: knowledge of existence does not imply creation, and pre-eternity does not necessitate existence.
They make the world self-caused.
3
Compare with the eternalists
Maturidi notes that those who affirm the eternity of the world are more consistent: if the world is not from another, it must be eternal. The deniers try to have it both ways.
4
Reflect on the inconsistency
Ask yourself: why is making the world self-caused while claiming it is originated a logical error?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكان العالم لا معنى منه إليه به كان، ثم صيروه دليلا عليه على القول بما ذكرت. فأما إن كذبوا بجعله دليلا، وبقولهم أن لم يكن منه غير كونه بعد يكن، ومذهبهم أن العلم بكون شيء لا يوجب تكوينه، والقدم لا يوجب كونه عندهم إلا هذين، لا يوجب واحد منها كونه، فأوجبوا كون به، وليس من الله العالم لا بأحد، على ما قال القائلون بقدم العالم إذ كان لا بغيره، فضاهوا بقولهم هذا قول أولئك، إلا أن أولئك ألزم للقياس؛ إذ لما كان العالم لا بغيره جعلوه أزليا، وهؤلاء جعلوه من الوجه الذي بينا لا بغيره حادثا. ثم أعجب منه أن جعلوا العالم خالقا ونفسه مخلوقة، وجعلوه صانعا ونفسه مصنوعة، فصار العالم عالما لا يصنع لغيره فيه، بصنع لغيره فيه، ثم ألزم نفسه كل اسم دنى لكان أعذر، ولكن ذا آية عواقب السوء. وكل اسم سنى، لو قلب اسم العالم العالم.
So the world has no meaning from itself to itself by which it existed, then they made it a proof for Him based on what I mentioned. As for if they deny making it a proof, and by their saying that nothing came from it except its existence after it was not, and their doctrine that knowledge of the existence of something does not necessitate its bringing into existence, and pre-eternity does not necessitate its existence according to them except these two, neither of them necessitates its existence, so they necessitated its existence by itself, and the world is not from God at all, according to what those who said the world is eternal said, since it was not from another, so they resembled in their saying the saying of those, except that those are more consistent with analogy; for since the world was not from another they made it eternal, and these made it from the aspect we mentioned not from another and originated. Then more astonishing than that is that they made the world a creator while itself is created, and they made it a maker while itself is made, so the world became knowing not making for others in it, by making for others in it, then it imposed upon itself every lowly name which would be more excusable, but this is a sign of evil consequences. And every lofty name, if the name of the world were inverted.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of those who deny that the world is a proof for the existence of God. Maturidi begins by stating that the world has no inherent meaning or self-sufficiency by which it exists; therefore, it must be from something else. Those who deny this claim that the world's existence does not prove a creator, and they argue that knowledge of a thing's existence does not imply its creation. They also assert that pre-eternity does not necessitate existence. Maturidi points out that they end up making the world self-caused, which is inconsistent because the world is not from God at all in their view. He compares them to those who affirm the eternity of the world, noting that the latter are more consistent in their reasoning: if the world is not from another, it must be eternal. But these opponents make it originated while still denying a creator. Maturidi then highlights the absurdity: they make the world a creator while itself being created, a maker while itself made. Thus the world would be a knowing agent that does not make for others but is made by others. He concludes that if they were to attribute lowly names to the world it would be more excusable, but this position is a sign of evil consequences. The inversion of the name 'world' is an allusion to the fallacy.

Key Terms
دليل
قدم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world's existence does not require a creator because it can be self-caused.
Correction: Maturidi shows that self-causation leads to contradiction: if the world is independent, it should be eternal, but deniers claim it is originated.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the logical inconsistency in the deniers' position?
A) They make the world eternal while denying a creator.
B) They make the world self-caused while claiming it is originated.
C) They claim the world is not from God.
D) They deny the world as proof.

Key Takeaway
Denying the world as proof for God leads to self-contradiction: the world cannot be both self-caused and originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you subconsciously act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every breath and moment depends on the Creator? How can you today practice a conscious acknowledgment of your utter dependence on Him?
?
When you face arguments that challenge the existence of God, are you intellectually honest enough to follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it demands a change in your lifestyle? Or do you resist the conclusion because it requires accountability?
?
If the world cannot be self-caused and originated, and yet you deny God as the cause, what does that say about the consistency of your worldview? Will you let this realization unsettle you into seeking the truth, or will you remain comfortable in contradiction?

The Absurdity of Making the World Creator and Created
Maturidi exposes the absurd consequence of denying the world as proof for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp Maturidi's demonstration that denying a creator leads to the absurd conclusion that the world is both creator and created.

Core Idea
If the world creates itself, it must be both maker and made—an impossibility.

Maturidi continues his refutation by highlighting a further absurdity: the deniers' position forces them to attribute to the world the roles of creator and created simultaneously. This lesson explores that logical dead end.

Guided Path
1
Read the absurd conclusion
Read from 'ثم أعجب منه' to the end of the passage. Notice how Maturidi highlights the contradiction.
He calls it 'more astonishing'.
2
Identify the dual roles
The deniers make the world a creator and created, a maker and made. Write down these pairs.
They are contradictory.
3
Understand the inversion
Maturidi says if the world's name were inverted, every lowly name would be excusable. What does he mean by that?
It shows the absurdity.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Why does Maturidi conclude that this is a sign of evil consequences? How does it reinforce the need for a creator?

The Qur'anic Proof for Divine Unity
Maturidi cites a Qur'anic verse and draws an implication against polytheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Qur'anic verse 'If each deity had gone off with what it created' to argue for the existence of a single Creator.

Core Idea
The Qur'an itself provides a logical argument against multiple gods.

Maturidi begins by citing a verse from Surah Al-Mu'minun that presents a hypothetical scenario: if there were multiple gods, each would claim exclusive ownership over its creation. He then applies this logic to refute alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how the verse is presented as a 'meaning by which Allah argued'.
The verse is from Surah Al-Mu'minun (23:91).
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
The verse imagines what would happen if there were multiple gods: each would go off with its own creation and set a mark upon it. This implies distinction and separation.
3
Follow Maturidi's application
Maturidi applies the logic to a specific opposing view (likely the Mu'tazila), arguing that since Allah did not set a mark on His creation according to their criteria, the condition is not met, implying that Allah alone is the creator.
The argument is subtle—it uses the opponent's own standards.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this verse provides a rational proof for tawhid. The unity of creation points to a single creator.
This is a common theme in kalam: the argument from harmony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعنى الذي احتج الله به على كون العالم بكليته به إذ قال: {إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق} [سورة المؤمنون 23:91]، وليجعلن عليه علما. فعلى قولهم فالله تعالى أيضا لم يذهب بما خلق؛ لأنه لم يجعل عليه علما. وبعد، فإن الأجسام اللطيفة إذا توهم تفرقها أجزاء مما لا يتجزى، كل جزء منها امتنع ذلك عن الإدراك بالحس ليؤدي إلى العقل، وقد يتهيأ جمعها بغير.
And the meaning by which Allah argued that the world in its entirety is from Him, when He said: {If each deity had gone off with what it created} [Surah Al-Mu'minun 23:91], and would have set a mark upon it. So according to their statement, Allah, exalted, also did not go off with what He created, because He did not set a mark upon it. And further, the subtle bodies, if one imagines their division into parts that are indivisible, each part of them, that [division] is prevented from perception by sense leading to the intellect, and it is possible to gather them by other [than Him?].
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments. First, Maturidi invokes the Qur'anic verse from Surah Al-Mu'minun (23:91) which says that if there were multiple gods, each would have gone off with what it created and would have set a mark upon it. He argues that according to the Mu'tazila's view, since Allah did not set a mark on His creation, He did not go off with it, implying that the verse's condition is not met. Second, he presents a brief argument about fine bodies (ajsām laṭīfa): if one imagines them dividing into indivisible parts, that division is not perceptible by the senses but only by the intellect, and it is possible to gather them by another agent. This argument is incomplete in the text.

Key Terms
إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق
وليجعلن عليه علما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the verse is purely descriptive or eschatological.
Correction: Maturidi uses it as a logical proof, showing that the hypothetical consequence leads to the necessity of a single creator.

Checkpoint
What does the verse say would happen if there were multiple gods?
A) They would cooperate in creation.
B) Each would go off with its creation and set a mark.
C) They would destroy each other's creations.
D) They would ignore each other.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse provides a rational argument: multiple gods would lead to chaos, so the unity of creation points to one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the order and unity of the universe, does it strike you as a logical necessity that there is only one Creator? Or do you find yourself taking that for granted? How does this verse force you to move from blind acceptance to rational conviction?
?
Maturidi used the absence of distinct 'marks' on creation to argue against multiple gods. In your life, are there 'marks' of anything else claiming ultimate authority over your heart? What would it mean for you to truly let go of every false lord and submit to the One who created you without a mark?
?
This argument is not just theoretical—it demands a response. If the unity of creation points to one God, what is holding you back from fully surrendering to Him in every area of your life? What would be the first step today to act on this truth?

The Argument from Fine Bodies
Maturidi argues about the division and gathering of fine bodies as evidence for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument about the division of fine bodies and its implications for the existence of a creator.

Core Idea
The indivisibility of fine bodies points to a creator beyond sensory perception.

Maturidi presents a second argument concerning subtle bodies (ajsām latīfa). He argues that if we imagine these bodies dividing into indivisible parts, such division cannot be perceived by the senses but only by the intellect, and their gathering together is possible only by another agent. The passage is incomplete, but the direction is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fine bodies
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the text is incomplete—it cuts off after 'bighayr'.
2
Understand the concept of indivisible parts
Maturidi refers to parts that are 'mimmā lā yatajazzā'—indivisible, like atoms. He says if you imagine these bodies dividing into such parts, that division is not perceptible by the senses.
This relates to the debate on atomism in kalam.
3
Note the role of intellect
The division is known only by the intellect ('al-'aql), not by the senses. This highlights the limitations of sensory perception.
4
Consider the incompleteness
The sentence ends with 'bighayr' (by other), likely meaning 'by other than Allah'. The argument probably aimed to show that these bodies are contingent and require a cause.
This is a common pattern: contingency arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And further, the subtle bodies, if one imagines their division into parts that are indivisible, each part of them, that [division] is prevented from perception by sense leading to the intellect, and it is possible to gather them by other [than Him?].

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments. First, Maturidi invokes the Qur'anic verse from Surah Al-Mu'minun (23:91) which says that if there were multiple gods, each would have gone off with what it created and would have set a mark upon it. ?

?

?

The World's Composition and the Dualist Claim
Maturidi describes the world's composition and introduces the dualist/materialist claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi describes the contingent nature of the world's composition and how dualists exploit it to argue for multiple creators.

Core Idea
The world's composition of accidents and bodies does not prove multiple creators; it merely allows possibility.

Maturidi begins by acknowledging that the world is made of combinations, separations, changes, and stability. These phenomena are also seen in human constructions and natural motions, which could be attributed to other than God. Dualists and materialists use this to claim multiple creators. In this passage, Maturidi sets up the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the world's states and the dualist claim. Then compare with the translation.
2
Identify the key elements
Note that the world cannot exist without accidents and bodies, and these are potentially from other than God.
3
Understand the dualist position
Realize that dualists claim two creators, while proponents of natures and stars claim more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن العالم عندهم إذ لا يخلو عن اجتماع وافتراق، وزوال وقرار، وهذه أحوال احتمل كونها بغير الله، على ما يكون من اجتماع آجر السفن والبنيان، والكتابة ونحوها، وكذلك التفريق، وعلى ما كان سير الشمس والقمر وحركات الخلق وسكونهم، ولا عالم بدون وجود هذين النوعين من الأعراض والأجسام، وكان ذلك جملة يحتمل الكون بالله وبالخلق، فكان العالم جملة بعدد، وذلك قول الزنادقة باثنين، وأصحاب الطبائع والنجوم بأكثر من اثنين. وبعد، فإن الله جل ثناؤه لم يقم على قولهم حجة إحداثه سوى العالم، ولا حجة قدمه، ثم لم يفصل بين الأعراض التي هي صنع غيره وبين التي هي له، لأن الاجتماع والزوال ونحو ذلك قد يوجد في العيان ولا يرى له الجامع المحرك، ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك لغيره لا له وإن لم يعاين، إذ هو نظير ما يعاين منه، فصار ذلك كقول الثنوية وأصحاب الطبائع: تكون الأشياء بها من غير أن أقام كل منها دليل صنعه مما يفصل به من صنع غيره، وذلك آية العجز. والمعنى الذي احتج الله به على كون العالم بكليته به إذ قال: {إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق} [المؤمنون:91] يجعل عليه علما. فعلى قولهم فالله تعالى أيضا لم يذهب بما خلق؛ لأنه لم يجعل عليه علما. وبعد، فإن الأجسام اللطيفة إذا توهم تفرقها أجزاء مما لا يتجزى كل جزء منها امتنع ذلك عن الإدراك بالحس ليؤدي إلى العقل، وقد يتهيأ جمعها بغير الله، وليس لهم حجة بما يعلمون به من الله على درك الأجسام أنه منه، وقد قدر الله لطف الجواهر وكثافتها، فصارت فعل غيره في الإمكان، ولم يبين الله تعالى الخلق بدليل يتدفع الإمكان من غيره ليقرر كونه منه فيما أحسوا، فكيف فيما غيب عنهم، فصهى به من ذكرت من الثنوية وغيرهم أنه لم يجعل أحد منهم على فعله دليلا، إذ ما من شر إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك خيرا لأحد، وكذا في الجواهر من الحرارة والبرودة إلى آخر ما تنتهي إليه الطبائع، وكذلك النجوم السيارة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
After this, the world, according to them, is not free from combination and separation, change and stability. These are states that could possibly be from other than God, just as there is the joining of bricks in ships and buildings, writing and the like, and likewise separation, and as there is the movement of the sun and moon and the motions and stillness of creatures. There is no world without the existence of these two types of accidents and bodies. And this totality possibly exists by God and by creation, so the world as a whole is [subject to] number. That is the saying of the heretics (zanadiqa) of two, and the proponents of natures and stars of more than two. After this, God, exalted be His praise, did not establish for their claim a proof of His bringing into existence other than the world, nor a proof of His pre-eternity. Then He did not distinguish between the accidents that are the making of other than Him and those that are His, because combination and separation and the like may be found in plain sight and no moving assembler is seen for them. It is possible that that is from other than Him, not from Him, even if not witnessed, since it is analogous to what is witnessed from Him. So that became like the saying of the dualists and proponents of natures: that things come to be by themselves, without each one establishing a proof of its making that distinguishes it from the making of another. That is a sign of incapacity. The meaning by which God argued for the world's entirety being from Him, when He said: 'If every god had taken what he created' (Quran 23:91), making it a sign. According to their saying, God also did not take what He created because He did not make it a sign. After this, if subtle bodies are imagined to be divided into indivisible parts, each part of them is imperceptible by sense, leading to reason, and their assembly could possibly be by other than God. They have no argument by what they know of God for grasping that bodies are from Him. God determined the subtlety and density of substances, so they became possibly the act of other than Him. God did not make clear creation by a proof that repels the possibility from other than Him, to establish its being from Him in what they perceived, so how about what is hidden from them? Thus it became clear that none of those mentioned, such as the dualists and others, placed a proof upon their act. For no evil exists except that it could be good for someone. Likewise in substances from heat and cold to the end of natures, and likewise the wandering stars. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualists and materialists (those who posit two or more creators or natural causes). Maturidi begins by stating that the world consists of accidents (like combination, separation) and bodies, and these can be produced by other than God, as seen in human constructions (ships, buildings) and natural motions (sun, moon). Since both types exist, the world's totality could be attributed to God or to creation. This is the claim of dualists (two gods) and naturalists/astrologers (more than two). Maturidi then argues that God did not provide a separate proof for His creative act other than the world itself, nor did He distinguish His own accidents from those made by others. The fact that we see combinations without seeing an assembler allows the possibility that they come from other than God, similar to the dualist claim that things occur by themselves. This, says Maturidi, is a sign of incapacity. He then invokes the Quranic argument (23:91): if there were multiple gods, each would take what he created. But God did not 'take' His creation because He did not set a distinguishing sign. The subtle bodies argument reinforces this: even indivisible parts (atoms) are imperceptible, and their assembly could be by other than God, since God did not provide a clear proof preventing that possibility. Thus the dualists have no valid proof for their act. The passage concludes that all phenomena, including heat, cold, and celestial motions, are subject to this ambiguity.

Key Terms
العالم
الأعراض
الأجسام
الثنوية
الزنادقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what do dualists claim?
A) One creator
B) Two creators
C) Many creators
D) No creator

Key Takeaway
The world's composition of accidents and bodies does not prove multiple creators; it only shows possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe order and complexity in your life—like your daily routines or natural occurrences—do you ever unconsciously attribute them to mere coincidence or your own effort, forgetting that they are ultimately from Allah? What would change if you consciously reminded yourself that possibility does not imply multiple sources, and that Allah alone is the creator of all causes?
?
The passage shows that the world's states can be caused by humans (like building ships) and natural forces (like the sun's motion). Do you find yourself sometimes attributing the outcomes of your actions more to your own skill or to secondary causes than to Allah? How does this lesson challenge you to realign your reliance solely on Him?
?
Reflect on a time when you faced a complex situation and felt that multiple factors were at play. Did you recognize that Allah is the ultimate creator of all those factors? How can you train your heart to see Allah's sole agency behind every cause, so that your trust in Him becomes unshaken by apparent multiplicity?

The Quranic Argument Against Multiple Gods
Maturidi argues that God's lack of distinguishing signs refutes dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's use of Quran 23:91 to argue that if there were multiple gods, each would take what he created, but God did not set a distinguishing sign, thus dualists lack proof.

Core Idea
The lack of distinguishing signs for God's acts undermines the dualist claim.

Maturidi continues his refutation by pointing out that God did not establish a separate proof for His creative act nor distinguish His accidents from those of others. He then invokes the Quranic verse: 'If every god had taken what he created' (23:91). The implication is that dualists cannot claim God took His creation because He did not set a sign. This passage examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that discusses God's proofs and the Quranic verse. Compare with the translation.
2
Identify the logical structure
Maturidi states that God did not establish a proof for His creation other than the world itself, nor did He distinguish His acts. This leads to the Quranic argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
If dualists claim multiple creators, then each would have taken his creation. But since God did not set a sign, the dualist position fails.

?

?

?

What step can you take today to set a visible sign that your whole life is under His sole sovereignty?

The Subtle Bodies Argument and Lack of Proof
Maturidi presents the subtle bodies argument and concludes that dualists lack proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that subtle bodies (indivisible parts) are imperceptible and their assembly could be from other than God, so dualists have no proof for their claim.

Core Idea
Since God did not provide a clear proof, dualists cannot demonstrate their position.

Maturidi presents a final argument based on the nature of subtle bodies (atoms). These are imperceptible, and their assembly could potentially be by other than God. He points out that God determined the subtlety and density of substances, making them possibly the act of others. Without a distinguishing proof, the dualists cannot establish their claim. This passage concludes the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage on subtle bodies and the conclusion. Use the translation for understanding.
2
Understand the argument
Maturidi argues that even indivisible parts are imperceptible, and their combination could be from other than God. God did not provide a proof to rule out this possibility.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Thus dualists have no proof for their act. All phenomena, including heat, cold, and stars, are ambiguous.

According to Maturidi, what do dualists lack?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God did not make creation perfectly clear to the dualists, what makes you so sure that your own beliefs are not based on assumptions you've inherited? Have you ever examined the foundation of your faith, or are you relying on 'proofs' that might have the same ambiguity Maturidi points out?
?
Maturidi says no evil exists except that it could be good for someone. When you face hardship or injustice, do you truly believe that God might have a hidden goodness, or do you question His justice? How does this teaching challenge your response to evil?
?
The dualists thought they had arguments from nature, but Maturidi says God did not provide a clear proof. Are there areas in your life where you are clinging to your own reasoning rather than submitting to God's guidance? What would it look like to let go of your own 'proofs' and rely solely on God?

4 min
First Proof Against Dualism: The Argument from Power and Knowledge
Maturidi presents the first logical proof against dualism based on power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that two equal gods would necessarily be ignorant or weak, which negates their divinity.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot both be omniscient and omnipotent without conflict.

Maturidi presents the first of two classic kalam arguments against the possibility of two gods. This argument focuses on the implications of mutual power and knowledge. If two gods exist, each must be able to act independently, leading to ignorance or weakness — both incompatible with true divinity. Let us examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional: if the dualists say 'yes' (each can act independently), they admit ignorance; if they say 'no', they admit weakness.
The key terms are 'جهل' (ignorance) and 'عجز' (weakness).
2
Understand the conditional structure
The proof hinges on a dilemma: either each god can prevent the other's actions (implying they know each other's intentions) or they cannot. But both options lead to a flaw in divinity.
3
Grasp why ignorance and weakness negate lordship
Lordship (rububiyya) requires complete knowledge and power. Ignorance and weakness are direct opposites. Therefore, if the dualists admit either, they have effectively denied the gods' divinity.
Reflect on why even one god being ignorant would be unacceptable.
4
Reflect on the necessity of one God
The only way to preserve perfect knowledge and power is to affirm a single God who is omniscient and omnipotent. This logical conclusion reinforces tawhid.
This proof is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واستدل أهل التوحيد على نفي قول الثنوية بحرفين : أحدهما بقدرة كل واحد منها على أن يسد شيئا عن الآخر، ويقدر أن يفعل ما لا يعلمه الآخر، حتى إذا قالوا : نعم، جهلوها، أو أحدهما، وإن قالوا : لا، عجزوهما، والجهل والعجز يسقطان الربوبية. والثاني، أن ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، فيتناقض، فدل الوجود على كون الواحد.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: And the people of tawhid (Ahl al-Tawhid) argued for the negation of the dualists' statement with two points: one of them is by the power of each of them to prevent something from the other, and to be able to do what the other does not know. So if they say: yes, they made them ignorant, or one of them ignorant; and if they say: no, they made them weak. Ignorance and weakness negate lordship (rububiyya). And the second: that what this one wants to affirm, the other wants to negate, so they contradict. So existence indicates the existence of the One.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage presents two logical proofs from Ahl al-Tawhid (the people of divine unity) against the dualists who claim two eternal gods. The first proof: each of two equal gods would have the power to prevent something from the other and to do something unknown to the other. If the dualists admit that each can do so (say 'yes'), then they have ascribed ignorance to the gods (since one is unaware of the other's action). If they deny this ability (say 'no'), then they have ascribed weakness to them (since they cannot hinder). Both ignorance and weakness are incompatible with lordship (rububiyya), which requires omniscience and omnipotence. The second proof: if two gods exist, what one wills to bring into existence, the other may will to negate or prevent. This leads to contradiction (tanāquḍ) between their wills, which cannot coexist in a perfect being. Therefore, the actual existence of the world—being consistent and orderly—points to the oneness of the Creator (al-Wahid).

Key Terms
أهل التوحيد
الثنوية
القدرة
الجهل
العجز
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist peacefully and harmoniously without conflict.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even the potential for one god to act without the other's knowledge or to hinder the other's actions implies ignorance or weakness, which are incompatible with divinity.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if the dualists admit that each god cannot prevent the other's actions, what does that imply?
A) Both gods are ignorant.
B) Both gods are weak.
C) Both gods are equal in power.
D) Both gods have free will.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against dualism demonstrates that assuming two equal gods leads inevitably to ignorance or weakness, both of which are impossible for a true deity, thus affirming the necessity of one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but do you ever act as though there are other forces outside His control? Reflect on a moment when you feared or relied on something other than Allah. How does Maturidi's argument strip away that false reliance?
?
If you truly believed that nothing can prevent what Allah wills, would you still worry about the future? Where in your life do you functionally deny Allah's absolute power?
?
The dualists' error was dividing attributes between two beings. In what ways do you unintentionally separate Allah's attributes in your heart—for example, trusting His mercy but doubting His justice? Write one concrete change you will make today to unify your belief.

Second Proof Against Dualism: The Argument from Contradictory Wills
Maturidi presents the second logical proof against dualism based on contradictory wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that two gods would have contradictory wills, leading to contradiction in creation, which points to the one true God.

Core Idea
If two gods exist, their wills must coincide absolutely — but that is impossible.

Maturidi's second argument against dualism is even more concise yet powerful. It focuses on the wills of two gods: what one wants to bring into existence, the other may want to negate. This inevitable conflict leads to contradiction (tanāquḍ), which cannot be associated with divinity. The existence of a coherent universe therefore points to the oneness of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof
Read the Arabic text. Notice the brief but impactful statement: 'ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، فيتناقض'.
Pay attention to the word 'التناقض' (contradiction).
2
Understand the contradiction in wills
If there are two gods, each with independent will, they could will opposite things. For example, one wants to create a mountain, the other wants to prevent it. This leads to a logical and practical conflict.
Why is this inevitable? Because independent wills can disagree.
3
See how existence indicates the One
The world we see is consistent and orderly. If two conflicting wills existed, the universe would be chaotic or contradictory. The existence of a coherent universe is thus evidence for a single, unified will.
This is an argument from the observable order of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
