Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: And the people of tawhid (Ahl al-Tawhid) argued for the negation of the dualists' statement with two points: one of them is by the power of each of them to prevent something from the other, and to be able to do what the other does not know. So if they say: yes, they made them ignorant, or one of them ignorant; and if they say: no, they made them weak. Ignorance and weakness negate lordship (rububiyya). And the second: that what this one wants to affirm, the other wants to negate, so they contradict. So existence indicates the existence of the One.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage presents two logical proofs from Ahl al-Tawhid (the people of divine unity) against the dualists who claim two eternal gods. The first proof: each of two equal gods would have the power to prevent something from the other and to do something unknown to the other. If the dualists admit that each can do so (say 'yes'), then they have ascribed ignorance to the gods (since one is unaware of the other's action). If they deny this ability (say 'no'), then they have ascribed weakness to them (since they cannot hinder). Both ignorance and weakness are incompatible with lordship (rububiyya), which requires omniscience and omnipotence. The second proof: if two gods exist, what one wills to bring into existence, the other may will to negate or prevent. This leads to contradiction (tanāquḍ) between their wills, which cannot coexist in a perfect being. Therefore, the actual existence of the world—being consistent and orderly—points to the oneness of the Creator (al-Wahid).

A) There are two cooperating gods.
B) There is one God.
C) The universe is eternal.
D) There is no god.

Key Takeaway
The second proof against dualism shows that a coherent universe cannot result from conflicting divine wills, so it must be the creation of one God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that a contradiction cannot exist in reality. Where in your own life do you find a contradiction between what you claim to believe about Allah's oneness and how you actually live — for example, seeking approval from others as if they hold a competing will?
?
When you face a situation where your desires conflict with Allah's command, do you act as if there are two competing wills? What would it mean for you to submit completely to the One whose will alone is supreme, starting today?

The First Proof Against Dualism: Power and Knowledge
First of two arguments against dualism, focusing on power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by Ahl al-Tawhid to refute dualism, which demonstrates that assuming two gods leads to ignorance or incapability, negating lordship.

Core Idea
If two gods exist, each must have power over the other, implying ignorance or weakness.

Maturidi begins by presenting the first of two proofs used by the proponents of divine unity. This argument focuses on the logical implications of having two beings with equal power. If each can act independently of the other's knowledge, that implies ignorance; if not, incapability. Both qualities are incompatible with divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional reasoning.
The phrase 'حتى إذا قالوا' introduces the dualists' possible responses.
2
Identify the core logical move
The argument presents two alternatives: either each god can prevent the other's actions (leading to ignorance) or cannot (leading to incapability). Both alternatives negate lordship.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why ignorance and incapability are incompatible with being a god. This shows why dualism is rationally untenable.
The conclusion that 'ignorance and incapability negate lordship' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واستدل أهل التوحيد على نفى قول الثنوية بحرفين : أحدهما بقدرة كل واحد منها على أن يسد شيئا عن الآخر ، ويقدر أن يفعل ما لا يعلمه الآخر ، حتى إذا قالوا : نعم ، جهلوها ، أو أحدهما ، وإن قالوا : لا ، عجزوهما ، والجهل والعجز يسقطان الربوبية . والثانى ، أن ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه ، فيتناقض ، فدل الوجود على كون الواحد .
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And the people of tawhid argued against the statement of the dualists with two points: One of them is by the power of each one of them to prevent something from the other, and to be able to do what the other does not know, until if they say: 'Yes', they made them ignorant, or one of them; and if they say: 'No', they rendered them incapable. And ignorance and incapability nullify lordship. And the second: that what this one wills to affirm, the other wills to negate, so they contradict. Thus existence indicates the existence of the One.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage presents two rational proofs used by the proponents of divine unity (Ahl al-Tawhid) to refute the dualists (al-Thanawiyya). The first argument is based on the concept of power (qudra) and knowledge ('ilm). If there were two gods, each would have power over the other and could prevent the other's actions or do things unknown to the other. If the dualists admit that each can do so, they ascribe ignorance (jahl) to the god that is unaware, and if they deny it, they ascribe incapability ('ajz) to the god that cannot prevent or act. Both ignorance and incapability negate lordship (rububiyya). The second argument is based on contradiction (tanaqud): if one god wills something to exist, the other wills it not to exist, resulting in a logical contradiction. The fact that existence is coherent indicates that there is only one divine being. The argument concludes that existence itself points to the oneness of God.

Key Terms
أهل التوحيد
الثنوية
القدرة
الجهل
العجز
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is simply that two gods cannot coexist because they would fight.
Correction: The argument is a precise logical dilemma about necessary attributes (knowledge and power) and their implications for divinity, not a mere analogy of conflict.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what do ignorance and incapability do to lordship?
A) They confirm it
B) They nullify it
C) They are neutral
D) They prove dualism

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that assuming two gods leads logically to ignorance or incapability, which are incompatible with divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you have ultimate knowledge or control, ignoring that only Allah possesses these perfectly? Will you consciously submit one specific fear or plan to Allah today, acknowledging your own ignorance and incapacity?
?
The dualists' error was attributing independent power to something besides Allah. Is there any person, possession, or ambition that you treat as having independent power over your happiness or destiny? How would your heart change if you truly believed nothing shares Allah's lordship?
?
If ignorance and incapacity negate lordship, then every time you claim to know the future or rely on your own strength, you are acting like a 'little lord' over your life. What is one practical step you can take today to dethrone that false lordship and place Allah alone as your Lord?

The Second Proof Against Dualism: Contradictory Wills
Second argument against dualism: contradictory wills and the unity of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument used by Ahl al-Tawhid to refute dualism, which shows that two independent wills would lead to contradiction, and that the coherence of existence points to one God.

Core Idea
If two gods will opposite things, contradiction results; thus only one can exist.

The second proof moves from divine wills to the observable unity of existence. If there were two gods, what one wills to bring about the other could will to prevent, leading to logical contradiction. The fact that the world exists in a coherent manner indicates that there is only one divine will behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the concise structure: premise, consequence, conclusion.
The passage is very compact.
2
Analyze the logic
If two gods exist, then for any possible thing, one may will its existence and the other its non-existence, causing a contradiction. Such contradiction cannot exist in reality.
3
Connect to the cosmological argument
The argument concludes from the coherent existence of the world that there must be a single divine being. This is a form of argument from harmony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واستدل أهل التوحيد على نفى قول الثنوية بحرفين : أحدهما بقدرة كل واحد منها على أن يسد شيئا عن الآخر ، ويقدر أن يفعل ما لا يعلمه الآخر ، حتى إذا قالوا : نعم ، جهلوها ، أو أحدهما ، وإن قالوا : لا ، عجزوهما ، والجهل والعجز يسقطان الربوبية . والثانى ، أن ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه ، فيتناقض ، فدل الوجود على كون الواحد .
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And the people of tawhid argued against the statement of the dualists with two points: One of them is by the power of each one of them to prevent something from the other, and to be able to do what the other does not know, until if they say: 'Yes', they made them ignorant, or one of them; and if they say: 'No', they rendered them incapable. And ignorance and incapability nullify lordship. And the second: that what this one wills to affirm, the other wills to negate, so they contradict. Thus existence indicates the existence of the One.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage presents two rational proofs used by the proponents of divine unity (Ahl al-Tawhid) to refute the dualists (al-Thanawiyya). The first argument is based on the concept of power (qudra) and knowledge ('ilm). If there were two gods, each would have power over the other and could prevent the other's actions or do things unknown to the other. If the dualists admit that each can do so, they ascribe ignorance (jahl) to the god that is unaware, and if they deny it, they ascribe incapability ('ajz) to the god that cannot prevent or act. Both ignorance and incapability negate lordship (rububiyya). The second argument is based on contradiction (tanaqud): if one god wills something to exist, the other wills it not to exist, resulting in a logical contradiction. The fact that existence is coherent indicates that there is only one divine being. The argument concludes that existence itself points to the oneness of God.

A) That there are two gods
B) That there is one God
C) That gods can be both good and evil
D) That existence is an illusion

Key Takeaway
The second proof argues that the coherent existence of the world would be impossible under two conflicting wills, so there must be one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This argument shows that two conflicting wills cannot produce a coherent world. Your life is a small world. Are your own will and Allah's will in contradiction? Where do you need to surrender your will to align with the One?

The Argument from the World's Dependence and the Combination of Opposites
Maturidi proves a creator with knowledge and power through the world's dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the world's inherent limitations and the coexistence of opposites to prove a Creator with knowledge and power.

Core Idea
The world's neediness and contrasting elements point directly to a wise and powerful Creator.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument grounded in everyday observation. He asks us to look at the world around us and notice its fundamental dependence: everything is constrained, lacks full self-knowledge, and yet contains opposites that naturally repel each other. From this, he draws a rational conclusion about the necessity of a Creator who possesses both knowledge and power. This passage is a classic example of the kalam cosmological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the observational starting point.
The passage is a single continuous argument, so pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the observed characteristics of the world
Maturidi lists several qualities: مضطر (constrained), عاجز عن تدبير نفسه (incapable of self-management), جاهل (ignorant of its own state), and the gathering of opposites (الأضداد). List these in your mind.
These characteristics point to the world's contingency.
3
Understand the significance of opposites
Why does Maturidi emphasize the gathering of opposites (like heat and cold, wet and dry)? Because by their nature they repel each other, yet they coexist in the world. This suggests an external harmonizer.
This is a key piece of evidence that the world is not self-caused.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
From the world's dependence, reason concludes (عقل) that it did not come to be by itself. Therefore, its manager must have knowledge (علم) to account for the order and power (قدرة) to bring it about.
Maturidi says: 'عقل أن الذي دبره وقدره كان له به علم وعليه قدرة'
5
Consider the remark about coincidence
The final sentence mentions 'احتمال الاتفاق لذاته' — the possibility of coincidence or agreement. Though somewhat ambiguous, Maturidi seems to rule out chance because the evidence is coherent.
The reviewer notes ambiguity here; but the main argument stands without it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنا لما وجدنا جميع ما يعاين من العالم مضطرا عاجزا عن تدبير نفسه، جاهلا ببدء حاله وبمقدار الأخذ في كل أحوال من الزمان والمكان فيه يتقلب و به يكون مجتمعا فيه الأضداد التي هي بحق الطباع متنافرة، عقل أنه لا كان بنفسه، وعقل أن الذي دبره وقدره كان له به علم وعليه قدرة؛ إذ خرج على ولا على دلالة قوة له بنفسه، وعلم بحاله، فلا بد من تحقيق المعنى الذي في الشاهد دليله؛ إذ لا وجه لمعرفته إلا به. وكذلك لو كان ذلك بتدبير من دونه فإليه يرجع الأمر الأول، وفي ذلك كله ما ذكرنا احتمال الاتفاق لذاته،
And after that, when we found all that is perceived from the world to be constrained, incapable of managing itself, ignorant of the beginning of its state and of the measure of taking in every state of time and place in which it turns and by which it exists, and in which opposites that are by nature mutually repellent are gathered, reason understood that it did not come to be by itself, and reason understood that the one who managed it and determined it must have knowledge of it and power over it; since it came forth neither upon nor upon an indication of strength from itself, and it is known in its state, so there is no avoiding the verification of the meaning whose proof is in the witnessed [world]; for there is no way to know it except through it. Likewise, if that were by the management of someone other than Him, then the first matter returns to Him. And in all that we have mentioned is the possibility of agreement for its essence.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a kalam argument from the contingency and dependence of the world. Maturidi observes that everything we perceive in the world is compelled ( مضطر ), incapable of self-management, ignorant of its own origin and future states, and subject to the combination of opposites (like hot and cold) that are naturally repellant. Reason concludes that such a being could not have brought itself into existence; it must have a manager who has knowledge (to account for the precise design) and power (to bring it about). The argument traces the order back to a first cause. The phrase 'احتمال الاتفاق لذاته' suggests that the possibility of coincidence is ruled out because of the coherence and need, though the text is somewhat obscure. The overall proof is that the observed dependency of the world necessitates a Creator with attributes of knowledge and power.

Key Terms
مضطر
عاجز
جاهل
الأضداد
تدبير
قدرة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could have come into existence on its own, by random chance, without need for a Creator.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world's inherent limitations and the coexistence of opposites show it cannot be self-caused; therefore, a wise and powerful Creator must exist.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the reason that the world cannot be self-caused?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world is composed of opposites that are naturally repellent, yet they coexist.
C) The world exhibits random patterns.
D) The world is too vast to have a cause.

Key Takeaway
The world's dependence and the coexistence of mutually repellent opposites necessitate a Creator with knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the very world you live in is 'constrained' and 'incapable of self-management', do you still try to control every detail of your life as if you were self-sufficient? What is one area where you need to admit your helplessness and turn to Allah tonight?
?
The world contains mutually repellent opposites held together only by Allah's power. What 'opposites' in your own heart—like pride and humility, greed and contentment—are at war? Which one are you refusing to surrender to His management, and what will you do this week to let Him bring peace?
?
If the world's very existence points to Allah's perfect knowledge and power, then your existence is also a sign. What does your life today say about your recognition of that? Is there a habit or thought that denies His role as the Creator, and what concrete step will you take tomorrow to align with this truth?

The Contingency Argument for a Creator
Maturidi argues from the world's contingency to a knowing and powerful Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses observable features of the world to rationally prove the existence of a Creator with knowledge and power.

Core Idea
The world's self-evident dependency points to a wise and powerful Creator.

Maturidi presents a rational proof for God's existence and attributes, grounded in everyday experience. He observes that the world shows signs of being constrained, composed of opposites, and ignorant of its own origins. These facts lead the intellect to conclude that it must have a Creator who is knowing and powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow.
Notice the logical connectors like لما and إذ.
2
Identify the observations
List the three facts Maturidi uses: constraint, lack of self-knowledge, and presence of opposites.
3
Trace the conclusions
See how from these facts, the intellect deduces two conclusions: that the world is not self-caused and that its arranger is knowing and powerful.
4
Understand the rejection of chance
Notice how Maturidi explicitly rules out chance (ittifāq) as a possible explanation.
This is a key point against naturalism.
5
Reflect on the epistemological principle
The only way to know the Creator is through the evidence (shāhid) of the created world.
This is a foundational principle of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنا لما وجدنا جميع ما يعاين من العالم مضطرا عاجزا عن تدبير نفسه، جاهلا ببدء حاله وبمقدار الأخذ فى كل أحوال من الزمان والمكان فيه يتقلب و به يكون مجتمعا فيه الأضداد التى هى بحق الطباع متنافرة ، عقل أنه لا كان بنفسه ، وعقل أن الذي دبره وقدره كان له به علم وعليه قدرة ؛ إذ خرج على ولا على دلالة قوة له بنفسه ، وعلم بحاله ، فلا بد من تحقيق المعنى الذى في الشاهد دليله ; إذ لاوجه لمعرفته إلا به . وكذلك لو كان ذلك بتدبير من دونه فإليه يرجع الأمر الأول ، وفى ذلك كله ما ذكرنا احتمال الاتفاق لذاته .
And after that, since we find all that is perceived of the world to be constrained, incapable of governing itself, ignorant of the beginning of its state and of the measure of taking in every condition of time and place in which it changes and by which it exists, having gathered in it opposites that are by the nature of things mutually repelling, the intellect concludes that it did not come to be by itself, and the intellect concludes that the one who arranged it and determined it must have had knowledge of it and power over it; for it came forth according to [؟] and according to an indication of power in itself, and knowledge of its state. So inevitably there must be verification of the meaning whose evidence exists in the witnessed world; for there is no way to know it except through that. And likewise, if that (arrangement) were by the arrangement of something other than Him, then the first matter returns to Him. And in all that is what we mentioned of the possibility of agreement in its essence.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) for the existence of a Creator with knowledge and power, based on the observable world. Maturidi presents several empirical observations: (1) Everything we see in the world is constrained and unable to govern itself; (2) It is ignorant of its own beginning and the proper measure in all its states across time and space; (3) It contains opposites (like heat and cold, wet and dry) which naturally repel each other, yet are brought together in a single entity. From these facts, the intellect ('aql) necessarily concludes that the world did not come into existence by itself (lam yakun bi-nafsihi). It also concludes that the one who arranged it (dabbara) and determined it (qaddara) must have had knowledge of it and power over it. The reasoning is that the world's arrangement displays a pattern that indicates a powerful and knowing agent. We cannot know that agent directly except through this evidence (shāhid). Furthermore, even if someone claims that the arrangement came from an intermediary, the ultimate cause still goes back to the First Cause. Finally, Maturidi notes that all these considerations point to the impossibility of the world arising by chance (ittifāq).

Key Terms
عقل ('aql)
تدبير (tadbīr)
قدرة (qudra)
علم ('ilm)
أضداد (aḍdād)
اتفاق (ittifāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world's complexity could be the result of chance or random events.
Correction: Maturidi points out that the presence of naturally repelling opposites coexisting in a single entity necessitates an external arranger, and chance cannot produce consistent order.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the observations Maturidi uses to argue that the world did not come to be by itself?
A) The world is constrained and incapable of self-governance
B) The world contains opposites that naturally repel
C) The world is eternal and uncreated
D) The world is ignorant of its own beginning

Key Takeaway
The world's contingency, ignorance, and composition of opposites rationally prove the existence of a knowing and powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe your own life's limitations and dependencies, do you acknowledge your need for a Creator, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? How does Maturidi's argument challenge your daily assumptions?
?
The world is described as 'ignorant of its beginning' and 'incapable of governing itself'. In what areas of your life do you pretend to have full control, ignoring your dependence on Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not self-caused?
?
Maturidi says the only way to know the Creator is through the world. Are you looking at creation as a sign, or are you distracted by its apparent independence? What specific habit can you adopt to turn your attention to the signs of Allah?

Why the Question 'Why Did God Create?' Is Invalid
Maturidi reports a group's argument that the question 'why' is invalid due to God's wisdom and self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some early theologians considered the question 'Why did God create?' invalid, based on God's attributes of wisdom, knowledge, and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
If God is infinitely wise and self-sufficient, His actions cannot be questioned as if they were human actions.

Imam Maturidi begins by recording a position that the question 'Why did God create creation?' is fundamentally invalid. This argument relies on God's perfect attributes: He is wise, all-knowing, and utterly self-sufficient. For such a being, any action naturally issues from wisdom, so seeking a 'reason' outside His essence is misguided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the tone and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the Quranic citation and how it supports the argument.
2
Identify the key attributes
The argument uses three divine attributes: wisdom (الحكمة), knowledge (العلم implied in عليم), and self-sufficiency (الغنى). Note how each is used to build the case.
The phrase 'لم يزل عليم غنى' emphasizes these are eternal attributes.
3
Trace the logic of invalidity
The passage explains that actions only depart from wisdom due to ignorance or fear of losing benefit. Since God is knowing and self-sufficient, neither applies, so His actions are always wise. Therefore the question 'why' is invalid.
The Quranic verse 'He is not asked about what He does' reinforces this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
اختلاف الناس في جواب سؤال السائل : ليم خلق الله الخلق ]
الحمد لله الذي لا يغماية لما يستحق من الشكر والمحامد على ما لدينا من جزيل المان وعظيم العوائد ، وإياه نسأل التوفيق لأهدى سبل المراشد . قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : اختلف الناس في جواب سؤال السائل : ليم خلق الله الخلق ؟
قال قوم : السؤال فاسد، لا يُسأل عن ذلك ؛ إذ الله سبحانه حكيم، لم يزل عليم غنى ، فعله لا يحتمل الخروج عن الحكمة ، إذ يخرج الفعل عنها لجهل بها ، أو لما يخاف فوت نفع لو حفظ طريق الحكمة ، فإذا كان الله سبحانه عليما لا يجهل ، غنياً لا يمسه حاجة ينتفع بدفعها بطل أن يخرج فعله عن جهة الحكمة . وسؤال لم ليست فيه الحكمة ؛ ولذلك نفى الله عز وجل توهم اللعب عن فعله فقال : وَمَا خَلَقْنَا السَّمَاءَ وَالأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا لَاعِبِينَ ١٠ إلى قوله : لا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْأَلُونَ ٢ ، والحق الويل بمن يظن به الحاجة أو فى فعله السفه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Disagreement of people concerning the answer to the question of the asker: Why did Allah create creation? Praise be to Allah, Who is not [unaware?] of what is deserved of gratitude and praise for what we have of abundant bounty and great benefits, and Him we ask for success to the most guided paths of rectitude. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: People differed regarding the answer to the question of the asker: Why did Allah create creation? Some people said: The question is invalid; one should not ask about that, because Allah, exalted is He, is wise, ever-knowing, self-sufficient. His action does not admit departure from wisdom, for action departs from it only due to ignorance of it, or due to fear of losing a benefit if the way of wisdom were preserved. So since Allah is knowing, not ignorant, self-sufficient, no need touches Him that He would benefit by repelling, it is invalid that His action could depart from the way of wisdom. And the question 'why' does not contain wisdom; therefore Allah, mighty and majestic, negated the supposition of play in His action, saying: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them as players' (21:16) up to His saying: 'He is not asked about what He does, but they are asked' (21:23). And woe to the one who supposes need in Him or foolishness in His action. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage records Imam Maturidi's report of one scholarly position on the question 'Why did God create creation?' The group argues that the question itself is invalid (فاسد). Their reasoning is based on God's attributes: He is eternally wise (حكيم), knowing (عليم), and self-sufficient (غني). Actions depart from wisdom only due to ignorance or fear of losing benefit. Since God is free from these, His actions cannot but be wise. The question 'why' (لم) mistakenly assumes that God might have a reason outside His wisdom. They support their view with Quranic verses (21:16-23) where God denies creating in play and states that He is not questioned about His acts. The passage ends with a warning that anyone who attributes need or foolishness to God incurs woe.

الحكمة
الغنى
اللعب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question 'Why did God create?' is always a legitimate theological inquiry.
Correction: The text argues that if God is infinitely wise and self-sufficient, His actions need no external justification, making the question invalid.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is not required to answer.
B) Because God is wise and self-sufficient, so His actions cannot depart from wisdom.
C) Because the question is disrespectful.
D) Because creation is for our benefit only.

Key Takeaway
The question 'Why did God create?' is invalid because God's perfect attributes ensure His actions are inherently wise, needing no external explanation.

?

Why Asking 'Why' About God's Creation Is Impossible
Maturidi presents and endorses an argument that the question of 'why' about God's creation is impossible due to its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is rationally impossible to ask 'why did God create?' because it implies either an infinite regress or an eternal cause other than God.

Core Idea
Asking why about God's creation leads to an impossible choice: infinite regress or another eternal being.

This passage presents a concise but powerful argument from a group of theologians that the question 'why did God create?' is rationally impossible. The argument is based on the nature of causation and the concept of pre-eternity (al-azal). By examining the two possibilities for what the 'cause' of creation could be, the passage shows that neither is acceptable, and thus the question itself is invalid. As you read, pay attention to the logical structure of the objection and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and the literal translation. This will give you the raw material of the argument.
2
Identify the two possibilities for the cause
The passage presents a dichotomy: either the cause of creation is itself a creation, or it is not. Reflect on why each option leads to an impossible conclusion.
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice how Maturidi endorses the argument and provides the correct answer: that creation is from God's essence, not from a prior cause.
4
Connect to broader Aqeeda
Ask yourself: How does this argument protect the uniqueness and eternality of Allah and avoid associating partners with Him?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : السؤال محال ؛ لما يوجب تقدم علة لما يخلق ، والعلة إما أن يكون خلقا ، فالسؤال عنها هو السؤال عن جملة ، أو لا يكون ، فيكون غير إله فى الأزل ، بل خلق بأن فعل الخلق بذاته على ما مر بيانه ، والله الموفق .
And a group said: The question is impossible; because it necessitates a prior cause for what He creates. The cause is either a creation [itself], so the question about it is the question about the whole, or it is not [a creation], then it would be other than God in pre-eternity. Rather, He created by the fact that He performed creation by His essence, as has been explained previously. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and answer. The group argues that asking 'why did God create?' is impossible (muḥāl) because it implies a cause (ʿillah) prior to the act of creation. If that cause is itself a creation, we ask about its cause ad infinitum. If it is not a creation, then something other than God exists in pre-eternity (fī al-azal), which is impossible. Thus, the proper answer is that creation is from God's essence, as earlier explained. Maturidi endorses this with 'wa-allāhu al-muwaffiq.'

Key Terms
العلة
الأزل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can legitimately ask 'why did God create?' because everything has a reason.
Correction: Asking 'why' implies a prior cause, which would either be a created thing (leading to infinite regress) or uncreated (making it another eternal being besides Allah), both of which are impossible. Therefore, the question itself is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why is asking 'why did God create?' considered impossible according to this passage?
A) Because God is too great to be questioned.
B) Because it requires a prior cause, leading to infinite regress or an eternal cause other than God.
C) Because creation is a free act with no cause.
D) Because the question is meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The question 'why did God create?' is impossible because it logically requires either an endless chain of causes or another eternal being, both of which contradict Islamic monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage says the question of 'why' is impossible. Have you ever secretly felt that Allah's actions need to be justified to you? Will you repent from that attitude and submit to His absolute sovereignty?
?
If you truly believe that Allah creates by His essence without any external cause, how does that change your trust in His decree? What is one area of your life where you still resist His will because you want an explanation? Commit to surrendering that area to Him today.

Purpose of Creation: Testing and Worship
Maturidi presents views on why the world was created: for testing humans and for them to know God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a rational argument for the purposefulness of creation and a specific theological view that humans are created for testing and worship.

Core Idea
Creation is purposeful: humans are created to be tested and to worship.

This passage from Imam Maturidi presents a rational argument that the world was created for specific purposes, contrary to purposelessness. It then records a view that the primary purpose is the testing of humans and their worship of God. Maturidi concludes by affirming God's transcendence above all purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the rational argument for purpose and the specific claim about testing and worship.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خلق جل العالم للممتحن فيه'.
2
Identify the rational principle
The passage states that any wise agent acts for purposes. This is a key principle in Islamic thought.
3
Understand the purpose of humans
According to the view, humans are created for testing (imtiḥān) and worship (ʿibādah). Other creatures are subjugated to them.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's caution
Maturidi ends by exalting God above both aspects, reminding us not to confine God to human-derived purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : خلق العالم لعليل يكون منها وفيها وما بعدها ، وذلك هو المعقول من جميع الحكماء أنه لمقاصد يعقب الصنيع ، وكذا كل فاعل لا يعلم عواقب فعله أنه لماذا يفعله فهو غير حكيم . ثم اختلف في المعني الذي له خلق : من يقول : خلق جل العالم للممتحن فيه ؛ إذ ظهور الحكمة فيهم فيهم يظهر العلو والسلطان والجلال والرفعة ، وبهم تظهر الحكمة والسفه ، المقصودون من الخلق ، وغيرهم من الخلائق خلقوا لهم ، لمنافع لهم ، وللامتحان بها ، وللدلالة ، وسخروا لهم ، والممتحنون خلقوا للعبادة ، أو لأنفسهم ليسعوا العواقب يحمدون عليها ويذمون ، إليهم يقع ذلك ، وضرورة جل خالقهم عن الوجهين؛
And a group said: The world was created for causes (ʿilal) that are from it, in it, and after it. And that is what is rationally understood from all the wise—that [acts] are for purposes that follow the action. Likewise, any agent who does not know the consequences of his action regarding why he does it is not wise. Then they differed about the meaning for which He created: Some say: He, majestic, created the world for the one being tested therein; for the manifestation of wisdom in them—in them appears exaltation, sovereignty, majesty, and loftiness, and by them wisdom and foolishness appear. They are the intended ones of creation. Other creatures are created for them, for their benefits, for testing by them, and for indication, and are subjugated to them. The tested ones are created for worship, or for themselves to pursue consequences for which they are praised or blamed. To them that belongs. And the majesty of their Creator is far above both aspects.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage presents a view that the world was created for purposes (ʿilal) that are part of the creation, in it, and after it. This is rational—any wise agent acts for ends. Then it records a difference of opinion: some say the world is created for the human being who is tested (mumtaḥan), because through humans wisdom and foolishness appear, and they are the intended (maqṣūdūn). Other creatures are created for them, subjugated for their benefit and testing. Humans themselves are created for worship (ʿibādah) or for themselves to pursue consequences. Maturidi ends by exalting God above both aspects—meaning that God's essence is far above being limited by such purposes, perhaps signaling a cautionary note.

Key Terms
العليل
الممتحن
العبادة
المقصودون
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation is purposeless or random.
Correction: The passage argues that a wise agent always acts for purposes; God is all-wise, so creation has a purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the view presented, what is the primary purpose of humans in creation?
A) To enjoy the world.
B) To be tested and to worship.
C) To rule over other creatures.
D) To multiply and fill the earth.

Key Takeaway
The world was created for a purpose: humans are tested and called to worship God, while God remains transcendent above all purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Now that you know you were created for worship or to pursue consequences yourself, which one governs your life? Examine your daily priorities—do they reflect the purpose of worship, or have you made yourself the ultimate end? Commit to one specific change today that aligns your actions with worship.
?
Other creatures have been subjugated for your benefit, yet you will be held accountable for how you treat them. When you use resources, consume food, or interact with nature, do you see them as signs and trusts from God? What thoughtless habit can you repent from right now to honor this trust?
?
Maturidi ends by exalting God above any purpose we attribute to His actions. Have you ever imagined that God needs your worship or benefits from your obedience? Humble yourself: your worship is for your own good, not God's. How will you cultivate a sense of awe that God is utterly self-sufficient, and still choose to worship Him out of love?

The Dualist and Jewish Misinterpretations
Maturidi presents the Dualist and Jewish misinterpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Dualist and Jewish interpretations of the saying 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord' and why Maturidi considers them flawed.

Core Idea
The saying is twisted by Dualists and Jews to support polytheistic or anthropomorphic views.

The saying 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord' is universally accepted, but its meaning is disputed. Maturidi begins by examining two erroneous interpretations: the Dualists see the self's mix of good and evil as evidence of two gods, while a corrupted Jewish view reduces God to a single part. These set the stage for the correct Sunni understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory statement
Notice that all groups agree on the saying but differ on the 'mode of knowledge' (wajh al-ma'rifa). This frames the discussion as a hermeneutical debate.
2
Understand the Dualist argument
The Dualists (al-thunawiyya) argue that because the self contains both good and evil, it follows that there must be two lords, one for each aspect.
This is a classic dualist position rejecting tawhid.
3
Examine the Jewish interpretation
The text says 'the Jews made Him one part' – likely meaning they limited God to a single component, possibly implying a physical or limited conception. Note that this section is textually corrupted.
OCR issues make this reconstruction tentative.
4
Reflect on the insufficiency
Ask yourself: do these interpretations truly arise from knowing oneself, or do they impose preconceived beliefs? Maturidi will later offer a more profound reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في التوحيد: [من عرف نفسه عرف ربه] فإن قال قائل: أجمع أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه، لكنهم اختلفوا في وجه المعرفة. فقالت الثنوية: لما عرف اشتمال نفسه على الخير والشر عرف أن لكل جهة منه رباً. واليهود صيروه واحداً جزءاً. وقالت المشبهة: هو جسم؛ إذ في الشاهد تكون معرفة النفس للجسم. وقال جهم: إذ عرف أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن، وهو شيء، [ليس بجسم]، عالم سميع بصير، علم أن كل ما كان له ذلك الأسم فهو حدث، وربه الذي أنشأه لا يحتمل أن يكون حدثا. وعندنا: أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل مما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك بإصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذه هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه، ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها، فهذا شأنه فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز، علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليه، بل لو كان الأمر إليه لديرها على ما جميع ذلك، إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيما أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها، فيعلم عند ذلك، إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيراً فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكاً لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفاً على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور، فيعلم.
A question concerning tawhid: [Whoever knows himself knows his Lord.] For if a questioner says: It is agreed that whoever knows himself knows his Lord, but they differ concerning the mode of knowledge. The Dualists said: When he knew that his self comprises good and evil, he knew that for each direction there is a Lord. The Jews made Him one part. The Anthropomorphists said: He is a body, because in the witnessed world, knowledge of the self is of a body. And Jahm said: When he knew that he was after not having been, and he is a thing, [not a body], knowing, hearing, seeing, he knew that everything that has that name is originated, and his Lord who created him cannot be originated. And according to us: Whoever knows himself knows his Lord; because he knows himself through the ignorance of what it has taken on of hearing, sight, and other accidents, and likewise through repairing what has become corrupted in it, and by the measure of what it takes of time and place, and by the kinds of needs that come upon it whose source he does not know, along with witnessing its passing away from what he witnesses of himself, and the reality of that by which it passes away. So this is its state; then his knowledge of what has passed of its states from the beginning of its existence to the state in which it is, along with knowledge of what changes upon it of states up to the time of its cessation from him is farther, and the conceptualization of that in his mind is more difficult, and the possibility of his intellect encompassing it is more incapable. He knows by necessity that he did not manage his own affairs as they are; rather, if the matter were in his hands, he would have managed it in all that. For if there were any power over any of that, he would not have fallen into the ignorance that is established, then into the incapacity regarding what [I informed] of pushing away needs from himself and repairing what has become corrupted in him. So he knows at that point, since he is the most capable of creatures in managing what he perceives, and the highest of them in grasping realities of what he encounters, and the quickest of them in understanding what he knows and remembers of matters, then he knows.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion (mas'ala fi al-tawhid) analyzing the well-known saying 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord.' Imam Maturidi begins by noting that all parties agree on the saying but differ on its interpretation. He then presents four views: the Dualists (al-thunawiyya) claim that knowledge of oneself as composed of good and evil leads to knowledge of two lords; the Jews make God a single part (some reference to a particular Jewish conception); the Anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) argue that since the self is known as a body, God must also be a body; and Jahm [ibn Safwan] holds that recognizing oneself as a contingent being (something created after non-existence) leads to knowing that God cannot be originated. Maturidi then presents the position of Ahl al-Sunna (indicated by 'according to us' – 'andana'). The Maturidi interpretation focuses on the self's limitations: one knows oneself through ignorance (jahl) of the true nature of one's own faculties like hearing and sight (which are accidents), and through the need to repair what corrupts. The self is dependent on time, place, and unknown sources of its needs. Moreover, one witnesses the passing of states without knowing the true cause of that passing. The more one contemplates the history of the self from beginning to end, the more one realizes one's incapacity to fully comprehend it. This leads to the necessary conclusion that the self is not self-governing; if it had any power, it would not have fallen into ignorance and incapacity. Thus, by knowing one's own weakness and dependence, one knows the perfection and power of the Lord who created and sustains it.

Key Terms
الثنوية
اليهود
المعرفة
من عرف نفسه عرف ربه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the saying means self-knowledge automatically equals knowledge of God, regardless of method.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the method matters; the Dualists and Jews claim to know themselves but reach false conclusions about God.

Checkpoint
What do the Dualists claim about the composition of the self?
A) That it is entirely good
B) That it comprises both good and evil
C) That it is a body
D) That it is originated after non-existence

Key Takeaway
The Dualist and Jewish interpretations misuse the saying to support polytheism and anthropomorphism, failing to arrive at true tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the good and evil within yourself, do you ever secretly imagine two opposing forces—as though God is not entirely in control of both? How does this lesson expose that subtle dualism in your heart? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself of Allah's sole sovereignty today.
?
The Jews 'made Him one part'—in what areas of your life have you limited God to a small compartment (e.g., only in rituals, only in certain times) rather than seeing Him as the all-encompassing reality? What will you do tomorrow to break that confinement?
?
Which of these two misinterpretations—Dualism or reductionism—do you find yourself falling into more often when you try to understand your own soul? What step will you take to replace that error with true tawhid in your daily thoughts?

The Anthropomorphist and Jahm Interpretations
Maturidi presents the Anthropomorphist and Jahm interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Anthropomorphist and Jahm interpretations of the saying and see how both err by focusing on isolated aspects of the self.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists and Jahm both reduce God to a concept derived from the self, missing the true argument from dependence.

Maturidi continues surveying misinterpretations. The Anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) argue that since the self is known as a body, so too must God be a body. Jahm ibn Safwan, on the other hand, focuses on the self's contingency to conclude that God is unoriginated. Both are partial truths that fail to grasp the full import of the saying.

Guided Path
1
Read the Anthropomorphist argument
The Anthropomorphists claim that in the witnessed world (fi al-shahid), knowledge of oneself is knowledge of a body; therefore, God must be a body.
This is a clear analogy-based reasoning (tashbih).
2
Read Jahm's argument
Jahm argues that knowing oneself as a contingent being (originated after non-existence) leads to knowing that God cannot be originated. He lists divine attributes (knowing, hearing, seeing) for the self and then infers that anything with those names is created, but God is not.
The text is corrupted; we have inserted 'not a body' to preserve Jahm's known position.
3
Identify the common flaw
Both groups focus on a single aspect of the self (bodyhood or contingency) and derive a conclusion about God without considering the self's full dependence and limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في التوحيد: [من عرف نفسه عرف ربه] فإن قال قائل: أجمع أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه، لكنهم اختلفوا في وجه المعرفة. فقالت الثنوية: لما عرف اشتمال نفسه على الخير والشر عرف أن لكل جهة منه رباً. واليهود صيروه واحداً جزءاً. وقالت المشبهة: هو جسم؛ إذ في الشاهد تكون معرفة النفس للجسم. وقال جهم: إذ عرف أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن، وهو شيء، [ليس بجسم]، عالم سميع بصير، علم أن كل ما كان له ذلك الأسم فهو حدث، وربه الذي أنشأه لا يحتمل أن يكون حدثا. وعندنا: أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل مما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك بإصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذه هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه، ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها، فهذا شأنه فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز، علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليه، بل لو كان الأمر إليه لديرها على ما جميع ذلك، إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيما أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها، فيعلم عند ذلك، إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيراً فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكاً لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفاً على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور، فيعلم.
A question concerning tawhid: [Whoever knows himself knows his Lord.] For if a questioner says: It is agreed that whoever knows himself knows his Lord, but they differ concerning the mode of knowledge. The Dualists said: When he knew that his self comprises good and evil, he knew that for each direction there is a Lord. The Jews made Him one part. The Anthropomorphists said: He is a body, because in the witnessed world, knowledge of the self is of a body. And Jahm said: When he knew that he was after not having been, and he is a thing, [not a body], knowing, hearing, seeing, he knew that everything that has that name is originated, and his Lord who created him cannot be originated. ?

?
Jahm uses a logical argument from the self, but Maturidi says it's still insufficient. Are you satisfied with intellectual arguments alone, or do you seek a deeper dependence on God that goes beyond logic? What will change in your search for God after this lesson?

Allah's Existence
advanced
10 min
The Maturidi Argument: From Human Limitation to Divine Perfection
Maturidi presents his own detailed argument from self-limitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that knowing one's own ignorance, dependence, and incapacity leads to the necessary knowledge of a perfect Creator.

Core Idea
Our very weakness and ignorance prove our total dependence on a perfect, self-sufficient God.

After rejecting flawed interpretations, Maturidi presents the Sunni position. He argues that true self-knowledge reveals profound limitations: we are ignorant of our own faculties, unable to repair our own corruption, bound by time and place, and incapable of comprehending the full sweep of our own existence. This inescapable dependence forces the rational mind to conclude that we are not self-governing—and therefore must have a Lord who is all-powerful and all-knowing.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the argument
Maturidi lists ways we know our own ignorance: of the true nature of our hearing and sight (accidents), of how to repair what corrupts, and of the source of our needs.
This establishes the self's lack of self-knowledge.
2
Note the temporal and spatial limits
The self is measured by time and place, and it needs what it does not control. Even witnessing its own passing away, it does not know the reality of that passing.
3
Understand the cumulative reasoning
The more one reflects on the self's history from beginning to end, the more impossible it becomes to fully grasp. This reveals the self's incapacity to manage itself.
Maturidi uses the superlative: 'farther', 'more difficult', 'more incapable'.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the self cannot manage its own affairs—if it had any power, it would avoid ignorance and incapacity—it must be governed by another. This 'other' is God.
The argument is a posteriori: from effect to cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: Some think 'knowing oneself' means introspective awareness of one's own existence, leading to a vague sense of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you were utterly helpless—unable to fix a mistake, overcome a weakness, or change a situation. Did you pause to consider that this very weakness points to a Lord who is never helpless? How can you train yourself to turn such moments into worship, rather than despair?
?
You know that you will die, yet you live as if you will be here forever. How does the Maturidi argument—that you cannot even manage your own passing states—challenge your daily priorities? What is one concrete change you will make this week to live in awareness of your dependence on Allah?
?
If you truly believe that your ignorance and incapacity prove Allah's perfection, then why do you still act arrogantly or independently in your decisions? What would it cost you to surrender that sense of control and rely fully on Him?

Allah's Existence

5 min
Self-Knowledge Reveals Our Limitations
Maturidi introduces the proof from self-knowledge, listing human limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reflecting on our own ignorance, need, and limited control leads to the recognition that we are not the true managers of ourselves.

Core Idea
Our own limitations are the first proof that we are not self-sufficient.

This lesson explores the beginning of Maturidi's famous argument from self-knowledge. By examining what we observe about ourselves—our ignorance of many things, our dependence on senses, our inability to fully control our bodies—we come to a crucial realization: we did not manage our own existence. This 'negative' knowledge paves the way for knowing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
List the observations about human limitations
The passage lists several things we know about ourselves: ignorance, dependence on senses, need for repair, limited control over time and space, and unknown needs. Write them down.
3
Identify the conclusion
After these observations, Maturidi concludes that 'he knows by necessity that he did not manage his own affairs.' Find that statement in the text.
This is the key turning point.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'knows himself knows his Lord'
Consider how recognizing our own limitations leads to knowledge of a manager beyond ourselves. How does this connect to knowing the Lord?
This links to the overall proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا: أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل مما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك باصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذا هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه، ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها، فهذا شأنه فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز، علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليها، بل لو كان الأمر إليه لديرها على ما جميع ذلك، إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيما أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها، فيعلم عند ذلك، إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيرا فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكا لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفا على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور، فيعلم خروجه من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين، تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه، ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من والوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعانى التى عليها نفسه، وفيها تقلبها، فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره، فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يشبهه شيء من ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذى يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه، وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والصنف، ثم فى الحدثية من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات، والجهات له لا المدبره، فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And with us: whoever knows himself knows his Lord; for he knows himself as ignorant of what he has of hearing, sight, and other accidents, and likewise by the repair of what is corrupted in him, and by the extent to which he takes from time and place, and by the kinds of needs that come upon him whose origin he does not know, along with what witnesses their removal from himself, and the true nature of that by which they are removed. So this is his state; his knowledge of what has passed of his states from the first of his existence to the present state, along with knowledge of what varies upon him of states until the time of their arising from him, is more remote, and more difficult to imagine in his imagination, and more incapable for his intellect to encompass. He knows by necessity that he did not manage his own affairs as they are; rather, if the matter were up to him, he would have managed them in the best way. For if there were any power over any of that, he would not have been driven to the ignorance that is established, then to the incapacity in what he has reported of repelling needs from himself and repairing what is corrupted from him. He knows then, since he is the most capable of creatures in management of what he senses, and the highest in perception of the realities he encounters, and the quickest in stopping at what he knows and remembers of matters, he knows his deprivation of managing his own soul in origination, annihilation, and preservation, and then of bringing forth all the sensory things, being under his management like those bewildered in their needs. And he knows that his like, upon what he is of stopping at matters and perceiving causes, is not but from one who is outside all the meanings that his soul is upon and in which it fluctuates. So he knows that He is powerful without incapacity, knowing without ignorance, compelling with no opposition to His management. He knows that He, may He be exalted, nothing resembles Him in any thing or meaning; for from the aspect that resembles Him would entail what is entailed of origination or pre-eternity, or management other than His. And similarly all things; for between them is agreement in needs and types of incapacity and category, then in origination from every aspect. So by this it is necessary that he know that He is different from him in every respect, and respects are for Him, not the thing managed. So in that is the definition of the Lord as He deserves. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Maturidi explains the correct understanding of the saying 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord'. He begins by noting what a person knows about himself: his ignorance, his dependence on senses, his need for repair of his body, his limited control over time and space, and his unknown needs and their removal. From these, one realizes that one did not manage one's own existence; if one had power, one would have avoided ignorance and weakness. Since humans are the most capable creatures in managing themselves yet still fail, they know they are not the true manager. Therefore, the true manager must be outside all the characteristics of the self—powerful without weakness, knowing without ignorance, compelling without opposition. Consequently, God is unlike anything in attribute or meaning; any resemblance would imply origination or change. Thus, one knows that God is completely different from creation. This is the true knowledge of the Lord.

Key Terms
العجز
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'knowing oneself' means psychological introspection or self-improvement.
Correction: Maturidi means recognising one's contingency, ignorance, and dependence on a creator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does one's lack of perfect management of oneself prove?
A) That one is free and can improve.
B) That one did not create or manage oneself.
C) That one should try harder to manage life.
D) That one is similar to God in some ways.

Key Takeaway
Reflecting on our own ignorance and dependence reveals that we did not create or manage ourselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you faced a problem or need that you could not solve on your own, yet it was resolved in a way you did not expect. How does that experience make the words 'he knows by necessity that he did not manage his own affairs' feel real to you right now?
?
The passage claims that if you were in control, you would have arranged everything perfectly. Look at an area of your life where you have struggled or failed despite your best efforts. What does that failure reveal about who truly holds your affairs?
?
You know yourself better than anyone else, yet the passage says you are ignorant of the true nature of your own hearing and sight. Does that make you uncomfortable? Will you let that unease drive you to seek the One who knows all, or will you ignore it and remain content with your ignorance?

From Human Incapacity to Divine Transcendence
Maturidi deduces God's attributes and incomparability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi deduces the attributes of God (power, knowledge, incomparability) from the fact that humans are not the managers of themselves.

Core Idea
The true manager must be powerful, knowing, and utterly unlike His creation.

Building on the realization that we are not the managers of our own existence, Maturidi now draws positive conclusions about the one who is the true manager. Since we, the most capable of creatures, still fail to manage ourselves, the one who manages us must be beyond all our limitations. This leads to a set of divine attributes and the absolute transcendence of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half of the passage
Read from 'إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة' to the end. Follow the logical progression from incapacity to divine attributes.
This part builds directly on the conclusion of the first half.
2
Identify the deduced attributes
Maturidi explicitly states that God is 'powerful without incapacity, knowing without ignorance, compelling with no opposition.' Find these in the text.
3
Understand why God must be unlike anything
The passage explains that any resemblance would imply origination or change. Reflect on this reasoning.
This establishes tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا: أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل مما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك باصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذا هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه، ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها، فهذا شأنه فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز، علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليها، بل لو كان الأمر إليه لديرها على ما جميع ذلك، إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيما أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها، فيعلم عند ذلك، إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيرا فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكا لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفا على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور، فيعلم خروجه من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين، تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه، ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من والوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعانى التى عليها نفسه، وفيها تقلبها، فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره، فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يشبهه شيء من ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذى يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه، وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والصنف، ثم فى الحدثية من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات، والجهات له لا المدبره، فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله، ولا قوة إلا بالله

How will you actively submit to His transcendence today?

Knowing God Through Human Limitation
Maturidi argues from human contingency to God's perfect attributes and absolute difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reflecting on your own contingency leads to knowing that God is utterly different from all creation and possesses perfect attributes.

Core Idea
Your own helplessness points to a Helper beyond all limitation.

Imam Maturidi begins by asking you to look inward at your own existence—how you did not create yourself, cannot sustain yourself, and are surrounded by things you cannot control. From this honest observation, he builds a rational path to knowing the Creator. The key move is contrast: recognizing what you are not shows you what God must be.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear the imam's rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice how he moves from human experience to divine attributes.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the starting point: human helplessness
The argument begins with your inability to manage your own creation, annihilation, and preservation. Also note how all perceptible things seem to be under a management beyond your control.
3
Understand the leap to God's attributes
Because you are limited in knowledge and power, the one who manages you must be unlimited: powerful without incapacity, knowing without ignorance, overwhelming without contest.
This is a classic kalam argument from contrast.
4
Grasp the conclusion: total dissimilarity
If anything resembled God, it would share in contingency or pre-eternity. Since all creatures are alike in need and contingency, God must be different in every respect.
This refutes any anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ بسم الاحتمال خروجه من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه، ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من الوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعاني التي عليها نفسه وفيها تقلبها، فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره، فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يشبهه شيء من شيء ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذي يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه، وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والصنف، ثم في الحدثية من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات، والجهات له لا المدبره، فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وعلى هذا يبطل قول جهم: إنه لم يكن عالما قادرا ثم صار كذلك، وقول من يقول: لم يكن فاعلا متكلما ثم صار كذلك؛ إذ مكنوا فيه تغير الجهات والأحوال التي هي سبب معرفة العبد نفسه خلقا وحدثا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبما ذكرت من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختيارا، واحتماله الصفات العليه من نحو العلم والقدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر ما يوضح كونه بالحمانع العليم، لا بالطبائع التي هي عاجزة عن الاختيار وجاهلة بالأحوال، وكذلك جميع الأغذية، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وكذلك باحتماله الخير والشر ومختلف الأحوال دليل صرف تدبيره إلى من لا يوصف بالاحتمال ولا بمختلف الأحوال، ليكون كل شيء على ما عليه تقديره، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
For indeed, due to the possibility of his emergence from his own management in creating, annihilating, and preserving, and then from the manifestation of all sensible things under His management like those bewildered in their needs, he knows that one like himself, in his state of stopping at matters and perceiving causes, can only be from one who is outside all the meanings upon which his own self is and in which it fluctuates. So he knows that He is powerful, not incapable, knowing, not ignorant, overwhelming, not contested in His management. So he knows that He, glorified and exalted, nothing resembles Him, neither a thing nor a meaning; for from the aspect that resembles Him would necessitate what is necessitated in it of contingency or pre-eternity, or management other than His; and similarly all things, for among them there is agreement in needs, types of incapacity and kind, then in contingency from every aspect. So by this it is necessary to know that He is different from him in all respects, and the respects are for Him, not for the managed one. So in that is a definition of the Lord as is worthy of Him, and there is no power except through God. And upon this is invalidated the saying of Jahm: that He was not knowing and powerful then became so, and the saying of whoever says: He was not an agent and speaker then became so; for they affirmed change of aspects and states in Him, which are the cause of the servant's knowledge of himself as created and contingent, and there is no power except through God. And by what I mentioned of the possibility of receiving states by choice, and his assumption of high attributes such as knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, it clarifies His being the Wise and Knowing [possibly: the Forbearing and Knowing], not by natures which are incapable of choice and ignorant of states, and similarly all necessities? [literally 'all foods'?], and there is no power except through God. And similarly, by his assumption of good and evil and varying states, it is a proof for diverting his management to One who is not described by assumption nor by varying states, so that everything may be as His decree requires, and there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument for knowing God through reflection on human contingency and limitation. It opens by describing how a person, by observing his own inability to manage his existence—his coming into being, passing away, and preservation—and by seeing the world of perceptible things under a control he cannot grasp, realizes that his own kind, which stops at causes and depends on them, must have a creator who is free from all the limitations that characterize himself. From this contrast, he infers that God is powerful without incapacity, knowing without ignorance, and overwhelming without contest. The argument then extends: if anything resembled God, that thing would share in either contingency or pre-eternity, which is impossible. All created things are alike in needing, being incapable, and being contingent; therefore God must be utterly different from them in every respect. This method defines God as He deserves. The author then uses this same principle to refute two erroneous positions: the claim of Jahm that God was not knowing and powerful but later became so, and the claim that God was not an agent or speaker but later became so. Both errors posit change in God's aspects and states, which are actually the very markers of creaturely contingency. Next, the author notes that a person's capacity to assume high attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) and to experience varying states (good, evil, choice) points away from inanimate natures and toward a Maker who is wise and knowing, not like blind, necessitated natures. Finally, the very fact that humans undergo change and possibility shows that their management must be diverted to a being who is above change and variation, so that all things proceed according to His decree.

Key Terms
الاحتمال
التدبير
الحدث
القدم
الجبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because humans have some power and knowledge, God's attributes are just greater versions of human attributes.
Correction: The passage argues that God is not just greater but utterly different: He is outside all the meanings that define human attributes, and any resemblance would force Him into the categories of contingency or pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
What is the starting point of Maturidi's argument for knowing God?
A) Revelation from prophets
B) Human inability to manage his own existence
C) The beauty of nature
D) The existence of evil

Key Takeaway
Reflecting on your own contingency reveals that God is absolutely different from all creation and possesses perfect attributes without limitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt completely helpless, unable to manage your own life? Did that moment point you to a Power beyond you, or did it crush you? How will you respond the next time you face such a moment?
?
Do you secretly imagine God as a larger, stronger version of a human? What would it mean to truly believe that He is unlike anything you have ever known or felt? Are you willing to let go of your comfortable image of God?
?
The passage ends with 'there is no power except through God.' Do you live as if that is true, or do you still try to manage your own affairs? What one area of your life do you need to hand over to Him completely?
