Refuting Change in God: The Argument from Contingency
Maturidi refutes Jahm and others who claim change in God using the argument from contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that God acquires new attributes (like knowledge or power over time) is false, because it would imply change, which is a mark of contingency.

Core Idea
Change belongs to creatures, not to the Creator.

After establishing that God is utterly different from creation, Imam Maturidi turns to refute a specific error: the belief that God was not always knowing, powerful, or active but later became so. He will show that such a claim actually forces God into the very creaturely categories we just used to deny. The same principle—that change and possibility are signs of contingency—serves as the backbone of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the second half of the source. Notice how Maturidi ties this refutation directly to the previous argument.
The phrase 'upon this is invalidated' shows connection.
2
Identify the two erroneous claims
Jahm claimed God became knowing and powerful after not being so; others claimed He became an agent and speaker after not being so. Both imply change.
3
Understand why change is impossible for God
Change is a property of contingent beings—it comes from the same category as 'states and aspects' that define creatures. If God changed, He would be like creatures.
4
See the positive argument from human possibility
The fact that humans can assume attributes (knowledge, power) and experience varying states (good, evil) proves that they are not self-sufficient, and their management must belong to a being beyond change and possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ بسم الاحتمال خروجه من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه، ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من الوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعاني التي عليها نفسه وفيها تقلبها، فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره، فيعرف أنه جل وعلا لا يشبهه شيء من شيء ولا معنى؛ إذ من الوجه الذي يشبهه يوجب ما أوجب فيه من حدث أو قدم، أو تدبير غير فيه، وكذلك جميع الأشياء؛ إذ بينها موافقة في الحاجات وأنواع العجز والصنف، ثم في الحدثية من كل الوجوه. فيجب بهذا أن يعرف أنه خلاف له بكل الجهات، والجهات له لا المدبره، فيكون في ذلك تعريف الرب بما هو أهله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وعلى هذا يبطل قول جهم: إنه لم يكن عالما قادرا ثم صار كذلك، وقول من يقول: لم يكن فاعلا متكلما ثم صار كذلك؛ إذ مكنوا فيه تغير الجهات والأحوال التي هي سبب معرفة العبد نفسه خلقا وحدثا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبما ذكرت من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختيارا، واحتماله الصفات العليه من نحو العلم والقدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر ما يوضح كونه بالحمانع العليم، لا بالطبائع التي هي عاجزة عن الاختيار وجاهلة بالأحوال، وكذلك جميع الأغذية، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
وكذلك باحتماله الخير والشر ومختلف الأحوال دليل صرف تدبيره إلى من لا يوصف بالاحتمال ولا بمختلف الأحوال، ليكون كل شيء على ما عليه تقديره، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
For indeed, due to the possibility of his emergence from his own management in creating, annihilating, and preserving, and then from the manifestation of all sensible things under His management like those bewildered in their needs, he knows that one like himself, in his state of stopping at matters and perceiving causes, can only be from one who is outside all the meanings upon which his own self is and in which it fluctuates. So he knows that He is powerful, not incapable, knowing, not ignorant, overwhelming, not contested in His management. So he knows that He, glorified and exalted, nothing resembles Him, neither a thing nor a meaning; for from the aspect that resembles Him would necessitate what is necessitated in it of contingency or pre-eternity, or management other than His; and similarly all things, for among them there is agreement in needs, types of incapacity and kind, then in contingency from every aspect. So by this it is necessary to know that He is different from him in all respects, and the respects are for Him, not for the managed one. So in that is a definition of the Lord as is worthy of Him, and there is no power except through God. And upon this is invalidated the saying of Jahm: that He was not knowing and powerful then became so, and the saying of whoever says: He was not an agent and speaker then became so; for they affirmed change of aspects and states in Him, which are the cause of the servant's knowledge of himself as created and contingent, and there is no power except through God. And by what I mentioned of the possibility of receiving states by choice, and his assumption of high attributes such as knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, it clarifies His being the Wise and Knowing [possibly: the Forbearing and Knowing], not by natures which are incapable of choice and ignorant of states, and similarly all necessities? [literally 'all foods'?], and there is no power except through God. And similarly, by his assumption of good and evil and varying states, it is a proof for diverting his management to One who is not described by assumption nor by varying states, so that everything may be as His decree requires, and there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument for knowing God through reflection on human contingency and limitation. It opens by describing how a person, by observing his own inability to manage his existence—his coming into being, passing away, and preservation—and by seeing the world of perceptible things under a control he cannot grasp, realizes that his own kind, which stops at causes and depends on them, must have a creator who is free from all the limitations that characterize himself. From this contrast, he infers that God is powerful without incapacity, knowing without ignorance, and overwhelming without contest. The argument then extends: if anything resembled God, that thing would share in either contingency or pre-eternity, which is impossible. All created things are alike in needing, being incapable, and being contingent; therefore God must be utterly different from them in every respect. This method defines God as He deserves. The author then uses this same principle to refute two erroneous positions: the claim of Jahm that God was not knowing and powerful but later became so, and the claim that God was not an agent or speaker but later became so. Both errors posit change in God's aspects and states, which are actually the very markers of creaturely contingency. Next, the author notes that a person's capacity to assume high attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) and to experience varying states (good, evil, choice) points away from inanimate natures and toward a Maker who is wise and knowing, not like blind, necessitated natures. Finally, the very fact that humans undergo change and possibility shows that their management must be diverted to a being who is above change and variation, so that all things proceed according to His decree.

Key Terms
جهم
الجهات
الأحوال
الاختيار
الطبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge and power can increase or change over time, like human knowledge.
Correction: The passage argues that any change in God would make Him contingent, like creatures. God's attributes are eternal and unchanging.

Checkpoint
What is the central reason for refuting Jahm's claim that God became knowing and powerful?
A) It contradicts the Quran
B) It implies change in God, which is a mark of contingency
C) It denies God's mercy
D) It cannot be rationally proven

Key Takeaway
Claiming that God changes over time contradicts His necessity and uniqueness; change is the hallmark of contingent creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the changes in your own life—your growth in knowledge, your shifting desires, your mistakes—do you see them as signs of your contingency? How does recognizing that change belongs to you alone deepen your conviction that God is utterly unlike you and does not change?
?
The passage teaches that your experience of good and evil is proof that your ultimate management belongs to God. Is there an area of your life where you are still trying to be the sole manager—holding onto control, anxious about outcomes? What would it actually mean for you to truly divert that management to Him today?
?
If God's attributes are eternal and unchanging, how should that transform the way you approach Him in prayer? Do you sometimes pray as if you need to inform God or persuade Him to change His mind? What concrete shift can you make in your next prayer to align with the truth that His attributes are not subject to change?

Third Wisdom: Diverse Substances Point to One Wise Creator
Maturidi presents the argument from diversity of substances to the unity and wisdom of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the diversity of substances to argue for the oneness and wisdom of the Creator.

Core Idea
Diversity of creation points to unity of the Creator.

In this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam al-Maturidi presents a profound wisdom: although creatures differ in their substances and in the harms and benefits they bring, they all consistently point to one wise Manager. This argument from diversity reinforces the concept of tawhid, showing that variety in creation does not imply multiple creators but rather one wise, knowing Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the key terms like الجوهر (substance), الدلالة (indication), and المدير (manager).
The passage begins with a possible heading about worship and morals, but the main argument starts with 'And also'.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Notice that Maturidi states: despite the differences in substances regarding harm and benefit, Allah has made them all point to a single wise manager and to His oneness. They agree in indication and testimony like a single substance.
The argument is a concise proof from diversity to unity.
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Ask yourself: Why does diversity actually prove oneness? If there were multiple creators, we would expect inconsistency or chaos. Instead, we see a unified design across diverse substances.
This argument counters the misconception that diversity implies multiple gods.
4
Connect to broader theology
This passage is part of Maturidi's rational proofs for tawhid. It shows that reflection on the natural world leads to affirming a single wise Creator.
The phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' reminds us that all power is from Allah.

Source Text

العبادات والأخلاق المحمودة وأيضا أن الخلق على اختلاف جوهرهم في المضار والمنافع جعلهم الله في الدلالة على مدير لهم حكيم عليم، وعلى وحدانيته كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق من جهة الدلالة والشهادة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Praiseworthy acts of worship and morals. And also that the creatures, despite the difference of their substances in harms and benefits, Allah made them in indicating a manager for them, wise, knowing, and on His oneness, like a single substance in agreement from the aspect of indication and testimony. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage presents another wisdom: although creatures differ in their substances (jawāhir) regarding harm and benefit, God has made them all point to a single manager (mudabbir) who is wise and knowing, and to His oneness (waḥdāniyya). They are likened to a single substance in their agreement in indicating and testifying to God. This argument from diversity points to unity: the variety of created things, despite their differences, consistently point to one Creator. The passage begins with a heading 'Praiseworthy acts of worship and morals' which might be a separate topic, but the text continues directly with 'And also...'.

Key Takeaway
Diversity in creation is not a proof against unity but a powerful indicator of a single wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you today consciously look for that indication in something you previously dismissed?
?
The passage says that every creature, despite its differences, unanimously testifies to Allah’s oneness—like a single substance. Does your life testify to that oneness, or are your actions contradictory, as if serving multiple masters? Which area of your life needs to be brought into harmony with this testimony of creation? What specific step will you take this week to align your actions with the shahada that all creation proclaims?
?
The closing phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' is a declaration of reliance. Do you truly believe that the same wise Manager who ensures all creation indicates Him is also managing your affairs? Or do you carry burdens as if you are in control? Will you surrender a specific worry to Him today, acknowledging that there is no power except with Allah?

Combining opposites shows God's oneness
Maturidi states the first wisdom: combining opposites indicates God's oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that the coexistence of opposites in creation indicates the oneness of God.

Core Idea
Opposite natures united point to a single Creator.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument for tawhid from the very structure of creation. He points out that harmful and beneficial, good and evil exist together despite being opposites – this contradiction points to a single divine agent. Let's explore this wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key opposites listed: harmful/beneficial, good/evil.
Observe that they are 'on تناقضهما' (despite their opposition).
2
Identify Maturidi's argument
Maturidi says that this combination indicates God's oneness and testifies to His lordship. Ask yourself: why does the coexistence of opposites point to a single Creator?
This refutes the idea of dualism or multiple creators.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider how this argument fits into the broader theme of divine wisdom in creation. The very structure of the world reveals God's unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكمته أن جمع بين الضار والنافع، والخير والشر على تناقضهما في الدلالة على وحدانيته والشهادة بربوبيته واحدا وأيضا إنه خلق ذلك ليذل به الجبابرة والملوك، فيعلو بذلك ضعفهم - ولئلا يغتروا بكثرة الحواشى والجنود فيتعدوا حدود الله بما يرون من سلطان في قدرته تسليط من يشاء على من يشاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
His wisdom is that He combined the harmful and the beneficial, and good and evil, despite their opposition, in indicating His oneness and testifying to His lordship as one. And also that He created that to humiliate the tyrants and kings, so that their weakness rises thereby, and lest they be deluded by the abundance of retinues and armies and transgress the limits of Allah by what they see of authority, in His power to give authority to whom He wills over whom He wills. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage presents two more wisdoms. First, God's wisdom is seen in combining opposites like harm and benefit, good and evil, despite their contradiction, to indicate His oneness and testify to His lordship. The very fact that opposite natures coexist in creation points to a single powerful Creator. Second, God created harmful things to humble tyrants and kings, so that their weakness becomes apparent, and they do not become deluded by their power and transgress God's limits. This shows that God can give authority to anyone He wills, humbling the powerful.

Key Terms
الضار
النافع
الخير
الشر
التناقض
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The coexistence of good and evil proves dualism (two gods: one good, one evil).
Correction: Maturidi argues that the combination of opposites, despite their contradiction, actually proves one God who controls all, not two.

Checkpoint

A) They show randomness
B) They indicate His oneness and lordship despite their contradiction
C) They prove evil is from another source
D) They have no significance

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of opposites in creation testifies to God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you experienced both harm and benefit from the same event. Did that experience lead you to affirm God's unity, or did it confuse you? What can you do now to see His wisdom in such oppositions?
?
If you truly believe that the coexistence of good and evil testifies to God's oneness, how should that affect your reaction when you face evil? Will you turn to Him in worship, acknowledging His sole lordship? What is one concrete change you will make today?
?
Maturidi says the combination of opposites is a sign of God's lordship. Do you live as if that is true? When you see contradictions in the world, do you let them strengthen your tawheed or shake it? Commit to one way you will intentionally recognize God's oneness through opposites starting now.

Proof of a Single Creator from the Impossibility of Multiple Gods
Maturidi begins a new section arguing that the coherence of the world proves a single Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the coherence of the world proves it has a single Creator, because multiple managers would inevitably conflict and cause corruption.

Core Idea
The world's harmony testifies to one Creator, not many.

Maturidi begins a new section with a prayer, then presents a profound argument: every part of the world testifies that it is created and managed. If there were multiple managers, they would inevitably disagree, leading to the world's destruction. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why it is central to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the opening prayer and the shift to the proof. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is clear.
The Arabic has some unclear words marked with [؟].
2
Identify the signs of originatedness
Maturidi refers to 'signs of originatedness' and 'proofs upon which the management of other than Him runs.' Understand that these are the observable marks that the world is created and governed.
3
Understand the testimony of substances
Every substance and pillar of the world testifies that it has a manager who knows its states and is self-sufficient. This points to a single, all-powerful Creator.
4
Grasp the argument against multiple managers
Maturidi argues that multiple managers would lead to disagreement and mutual annihilation. The only exception is if one dominates; but that effectively makes it a single manager. This proves the necessity of one Creator.
This is a classic kalam proof for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف الفرق في العالم

نبتدأ الحمد لله العلى الحميد، ونتوجه إليه بالشكر له والتمجيد على ما أيدنا به من التسديد، ونرغب إليه في العون على ما قصدنا له والتأييد، فإنه على كل شيء شهيد، ونسأله أن يصلي على محمد، أفضل ما صلى على أحد من خيار [؟]، وأن يعطيه سؤله، وأن يلحقنا به بجوده، فإنه غني كريم.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: أما فإني تأملت بعاء [؟] اختلاف البشر في العالم بعد ظهور آيات حدثة، وأدلة جرى تدبير غيره عليه؛ إذ ما من شيء من جوهر العالم وأركانه إلا وهو جوهره يشهد بأنه مدبر مفروط [؟]، وأنه مضطر إلى عايم [؟] بأحواله، غني بملك حوائجه لئلا يتناقض فيتبدد، وأنه لا يحتمل بجوهره أن يرج [؟] وجه إلى عدد من المدبرين، بما [؟] لديه تمكن الاختلاف الذي عنده يريد كل أن يظهر سلطانه، ويغلب ملكه، ويقهر كل من نازعه، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد، اللهم إلا أن يكون لواحد منهم فضل قوة أو نصر يخضع له الجميع، فيصير كل خاضعا له ذليلا، بمعنى كل ... جوهر من جواهر العالم في خروجه على مشيئة غيره وجريه عليه سلطانه، وهو
The Difference of Sects in the World

We begin: Praise be to God the Most High, the Praiseworthy, and we turn to Him with thanks and glorification for what He has supported us with of right guidance, and we seek His help in what we intend and His support, for He is witness over everything. And we ask Him to bless Muhammad, the best of those He has blessed among the chosen [؟], and to grant him his request, and to join us with him through His bounty, for He is Rich, Generous.

Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: As for me, I have contemplated the [؟] difference of humans in the world after the appearance of signs of originatedness, and proofs upon which the management of other than Him runs; because there is not a single thing from among the substances and pillars of the world except that its substance testifies that it has a created [؟] manager, and that it is compelled to a [؟] knower of its states, self-sufficient in possessing its needs, so that it does not contradict and disintegrate, and that it does not allow by its substance that [؟] face towards a number of managers, because [؟] with him the possibility of disagreement is established, by which each wants to manifest his authority, and dominate his kingdom, and vanquish every opponent, and in that is mutual annihilation and corruption, unless one of them has an excess of power or victory so that all submit to him, and thus everything becomes submissive to him, humiliated, meaning that every... substance of the substances of the world proceeds according to the will of another and runs under his authority, and he is
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new section titled 'The Difference of Sects in the World.' Maturidi opens with a praise of God and a prayer for the Prophet Muhammad. He then states that he has contemplated the difference of humans after the clear signs of originatedness (i.e., the evidence that the world is created). The proof he presents is that every substance and pillar of the world testifies that it is created and has a manager who knows its states and is self-sufficient in its needs, so that it does not become contradictory and fall apart. He argues that it is impossible for the world's substance to be directed by multiple managers, because disagreement among them would lead to mutual annihilation and corruption. The only exception would be if one among them had superior power and all others submitted. However, that would effectively make that one the sole manager. The argument is a classic kalam proof for tawhid: the coherence of the world requires a single, all-powerful Creator. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the argument that all substances proceed under the will of that one manager.

فرق (firaq)
جوهر (jawhar)
مدبر (mudabbir)
تفاني (tafānī)
فساد (fasād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument from coherence shows that the world's order cannot result from multiple deities, proving the oneness of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What step can you take today to train your heart to see only His hand behind every blessing?
?
Maturidi warns that multiple masters lead to corruption. Are there internal 'masters'—such as desire for status, comfort, or approval—that compete for your ultimate loyalty? How would your life change if you surrendered all those to the sole authority of Allah, even when they seem to conflict?
?
The passage concludes that even a dominant god among many would end up being the sole effective manager. Is there an area of your life where you have been trying to serve multiple goals, thinking you can balance them, only to find inner chaos? What would it mean to make Allah's will the only manager of that arena, starting now?

The Dual Nature of Substances: A Proof of Tawheed
Maturidi argues that every substance contains both harm and benefit, refuting dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the coexistence of harm and benefit in every substance refutes dualism and points to God's oneness and complete power.

Core Idea
Every substance contains both harm and benefit—a sign pointing to one Creator.

Maturidi presents a profound observation from the physical world: every substance we encounter contains within it both the potential for harm and for benefit. This simple fact, he argues, refutes the dualistic notion that good and evil come from separate sources. In this lesson, we will unpack this argument and see how it leads to recognizing God's complete power and the basis for divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally before interpreting.
The passage uses the example of a jewel that can both cure and cause disease.
2
Identify the core claim
Locate the central claim: every jewel (substance) contains both harm and benefit. Notice how the author states this as a general rule.
The phrase 'كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع' is key.
3
Understand the refutation of dualism
The author explicitly says that saying evil comes from the jewel and good from another source is false. Recognise how this observation dismantles dualism.
Dualism often attributes evil to matter and good to spirit; Maturidi rejects that.
4
Examine the two aspects
The passage lists two aspects that follow from the dual nature: 1) it demonstrates God's complete power over harm and benefit, and 2) it provides a lesson for command and prohibition, reflection and gratitude.
These aspects connect the physical observation to theological and moral implications.
5
Connect to tawheed
Reflect on how the argument leads to tawheed: the coexistence of opposites in one substance points to a single Creator who controls all aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
11.
الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي
وكذلك [كل] جوهر مر أو إلا فيه دواء للداء المعضل، ليعلم الناظر أن القول بالشر بالجوهر والخير خطأ باطل، بل كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع، فيكون في ذلك أعظم آيات التوحيد. مع ما فيه وجهان: أحدهما القدرة التامة على ملك ما يضر وينفع ليرجو ويخاف، ومن لا يكون كذلك لا به، لأنه لا يتم الأمر يرهب منه ولا يرغب فما عنده، وقد يغلبه من له الأمران أيضا. والثاني العبر وليصبح الأمر والنهي فيكون للنظر والشكر مجال في الأمرين، عظة تهما وعبرة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
11. Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. Likewise, every jewel that passes or except that in it is a cure for the chronic disease, so that the observer knows that saying evil is from the jewel and good is false and invalid. Rather, every jewel from it is harm and benefit, and in that are the greatest signs of tawheed. With it are two aspects: one of them is complete power over possession of what harms and benefits so that he hopes and fears, and whoever is not like that, not with him, because the matter is not complete: one does not fear him nor desire what is with him, and he can be overcome by one who has both [qualities] also. And the second is the lesson so that command and prohibition become, so that there is scope for reflection and gratitude in both matters, an admonition and a lesson, and no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument from the properties of material substances (jewels). The author begins by stating that every jewel contains within it both a cure and a disease—i.e., both benefit and harm. This refutes the dualistic notion that evil comes from one source (the jewel) and good from another. Rather, each substance has both qualities, and this very fact is a profound sign of tawheed (God's oneness). The author then explains two aspects (wajhan) that follow from this. First, the fact that a substance can both harm and benefit points to the complete power of the One who owns it; for people to properly hope and fear, the entity they turn to must have full control over both harm and benefit. If a being lacks one of these, it is not fully sovereign and can be overpowered by another. Second, the coexistence of harm and benefit in the same substance serves as a lesson, establishing a basis for divine command and prohibition and giving scope for reflection and gratitude. The passage concludes with the formula 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah.'

Key Terms
جوهر
ضر
نفع
آيات التوحيد
القدرة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harm and benefit come from separate sources (e.g., evil from matter, good from spirit).
Correction: Maturidi shows that each substance itself contains both harm and benefit, so there can be only one Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the fact that every substance contains both harm and benefit refute?
A) The existence of God
B) The claim that evil comes from one source and good from another (dualism)
C) The idea that substances are created
D) The concept of divine power

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of harm and benefit in every substance is a powerful sign of tawheed, refuting dualism and pointing to one all-powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience harm in your life, do you genuinely believe it comes from the same merciful Creator who sends you benefit? Or does your heart attribute it to another source? Reflect on a recent difficulty—can you see the sign of tawheed in it? Commit to thanking Allah for that trial today.
?
If you truly believed that every substance has both harm and benefit from Allah alone, how would your fear and hope of Him change? Would you still fear people or circumstances more than you fear Allah? Write one specific fear you will replace with hope in Allah's complete power.
?
Maturidi says this reality gives scope for reflection and gratitude. When was the last time you paused to thank Allah for a trial that brought you closer to Him? Set a reminder to do a gratitude journal entry tonight specifically for a hardship you have faced.

Allah's Existence

Proof of a Single Creator from Cosmic Harmony
Maturidi presents a multi-faceted proof for the oneness of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi proves the oneness of God by arguing that the world's order, its dependence on a manager, and its temporal nature all point to a single, wise, and powerful Creator.

Core Idea
The world's order and dependence demand a single Manager, not many.

In this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam Maturidi presents a comprehensive proof for the oneness of God. He begins by observing that the world shows clear signs of being created and governed by an external power. Every part of the world bears witness that it is a contrived being, managed by a wise ordering that prevents contradiction. Maturidi then argues that this ordering cannot come from multiple managers, as they would inevitably conflict, leading to destruction. He also points to the world's origin and its continuation as signs of a single Creator. Finally, he notes that both the dead and the living are sustained by similar causes, indicating the same Manager.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the entire Arabic passage and its translation. Note the flow from doxology to the core argument to the subsidiary proofs.
The passage is dense, so read carefully.
2
Identify the main argument
Locate the statement that every substance is a 'mudabbar mafṭūr' and the reasoning that multiple managers lead to conflict.
This is the key logical proof.
3
Examine the subsidiary arguments
Notice the arguments from the world's origin (abtida' kawnihi) and from the dead and living (al-mawāt wa al-aḥyā'). How do they support the main claim?
These show the need for a single Manager from different angles.
4
Understand the exception
Consider the exception: if one manager has overwhelming power, all submit. Why does this still point to one God?
This actually reinforces tawheed.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that all this proves it must be a single, wise, knowing Creator. Reflect on how the parts fit together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف الفرق في العالم
نبتدأ الحمد لله العلى الحميد، ونتوجه إليه بالشكر له والتمجيد على ما أيدنا به من التسديد، ونرغب إليه في العون على ما قصدنا له والتأييد، فإنه عاني كل شيء شهيد، ونسأله أن يصلى على محمد، أفضل ما صلى على أحد من خيار خامه، وأن يعطيه سوله، وأن يلحقنا به بجوده، فإنه غني كريم. قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: أما فإني تأملت بعاء اختلاف البشر في العالم بعد ظهور آيات حدثة، وأدلة جرى تدبير غيره عليه؛ إذ ما من شيء من جوهر العالم وأركانه إلا وهو جوهره يشهد بأنه مدبر مفطور، وأنه مضطر تحكيم يضع كل شيء موضعه؛ لئلا يتناقض فيتبدد، وأنه لا يحتمل بجوهره أن الوجه إلى عدد من المدبرين، بما لديه تمكن الاختلاف الذي عنده يريد كل أن يظهر سلطانه، ويغلب ملكه، ويقهر كل من نازعه، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد، اللهم إلا أن يكون لواحد منهم فضل قوة أو نصر يخضع له الجميع، فيصير كل خاضعا له ذليلا، بمعنى كل جوهر من جواهر العالم في خروجه على مشيئة غيره وجريه عليه سلطانه، وهو ويتم ويخرج من العام المعنى الذي هو دليل مدبر العالم عليم حكيم ليقوم به هو، إلى الوجود، إذ الأعجوبة في ابتداء كونه إلى من يعلم كيفية إنشاء الأشياء ليست بدون الأعجوبة في دوامه وقيامه على ما هو عليه، بل كانت أظهر، والحاجة في ذلك إلى غيره أعظم؛ إذ هو عن تدبير نفسه أعجز، وأسباب آجالها له به أعظم، مع ما في كل براهين كونه بعد أن لم يكن أبين، إذ كل ذي عقل وبصر لعله يذكر ابتداءه أو تقلبه من أحوال تقدمت من الصغر واللطافة إذا لم يجعل لتلك الجملة ابتداء يبطل كونه. ثم احتمال كل الوهن والضعف لا أن يتلاشى ويبطل مما يضطره إلى العلم بكونه بعد أن لم يكن. وإن كان ذا أ.. من يملك التدبير ويعلم بالأحوال فالموات هو التي تحت تدبير الأحياء نداء به من حيث لا يشعر بذلك أحق بذلك. ثم دل كون الأموات على ١٠ ٠١٠٠١ بها الاستمتاع على أن الذي دبرها هو الذي دبر الأحياء، إذ يعانها لهم، بها صلاحهم. من بعد ما بينا مما يجب أن يجها.
The difference of the sects concerning the world. We begin with praise to Allah, the High, the Praiseworthy, and we turn to Him with thanks and glorification for what He has supported us with of guidance, and we seek His help in what we intend and confirmation, for He is aware of all things, Witness. And we ask Him to send blessings upon Muhammad, the best of what He has blessed any one of His chosen, and to grant him his request, and to join us with him by His bounty, for He is Independent, Generous. The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: As for me, I have reflected upon the difference of mankind concerning the world after the appearance of signs of its temporality, and indications of the running of another's governance upon it; for there is nothing of the substance of the world and its pillars except that its essence bears witness that it is a contrived manager, and that it is compelled by an ordering that places everything in its place; so that it does not contradict and perish, and that it does not bear by its essence the turning towards a number of managers, because there is established within it the possibility of difference which each of them intends to show his authority, and dominate his kingdom, and overcome every contender, and in that is annihilation and corruption, unless one of them has an advantage of power or victory such that all submit to him, so that everyone becomes submissive to him humiliated, meaning that every substance of the substances of the world in its coming out under the will of another and its running upon his authority, and it completes and exits from the general meaning which is the proof of the Manager of the world, Knowing, Wise, that it comes into existence through Him, for the wonder in its coming into being from one who knows the manner of creating things is not without the wonder in its continuance and standing as it is, rather it is more apparent, and the need in that for another is greater; for it is more incapable of managing itself, and the causes of its terms are greater for it through Him, along with what is in all of it of proofs of its coming into being after non-existence are clearer, for every possessor of intellect and sight may mention its beginning or its change from states that preceded from smallness and subtlety, if not made for that whole a beginning would invalidate its existence. Then the possibility of all weakening and frailty does not that it should vanish and perish from what compels it to knowledge that it came into being after non-existence. And if it is possessor of [...] of who owns management and knows the states, then the dead are those under the management of the living, a call by it from where it does not perceive that is more deserving of that. Then the fact of the dead upon [...] enjoyment indicates that the one who managed them is the one who managed the living, since He helps them through it, their well-being. After what we have explained of what must be rejected.
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof for the oneness of God (tawheed) and a refutation of polytheism and dualism. It begins with a doxology and then presents the core argument: the world shows signs of being created and governed by an external power. Every substance in the world bears witness that it is a contrived manager (mudabbar mafṭūr), meaning it is not self-sufficient but is managed by another. The world is compelled by a wise ordering that prevents contradiction and dissolution. This ordering cannot come from multiple managers because that would inevitably lead to conflict, each wanting to assert his authority, resulting in annihilation and corruption. The only way multiple managers could work is if one is supremely powerful and all others submit, but then that one is effectively the sole manager. The passage then continues onto the next page, discussing the wonder of the world's origin and continuation. The initial creation is as wondrous as its continued existence, and the need for a creator is even more evident from the fact that the world is incapable of managing itself. The argument from the beginning of the world—its coming into being after non-existence—is presented as a clear proof. The weakness and decay observable in things compel the rational person to recognize that the world came into being. Furthermore, the dead and the living all point to the same Manager, because both are sustained by similar causes. Thus, the proof concludes that the world must have a single, wise, and powerful Creator.

الوجه إلى عدد من المدبرين
ابتداء كونه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple gods could cooperate to manage the world harmoniously.
Correction: Maturidi shows that independent wills necessarily lead to conflict, as each would seek to assert authority, resulting in annihilation and corruption. Thus, only one God can be the true Manager.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue would happen if there were multiple independent managers of the world?
A) The world would be more harmonious
B) They would cooperate perfectly
C) There would be conflict, annihilation, and corruption
D) They would appoint a leader among themselves

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument proves that the world's order, origin, and sustenance all necessitate a single, wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire world—every substance and pillar—cannot manage itself but needs a single Manager, then what about you? Which area of your life are you still pretending to manage on your own, without turning to the One who places everything in its place? Will you hand over the control today?
?
The proof shows that multiple independent wills lead to conflict and corruption. Look inside yourself: how many competing desires and allegiances are pulling you apart? Are you living under one sovereign will, or are you trying to serve multiple masters? What will you do right now to submit to the only true Manager?
?
Maturidi says that the wonder of the world's coming into being is greater than its continuance, yet you take both for granted. When was the last time you truly felt awe that you exist? If you had to depend on yourself for your next breath, you would perish. Will you let this truth shatter your illusion of self-sufficiency and drive you to your knees before the Creator?

Proof from the World's Continuance
Maturidi argues that the world's continuance proves a wise creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world's ongoing existence requires a wise creator, and how this complements the argument from origination.

Core Idea
The world's continuance is as miraculous as its beginning.

Maturidi presents a proof for God's existence based not on the world's origin, but on its preservation. He argues that the world's inability to sustain itself and the order observed in its continuance point to a wise manager. This lesson examines this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms like 'أعجوبة' (wonder) and 'دوام' (duration).
Some words are unclear due to OCR, but the main argument is discernible.
2
Identify the two wonders
Maturidi compares the wonder of the world's beginning with the wonder of its continuance. Identify why he says continuance is an even greater sign.
He says it is 'أظهر' (more apparent) and need for another is greater.
3
Understand the analogy of the dead and living
He draws an analogy: the dead are managed by the living without awareness. How does this support the need for a creator?
This shows that the inanimate world is managed by a living manager.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this proof complements the standard kalam argument from contingency. Why is the continuance argument powerful?
It focuses on the world's ongoing dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتم ويخرج من العام المعنى الذي هو دليل مدبر العالم عليم حكيم ليقوم به هو، إلى الوجود، إذ الأعجوبة في ابتداء كونه إلى من يعلم كيفية إنشاء الأشياء ليست بدون الأعجوبة في دوامه وقيامه على ما هو عليه، بل كانت أظهر، والحامة في ذلك إلى غيره أعظم؛ إذ هو عن تدبير نفسه أعجز، وأسباب آجالها له به أعظم، مع ما في كل براهين كونه بعد أن لم يكن أبين، إذ كل ذي عقل وبصر لعله يذكر ابتداءه أو تقلبه من أحوال تقدمت من الصغر واللطافة إذا لم يجعل لتلك الجملة ابتداء يبطل كونه. ثم احتمال كل الوهن والضعف لا أن يتلاشى ويبطل مما يضطره إلى العلم بكونه بعد أن لم يكن. وإن كان ذا أ[؟] من يملك التدبير ويعلم بالأحوال فالموات هو التي تحت تدبير الأحياء نداء به من حيث لا يشعر بذلك أحق بذلك. ثم دل كون الأموات على [؟] بها الاستمتاع على أن الذي دبرها هو الذي دبر الأحياء، إذ يعانها لهم، بها صلاحهم. من بعد ما بينا مما يجب أن يجها.
And it completes and emerges from the general meaning which is the proof of a wise, knowing manager of the world for it to stand, to existence. For the wonder in its beginning to one who knows how to create things is not without the wonder in its duration and its standing as it is; rather it is more apparent, and the need in that for another is greater; since it is more incapable of managing itself, and the causes of its terms are greater for it [to rely] on Him, along with what in every proof of its existence after non-existence is clearer. After what we have shown of what must be directed.

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a wise creator based on the continuance of the world. Maturidi argues that the ongoing preservation of the world is as miraculous as its initial creation, perhaps even more so, because the world's inability to sustain itself shows its dependence on an external manager. He notes that the world is subject to weakness and change, which would lead to its disintegration if not for a sustaining power. He also draws an analogy: the dead are managed by the living, even without the dead's awareness, so the world's inanimate parts are managed by a living creator. The passage concludes by emphasizing that this reasoning forces one to acknowledge the world's createdness and its need for a wise designer.

Dualism Leads to Unreliable Human Judgment
Maturidi critiques dualist epistemology and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Maturidi, dualist premises make human judgments about wisdom and foolishness unreliable.

Core Idea
If humans are a mix of light and darkness, their judgments are untrustworthy.

Maturidi begins by attacking the dualist epistemology. Their claim that humans are composed of both good and evil essences leads to a contradiction: a person's statements about wisdom or foolishness cannot be trusted because they may be speaking from either essence. This undermines any claim to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how Maturidi builds his argument.
The passage uses a complex conditional structure.
2
Identify the dualist premise
Note the dualist claim: good comes only from the essence of light, evil only from the essence of darkness, and humans are a mixture.
This premise is key to the refutation.
3
Follow the logical consequences
Maturidi shows that if a human is a mixture, they may mistake wisdom for foolishness and vice versa, and their speech cannot be reliably attributed to either essence.
This makes their testimony worthless.
4
Consider the loss of hope and fear
If neither essence has power over the other, hope and fear become meaningless, removing the incentive to distinguish wisdom and foolishness.
This destroys the practical benefit of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولو أنعم هؤلاء الفرق النظر فيما تقدم من ذكر الأدلة لعلموا قصور عقولهم عن الوقوف على الحكمة البشرية فضلا عن يحيدلوا بحكمة الربوبية، مع ما فيها إليه صاروا في الاختيار منع لهم عن دعوى معرفة حقيقة الحكمة والسفه؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن لا يرون بجوهر الشر إلا الشر وبجوهر الخير إلا الخير، ثم لا يدرى فيها سموه سفها أو حكمة إنه فعل الشر أو فعل الخير، وكل الإنسان عندهم مشوب من الأمرين، يرى بكل واحد خلاف ما يرى بالآخر، فلعله رأى الحكمة سفها والسفه حكمة، ثم لا يوثق بقوله لأنه خير فهو من جوهر الظلمة كذب كله ومن جوهر النور صدق كله فلا يدرى بأى جوهرين ينطق، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم إذ لم يكن لواحد منهما قدرة على الضرر ولا للاخر قدرة على النفع فانقطع الرجاء والخوف جميعا فيذهب منفعة معرفة الحكمة والسفه. ثم إذ كان كل واحد من الجوهرين يعمل بالطبع فوقوع العلم بالحكمة إذا حال والسفه بالطبع، والحكمة هي وضع كل شيء موضعه والسفه وضع كل شيء في غير موضعه، ومحال وصف ذي طبع به؛ إذ هو اختيار، والنور عندهم يعلم ما السفه فيحذره ولا الظلمة تعلم ما الحكمة، والجهل بمائية الشيء وبالوضع له شره فصار جوهر النور عندهم هو الذي اجتمع فيه العلم والجهل ثم القدرة والعجز بما لا يقدر على صرف السفه نفسه ولا يمنع الظلمة عن الضرر به، فصار جوهر الخير عندهم مشوبا بالشر وجوهر الظلمة لا خير فيه، فلزم على قولهم غلبة الشر على الخير، والذي هو خير لم يعرف البشر والسفه، فكيف يولد عن جوهر الخير بعد غلبة الشر عليه الخير والشر؟ على أن كل ذي طبع مقهور، إذ لا يملك صرف ما يوجبه الطبع وإيجاب الخلاف، وفي ذلك إيجاب قاهر يجعل ذا شرا بالطبع وهذا خيرا، ولو رد ذا إلى اثنين كان فيها ما في هذين نحو التسخين والتبريد أنه يكون بمن جعله كذلك، وفي ذلك إيجاب القول بالواحد.
The jurist Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: If these groups had thoroughly examined the preceding mention of proofs, they would have known the deficiency of their intellects in grasping human wisdom, let alone deviating from divine wisdom, along with what they fell into in choice preventing them from claiming knowledge of the reality of wisdom and foolishness; since from their doctrine they do not see in the essence of evil except evil, and in the essence of good except good, then it is not known whether they call it foolishness or wisdom that it is an act of evil or an act of good, and every human according to them is mixed from both aspects, he sees with each one contrary to what he sees with the other, so perhaps he saw wisdom as foolishness and foolishness as wisdom, then his statement is not reliable because if he is good, he is from the essence of darkness, all lying, and if from the essence of light, all truth, so it is not known by which of the two essences he speaks, and there is no power except with God. Then if neither of them has power to harm nor the other power to benefit, then hope and fear are both cut off, so the benefit of knowing wisdom and foolishness disappears. Then since each of the two essences acts by nature, the occurrence of knowledge of wisdom when it is a state and foolishness by nature, and wisdom is placing everything in its proper place and foolishness is placing everything not in its proper place, and it is impossible to describe one with a nature by that, since it is choice; and light according to them knows what foolishness is and avoids it, and darkness does not know what wisdom is, and ignorance of the quiddity of a thing and its placement is its evil; so the essence of light according to them becomes that in which knowledge and ignorance, then power and incapacity, are combined, insofar as it cannot turn away foolishness from itself nor prevent darkness from harming it; so the essence of good according to them becomes mixed with evil, and the essence of darkness has no good in it; so upon their statement, the dominance of evil over good is necessitated; and what is good did not know good and evil, so how can what is born from the essence of good after evil dominates it produce good and evil? Yet every being with a nature is subdued, for it does not have the power to turn away what nature necessitates and to necessitate the opposite; and in that is the necessity of a subduer who makes this evil by nature and that good; and if one were to reduce this to two, there would be in them what is in these two, like heating and cooling, that it is from the one who made it so; and in that is the necessity of affirming the One.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's refutation of the dualist arguments. He begins by stating that if the dualists had properly considered the earlier proofs, they would realize the limitation of their intellects regarding human wisdom, let alone divine wisdom. The refutation proceeds as follows: First, within their own doctrine, since each essence produces only its own kind (good from light, evil from darkness), and humans are a mixture, a person's judgment about wisdom or foolishness is unreliable—he might call wisdom foolishness and vice versa, because his speech comes from both essences, making his statements untrustworthy. Second, if neither essence has power over the other (light cannot harm, darkness cannot benefit), then hope and fear are eliminated, and the very benefit of distinguishing wisdom and foolishness is lost. Third, since each essence acts by nature, not by choice, wisdom (placing things in their proper place) and foolishness (misplacing them) cannot be attributed to them, because natural action lacks discrimination. Furthermore, if light knows foolishness and avoids it, but darkness does not know wisdom, then light's essence becomes a mix of knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity—it cannot prevent darkness from harming it. Thus, good becomes mixed with evil, and evil dominates good. But if good does not know evil, how can it produce good and evil after being dominated? Finally, anything acting by nature is subdued and does not control its own effects, which necessitates a subduer who makes one evil by nature and another good. If one postulates two such natures, they still require an external cause (like heating and cooling require a source). This leads to the necessity of affirming a single Creator (al-Wāḥid).

Key Terms
حكمة
سفه
جوهر
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dualism allows humans to objectively know good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that because humans are a mixture of conflicting essences, their judgments are inherently unreliable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what problem arises for a human's speech in the dualist framework?
A) It is always truthful because from the essence of light.
B) It is always false because from the essence of darkness.
C) It cannot be reliably attributed to either essence, so it is untrustworthy.
D) It is a mixture of truth and falsehood, but still reliable.

Key Takeaway
Dualist premises lead to an epistemological crisis where human judgments and speech cannot be trusted, and hope and fear lose their meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you find yourself 'seeing with two essences'—where your judgments conflict because you are drawing from both good and bad inclinations? How do you decide which voice to trust, and does this lesson challenge that method?
?
If you believed that your speech could come from either light or darkness without your control, would you ever be confident in what you say? How does this contrast with the Islamic teaching that you are accountable for every word?
?
Maturidi argues that dualism cuts off hope and fear, the motivators for seeking wisdom. Is there any belief in your own heart that diminishes your hope in Allah’s reward or fear of His punishment? How does that affect your pursuit of wisdom in daily life?

Allah's Existence
advanced
7 min
Nature Necessitates a Single Subduer
Maturidi refutes dualist argument from nature and concludes with the necessity of a single Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if essences act by nature, they must be subdued by a single Creator, leading to the affirmation of tawhid.

Core Idea
Whatever acts by nature is subdued and points to a subduer.

In this continuation, Maturidi shifts to the argument from natural action. The dualists claim the two essences act by nature, not choice. Maturidi shows this leads to contradictions: wisdom and foolishness cannot be attributed to natures, light is mixed with ignorance, and evil dominates good. Ultimately, anything acting by nature is subdued, necessitating a subduer who makes them as they are, which leads to affirming the One.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the shift from the earlier argument to a new focus on nature (ṭabʿ).
2
Understand the nature premise
The dualists say each essence acts by nature, not by choice. Maturidi argues that wisdom and foolishness are about placement, which requires choice, so they cannot describe a nature.
3
Trace the contradiction within light
If light knows foolishness and avoids it, it must know it, but darkness does not know wisdom. This forces light to be a mixture of knowledge and ignorance, which contradicts its pure nature.
This is a key internal inconsistency.
4
Consider the dominance of evil
Since light cannot prevent darkness from harming it, evil comes to dominate good, and then it is unclear how good can produce good after being dominated.
5
Conclude with the subduer
Anything with a nature is subdued and cannot change it. This necessitates a subduer who makes one evil and the other good. Even if one posits two, they require an external cause, leading to the affirmation of the One.
This is the final refutation proving tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ولو أنعم هؤلاء الفرق النظر فيما تقدم من ذكر الأدلة لعلموا قصور عقولهم عن الوقوف على الحكمة البشرية فضلا عن يحيدلوا بحكمة الربوبية، مع ما فيها إليه صاروا في الاختيار منع لهم عن دعوى معرفة حقيقة الحكمة والسفه؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن لا يرون بجوهر الشر إلا الشر وبجوهر الخير إلا الخير، ثم لا يدرى فيها سموه سفها أو حكمة إنه فعل الشر أو فعل الخير، وكل الإنسان عندهم مشوب من الأمرين، يرى بكل واحد خلاف ما يرى بالآخر، فلعله رأى الحكمة سفها والسفه حكمة، ثم لا يوثق بقوله لأنه خير فهو من جوهر الظلمة كذب كله ومن جوهر النور صدق كله فلا يدرى بأى جوهرين ينطق، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم إذ لم يكن لواحد منهما قدرة على الضرر ولا للاخر قدرة على النفع فانقطع الرجاء والخوف جميعا فيذهب منفعة معرفة الحكمة والسفه. ثم إذ كان كل واحد من الجوهرين يعمل بالطبع فوقوع العلم بالحكمة إذا حال والسفه بالطبع، والحكمة هي وضع كل شيء موضعه والسفه وضع كل شيء في غير موضعه، ومحال وصف ذي طبع به؛ إذ هو اختيار، والنور عندهم يعلم ما السفه فيحذره ولا الظلمة تعلم ما الحكمة، والجهل بمائية الشيء وبالوضع له شره فصار جوهر النور عندهم هو الذي اجتمع فيه العلم والجهل ثم القدرة والعجز بما لا يقدر على صرف السفه نفسه ولا يمنع الظلمة عن الضرر به، فصار جوهر الخير عندهم مشوبا بالشر وجوهر الظلمة لا خير فيه، فلزم على قولهم غلبة الشر على الخير، والذي هو خير لم يعرف البشر والسفه، فكيف يولد عن جوهر الخير بعد غلبة الشر عليه الخير والشر؟ على أن كل ذي طبع مقهور، إذ لا يملك صرف ما يوجبه الطبع وإيجاب الخلاف، وفي ذلك إيجاب قاهر يجعل ذا شرا بالطبع وهذا خيرا، ولو رد ذا إلى اثنين كان فيها ما في هذين نحو التسخين والتبريد أنه يكون بمن جعله كذلك، وفي ذلك إيجاب القول بالواحد.

What does Maturidi conclude about anything that acts by nature?
A) It is free and independent.
B) It is subdued and requires a subduer.
C) It can change its nature by choice.

Key Takeaway
The dualist premise that essences act by nature leads to contradictions and ultimately necessitates affirming a single, all-powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When evil or difficulty strikes you, do you ever internally split the world into two forces—one good and one evil—rather than submitting to the One who alone subdues all? What would it take for you to see that difficulty as coming from the same single Subduer as all good?
?
You have habits and impulses that feel like 'second nature.' Do you live as though you are subdued by them, or do you affirm your ikhtiyār (choice) and turn to the Qāhir for strength to overcome them? What is one area where you need to stop excusing yourself by 'that's just how I am'?
?
If you truly believe that everything acting by nature is subdued and points to a single Subduer, how does that change your perception of the natural world and your place in it? Are you living in awe of the One who made things hot and cold, good and evil by nature, or have you become numb to His constant subjugation?

Allah's Existence
beginner
4 min
Every Nature is Compelled, Necessitating the One
Maturidi's proof from compulsion of natures for the One.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument from the compulsion of natures to the necessity of a single Creator.

Core Idea
Compelled natures demand a single Compeller — not two.

Maturidi presents a concise yet powerful argument for tawhid based on the observation that everything that acts by nature is compelled — it cannot change its own inherent properties. This leads to the necessity of a being that has determined these natures. Read on to see how he refutes dualism and affirms the One.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once silently, then aloud. Let the cadence of the argument sink in.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on طبع (nature) and قهر (compulsion). Maturidi uses these to build his proof.
The term قاهر (compeller) is central.
3
Follow the logic
Trace the argument: nature is compelled -> needs a compeller -> if two compellers, they'd also be compelled -> only one.
He uses the example of heating and cooling.
4
Consider the conclusion
The argument necessitates affirming the One (إيجاب القول بالواحد). Reflect on how this refutes dualism.
This is the core of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن كل ذي طبع مقهور , إذ لا يملك صرف ما يوجبه الطبع وإيجاب الخلاف , وفي ذلك إيجاب قاهر يجعل ذا شرا بالطبع وهذا خيرا , ولو رد ذا إلى اثنين كان فيها ما في هذين نحو التسخين والتبريد أنه يكون بمن جعله كذلك , وفي ذلك إيجاب القول بالواحد .
Moreover, every possessor of nature is compelled, since it does not have the power to avert what nature necessitates or to necessitate its opposite. And in that is the necessitation of a Compeller who makes this one evil by nature and that one good. And if this were reduced to two, there would be in them what is in these two, such as heating and cooling, that it is by the one who made them so. And in that is the necessitation of affirming the One.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of God from the compulsion of natures. Maturidi argues that anything that acts by nature is compelled (مقهور) because it cannot change its own nature. Therefore, there must be a Compeller (قاهر) who determined one essence to be evil by nature and another to be good by nature. If one were to posit two such compellers, they would themselves have to be governed by the same principle — for example, the qualities of heating and cooling must come from a single source that makes them so. Thus, the argument leads to the necessity of affirming one single Creator, destroying the dualist position.

Key Terms
طبع
قهر
قاهر
واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is arguing against natural causation itself.
Correction: He is not arguing against natural causation per se, but against the sufficiency of two uncreated natures as ultimate principles.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that every nature is compelled?
A) There must be two compellers.
B) There must be a single compeller, necessitating the One.
C) Natures are self-sufficient.
D) Good and evil are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof shows that the compulsion of natures logically leads to affirming one single Creator, not two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt the overwhelming pull of your own nature—anger, desire, or laziness—and realized you cannot change it by yourself? This lesson says every nature is compelled. Who controls your nature? Are you willing today to submit to the only One who can truly change you?
?
When you see two people with vastly different dispositions—one naturally inclined to good, another to evil—do you ever wonder why? Maturidi explains that a single Compeller made them so. If that is true, does your resentment or judgment of others still hold? Can you accept God's wisdom in their nature and yours?
?
This proof shows that if there were two ultimate powers, they would be limited by their own natures. Do you live as if there is only One who has absolute control, or do you mentally attribute ultimate power to other things—your wealth, health, or relationships? What would change today if you truly believed that only Allah is the Compeller of all?

intermediate

The Subjugation of Natures
Maturidi argues that nature is subjugated because it can be prevented from acting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that a nature (tabi‘ah) is subjugated because it can be prevented from acting, proving it is not autonomous.

Core Idea
A nature cannot refuse to act but can be stopped, showing it is controlled by another.

Maturidi begins by attacking the foundational claim of the Naturalists: that natures are independent causal agents. He points out a simple fact: a nature cannot help but do what it does, yet it can be prevented by something else. This shows its action is not its own. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi defines mqrūr (subjugated).
The key point is that a nature is unable to refrain from its action.
2
Identify the proof that nature is not autonomous
Maturidi argues: if a nature could act on its own, it would not tolerate being prevented. Since it can be prevented, its action is for another.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion: nature is subjugated under a knowing subjugator. This establishes that natural causality is not independent.
This refutes the Naturalist claim of autonomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحاب الطبائع فإن الطابع مقهور لا يقدر على الامتناع عما طبع عليه، بل يقدر غير كل ذى طبع أن يمنع إياه عن توليده، فثبت أن عمله لغيره ما يعمل؛ إذ قد يمنع بغيره عن العمل، ولو كان بنفسه يعمل ذلك ما احتمل ما دامت نفسه، ما إذ كان لا يمتنع من عمل ثبت أنه مقهور تحت قاهر عليم. مع ثم كل ذى طبع لا يعمل في شيء بطبعه إلا أن يكون الآخر مجعولا بحيث يقبل ذلك، نحو الشيء الذي يتأذى لا يؤذيه الفعل الذي في غيره مؤذ، وكذلك المؤلم والملذ، وكذلك الأصباغ. وليس عمل الطبع أن يجعل شيئا يقبل طبعه ويتأثر به، فثبت به كون غير الطبائع. مع ما لو خلى بين ذى الطبع وعمله لكان لا يؤلف ولا يصور، فدل وجودها على غير ذلك: أن لها منشئا. وبعد، فإنه لو خلى بين الأصباغ وانصباغ الأشياء بها ليخرج فاسدا مسمما وإنما يصلح ذلك الحكيم عليم يضع كل شيء موضعه، فمثله أمر الطبائع وهو في شأن الطبائع أحق؛ إذ هي تتنافر وفيها التباعد، أو يقدح في الأشياء بلا حد، وفيه الفساد، فدل الاتساق وقيام الأعيان بها على عليم قاهر جمع بينها ما كان لكل مجتمع الطبائع حامل يحملها ليس هو لهن، فثبت بالضرورة وجودهن، وقد مضى من هذا النوع ما فيه مقنع.
As for the partisans of natures (as-hab al-taba'i), indeed the nature is subjugated; it is unable to refrain from what it is naturally disposed to, but another other than every owner of a nature is able to prevent it from its generation. So it is established that its action is for other than itself; for it is prevented by another from acting. And if it were itself acting, it would not tolerate prevention as long as its nature persists. Since it does not refrain from acting, it is established that it is subjugated under a subjugator who is knowing. Moreover, every owner of a nature does not act in a thing by its nature unless that other is made so as to accept that, like the thing that is harmed—the act that is harmful in another does not harm it—and likewise the painful and pleasurable, and likewise dyes. And the action of nature is not to make a thing accept its nature and be affected by it; so the existence of other than natures is established. Moreover, if one were to leave between the owner of a nature and its action, it would not compose nor form. So their existence indicates something else: that they have an originator. And then, if one were to leave between dyes and the dyeing of things with them, it would come out corrupt and poisonous. Only the wise, knowing one who places everything in its proper place makes it sound. So likewise is the matter of natures, and it is more fitting concerning the affair of natures; for they repel each other and there is distance in them, or they attack things without limit, and therein is corruption. So harmony and the subsistence of entities through them indicate a knowing, subjugating one who combined them, since for every assembly of natures there is a carrier that carries them and is not from them. So their existence is established by necessity, and enough of this type has passed.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that natures (taba'i) are autonomous causal principles. Maturidi argues that a nature is subjugated (maqhur); it cannot refrain from its inherent action, but it can be prevented by an external agent. This shows that its action is not truly its own but is controlled by another. He then uses the analogy of dye: a dye only works if the object is made receptive, proving the need for a wise agent who prepares receptivity. Similarly, natures are diverse and conflicting; if left to themselves, they would produce disorder. The observed harmony in the world, where opposites like heat and cold coexist in a single body, proves a wise, powerful manager (qahir 'alim) who brings them together. Finally, the argument concludes that the existence of natures themselves requires an originator (munshi').

Key Terms
طبيعة
مقهور
قاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies causality altogether.
Correction: Maturidi only denies that natures are autonomous; he affirms that they are real causal instruments subjugated to God's will.

Checkpoint
What proves that a nature is not acting autonomously according to Maturidi?
A) It cannot refrain from its inherent action
B) It can be prevented from acting by something else
C) It is eternal
D) It is visible

Key Takeaway
A nature is subjugated because it can be prevented; thus it is not autonomous but under a knowing subjugator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your own 'nature'—your habits, desires, and inclinations—is autonomous and cannot be changed? Or do you acknowledge that it is subjugated to the will of Allah? What is one specific habit you will surrender to Him today, recognizing that He can prevent its effect or transform it?
?
When you rely on natural means (e.g., medicine, wealth, relationships), do you truly believe they are merely instruments under the control of al-Qahir al-'Alim, or do you secretly trust them as independent causes? How can you align your heart's reliance with the truth that all causation is ultimately from Allah?
?
This passage proves that natures can be prevented. Is there an area of your life where you have been obstinate, acting as if your character or circumstances are fixed? What would it look like for you to submit to the Subjugator who can override every natural obstacle?

Harmony and the Need for an Originator
Maturidi provides three arguments to prove natures require an originator and a subjugator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments from the need for receptivity, composition, and harmony that prove the existence of a wise originator.

Core Idea
Natures depend on external conditions and produce order, pointing to a wise subjugator.

Maturidi extends his critique, showing that natures cannot act without proper receptivity, cannot compose forms on their own, and that the observed harmony in creation demands a wise manager. These arguments reinforce that natures are not self-sufficient but require an originator.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three sub-arguments: receptivity, composition, and harmony.
The dye analogy is central to the first sub-argument.
2
Analyze the receptivity argument
A nature only acts if the object is made receptive. For example, dye only works if the fabric accepts it. This shows a need for a wise preparer.
This proves the existence of something beyond natures.
3
Analyze the composition argument
If left alone, a nature would not compose or form anything. The fact that we see formed beings indicates an originator.
This is a simple teleological argument.
4
Analyze the harmony argument
Natures are conflicting (hot, cold, wet, dry). Their coexistence and order in entities prove a wise subjugator who combines them.
This is the strongest argument from design.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحاب الطبائع فإن الطابع مقهور لا يقدر على الامتناع عما طبع عليه، بل يقدر غير كل ذى طبع أن يمنع إياه عن توليده، فثبت أن عمله لغيره ما يعمل؛ إذ قد يمنع بغيره عن العمل، ولو كان بنفسه يعمل ذلك ما احتمل ما دامت نفسه، ما إذ كان لا يمتنع من عمل ثبت أنه مقهور تحت قاهر عليم. مع ثم كل ذى طبع لا يعمل في شيء بطبعه إلا أن يكون الآخر مجعولا بحيث يقبل ذلك، نحو الشيء الذي يتأذى لا يؤذيه الفعل الذي في غيره مؤذ، وكذلك المؤلم والملذ، وكذلك الأصباغ. وليس عمل الطبع أن يجعل شيئا يقبل طبعه ويتأثر به، فثبت به كون غير الطبائع. مع ما لو خلى بين ذى الطبع وعمله لكان لا يؤلف ولا يصور، فدل وجودها على غير ذلك: أن لها منشئا. وبعد، فإنه لو خلى بين الأصباغ وانصباغ الأشياء بها ليخرج فاسدا مسمما وإنما يصلح ذلك الحكيم عليم يضع كل شيء موضعه، فمثله أمر الطبائع وهو في شأن الطبائع أحق؛ إذ هي تتنافر وفيها التباعد، أو يقدح في الأشياء بلا حد، وفيه الفساد، فدل الاتساق وقيام الأعيان بها على عليم قاهر جمع بينها ما كان لكل مجتمع الطبائع حامل يحملها ليس هو لهن، فثبت بالضرورة وجودهن، وقد مضى من هذا النوع ما فيه مقنع.
As for the partisans of natures (as-hab al-taba'i), indeed the nature is subjugated; it is unable to refrain from what it is naturally disposed to, but another other than every owner of a nature is able to prevent it from its generation. So it is established that its action is for other than itself; for it is prevented by another from acting. And if it were itself acting, it would not tolerate prevention as long as its nature persists. Since it does not refrain from acting, it is established that it is subjugated under a subjugator who is knowing. Moreover, every owner of a nature does not act in a thing by its nature unless that other is made so as to accept that, like the thing that is harmed—the act that is harmful in another does not harm it—and likewise the painful and pleasurable, and likewise dyes. And the action of nature is not to make a thing accept its nature and be affected by it; so the existence of other than natures is established. Moreover, if one were to leave between the owner of a nature and its action, it would not compose nor form. So their existence indicates something else: that they have an originator. And then, if one were to leave between dyes and the dyeing of things with them, it would come out corrupt and poisonous. Only the wise, knowing one who places everything in its proper place makes it sound. So likewise is the matter of natures, and it is more fitting concerning the affair of natures; for they repel each other and there is distance in them, or they attack things without limit, and therein is corruption. So harmony and the subsistence of entities through them indicate a knowing, subjugating one who combined them, since for every assembly of natures there is a carrier that carries them and is not from them. So their existence is established by necessity, and enough of this type has passed.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that natures (taba'i) are autonomous causal principles. Maturidi argues that a nature is subjugated (maqhur); it cannot refrain from its inherent action, but it can be prevented by an external agent. This shows that its action is not truly its own but is controlled by another. He then uses the analogy of dye: a dye only works if the object is made receptive, proving the need for a wise agent who prepares receptivity. Similarly, natures are diverse and conflicting; if left to themselves, they would produce disorder. The observed harmony in the world, where opposites like heat and cold coexist in a single body, proves a wise, powerful manager (qahir 'alim) who brings them together. Finally, the argument concludes that the existence of natures themselves requires an originator (munshi').

?
The passage teaches that natures cannot produce order or harmony on their own. Consider your own life: in what areas have you been relying on your 'nature'—your habits, talents, or instincts—as if they were self-sufficient, forgetting the One who must constantly subjugate and arrange them? What would it look like to consciously depend on Him today?
?
Maturidi argues that if left to themselves, conflicting natures would cause corruption. Think of a conflict inside you—maybe between desires and duties, or between different roles you play. Are you trying to resolve it in your own strength, or have you submitted it to the knowing, subjugating One who places everything in its proper place? What specific step can you take to invite His order into that area?
?
The 'carrier' that is not from the natures reminds us that we are not self-originating. Your very existence is carried by a reality beyond you. How does it feel to know that you are not the source of your own being? In light of this, what would it mean to live with radical gratitude and humility today?
